System Design Notes

A complete, doubt-free journey from absolute basics to advanced distributed systems. Explained in simple, clear language with real-world examples.

0Doubts left

#1What is System Design?

One-line definition

System Design is the process of deciding how to build a software application so that it works correctly, stays fast, never breaks, and keeps working even when millions of people use it at the same time.

Let us understand this slowly, because this single idea is the foundation of everything we will learn.

When you build a very small program (like a calculator that runs only on your laptop), you don't need to think much. Only one person uses it. There is no internet, no millions of users, no money lost if it crashes.

But real apps like YouTube, Instagram, Swiggy, Uber, Google Search, Facebook, or even AI tools like Cursor and Claude are used by crores of people at the same time, from all over the world, on slow and fast internet, day and night. If such an app becomes slow or goes down even for 5 minutes, the company can lose crores of rupees and millions of users get angry.

System Design is the planning and decision-making that makes such big apps possible. It answers questions like:

  • How do we handle 1 crore users at the same time without the app crashing?
  • How do we make sure a video on YouTube starts playing in 1-2 seconds, not 30 seconds?
  • Where do we store billions of photos (Instagram) or messages (WhatsApp)?
  • What happens if one computer (server) suddenly dies? Does the whole app go down, or does it keep running?
  • How do we make the app fast for a user in India AND a user in America at the same time?

Real-life Analogy: Building a Hut vs Building a City

Think of it like construction

Building a small program is like building a single hut — one person can do it in a day, no planning needed. But building a large app like Swiggy is like building an entire city for lakhs of people.

A city needs careful design before construction:

  • Roads so traffic does not jam (in apps: how data travels).
  • Water & electricity supply that never stops (in apps: databases and servers that stay up).
  • Multiple hospitals so if one is full, people go to another (in apps: backup servers).
  • Traffic police to direct cars to less crowded roads (in apps: load balancers).
  • Local shops in every area so people don't travel far (in apps: caching and CDNs).

If you build a city without planning, it collapses the moment too many people arrive. The same happens to apps built without system design — they crash the moment they become popular. System Design is the city-planning of software.

A Concrete Example: What Happens When You Press "Play" on YouTube?

This looks simple to you — you tap a video, it plays. But behind that one tap, a huge designed system works in milliseconds:

Youphone / laptop
→
InternetDNS finds YouTube
→
Load Balancerpicks a free server
→
Servershandle your request
↓
CDNvideo stored near you
→
Cachefast temporary memory
→
Databasevideo info, likes, comments
One tap on "Play" triggers this entire designed journey — and it finishes in about 1-2 seconds.

Every box in that picture is a design decision. Why a load balancer? Why a CDN? Why a cache? Each one solves a real problem (speed, crowd, failure). We will learn every single box in detail in the coming topics. For now, just understand: system design = choosing and connecting these boxes the right way.

Real Apps and the Hard Problems They Solve

Every famous app you use is a masterpiece of system design. Here is what makes each one hard to build:

AppThe Hard System Design Problem
Google SearchSearch the entire internet (billions of pages) and return answers in under half a second, for billions of searches every day.
YouTubeStore and stream billions of videos. Play instantly anywhere in the world without buffering, on slow and fast internet.
Instagram / FacebookStore billions of photos and posts. Build a personalised feed for each of 2+ billion users in real time.
Swiggy / ZomatoMatch lakhs of live orders with restaurants and delivery partners, track them on a live map, and update prices and ETAs every second.
Uber / OlaFind the nearest free driver out of millions in real time, calculate routes and fares, and handle surge pricing instantly.
WhatsAppDeliver 100+ billion messages a day instantly, in order, even if the receiver's phone is switched off.
Cursor / Claude (AI tools)Send your request to massive AI models running on expensive GPUs, stream the answer back word-by-word, and serve millions of users without huge delays or huge cost.

Notice the common themes in every row: huge scale (many users), speed (fast response), reliability (never goes down), and cost (do it without wasting money). These four goals are the heart of system design.

Why Should YOU Care About System Design?

  • For interviews: Big companies (Google, Amazon, Flipkart, startups) have a dedicated "System Design Interview" round for any developer with even 1-2 years of experience. You cannot get a good job at scale without it.
  • For real work: When your app grows from 100 users to 10 lakh users, only system design knowledge will save it from crashing.
  • For becoming a senior engineer: Writing code is a junior skill. Deciding how the whole system fits together is what makes you a senior engineer or architect (and gets you paid more).

Two Levels of System Design (Quick Preview)

High-Level Design (HLD)

The big picture. Which major pieces exist and how they connect — servers, databases, load balancers, caches.

Like an architect's map of a whole city.

Low-Level Design (LLD)

The fine details inside one piece — classes, functions, database tables, exact logic.

Like the detailed blueprint of one single building.

Don't worry

We have a full dedicated topic (#2) on HLD vs LLD next. For now, just know that "System Design" usually means the big-picture HLD, which is what interviews focus on most.

Common Misconceptions (Cleared Right Now)

Myth 1: "System Design is only about coding."

Reality: System design is mostly about thinking and deciding, not writing code. It is about trade-offs — choosing option A over option B and explaining why. You draw boxes and arrows, not always code.

Myth 2: "There is one correct answer."

Reality: There is no single right answer in system design. Every choice has pros and cons. A good engineer picks a reasonable option and clearly explains the trade-offs. We will learn to think this way.

Myth 3: "It is too advanced for beginners."

Reality: If you understand simple analogies (city, restaurant, post office), you can understand system design. We start from absolute zero and build up. No step is skipped.

Interview Tips

  • Interviewers don't expect a perfect answer. They want to see how you think and whether you discuss trade-offs.
  • Always ask clarifying questions first ("How many users? What features?") before designing. This shows maturity.
  • Start simple, then improve. First design for 100 users, then scale up to 1 crore. This step-by-step approach impresses interviewers.
  • A very common opening question is literally: "What do you understand by system design?" — now you can answer it confidently.

Key Takeaways

  • System Design = planning how to build software that is fast, reliable, and works for millions of users at once.
  • It is like city-planning for software — without it, popular apps crash.
  • The four core goals are: Scale, Speed, Reliability, and Cost.
  • Every big app (YouTube, Swiggy, Uber, Instagram, Cursor, Claude) is solving these same goals in its own way.
  • There is no single correct answer — it is all about understanding and explaining trade-offs.
  • HLD = big picture; LLD = fine internal details (full topic coming next).
Previous— NextHLD vs LLD vs System Design →

#2HLD vs LLD vs System Design

One-line definition

System Design is the full work of planning a software system before and while building it. It has two zoom levels: HLD (High-Level Design) is the bird's-eye "big picture", and LLD (Low-Level Design) is the close-up "inside details" of each piece.

These three words confuse almost every beginner. People think HLD, LLD, and System Design are three separate subjects you study from three different books. That is wrong. Let us remove this confusion forever with one simple idea:

The core idea (remember this one line)

HLD and LLD are not different things. They are the same design seen at two different zoom levels, and "System Design" is the name for the whole thing together.

Think about Google Maps. It is one single map, but you can zoom it:

  • Zoom all the way out → you see the whole country: states, big highways connecting cities. You do not see individual houses. → This view is HLD.
  • Zoom all the way in → you see one single street, one house, even the front gate. But now you cannot see the whole country. → This view is LLD.
  • The complete map (which lets you do both) → that is System Design.

It is the same map the whole time. You just changed how close you are looking. HLD and LLD work exactly the same way on a software system.

Interactive Demo — Zoom between HLD and LLD

This is the same system. Click the buttons to zoom out (see the big picture) or zoom in (see what is inside one box). Watch how the view changes — this is exactly the difference between HLD and LLD.

Mobile Appclient
→
Load Balancer
→
Backend Serverclick "Zoom In"
→
Database
HLD view: only the major boxes and how they connect. We see that a Backend Server exists, but not what is inside it.

You zoomed inside the Backend Server box. Now you see its internal code design:

class OrderController {        // receives the web request
    placeOrder(request) { ... }
}

class OrderService {           // the business logic
    validate(order) { ... }
    calculatePrice(order) { ... }
}

class OrderRepository {        // talks to the database
    save(order) { ... }
    findById(id) { ... }
}

TABLE orders (
    order_id    BIGINT  PRIMARY KEY,
    user_id     BIGINT,
    amount      DECIMAL,
    status      VARCHAR(20)
)
LLD view: the exact classes, functions, and database tables inside that one box.

Real-life Analogy: Building a House

Architect vs Electrician

When you build a house, two completely different kinds of drawings are made by two different experts:

1. The Architect's plan (HLD): Shows the big picture — where the bedrooms, kitchen, bathroom, and stairs go, and how the rooms connect. It does NOT show where each individual electrical wire or water pipe goes.

2. The Electrician's & Plumber's blueprint (LLD): Zooms into one room and shows the exact wiring path, every switch position, every pipe joint, and exact measurements in centimetres.

You need both to build the house. The architect's plan alone is not enough to actually wire the house; the wiring blueprint alone makes no sense without the room layout. Both drawings together = the complete plan = System Design.

High-Level Design (HLD) — The Big Picture

HLD shows the major building blocks of the system and how they talk to each other. It answers the question "What are the main pieces, and how do they connect?" — not "How is each piece coded inside?".

When you do HLD, you are making decisions like these:

  • How many servers do we need, and in which regions of the world?
  • Which type of database (SQL or NoSQL), and how many copies of it?
  • Do we need a load balancer? A cache? A CDN? A message queue?
  • How does data flow from the user, through the system, to the database, and back?
  • How will the system grow (scale) when users increase, and how will it survive if one server dies?

Notice that none of these questions mention class names or function logic. HLD stays at the level of boxes and arrows.

Animated Demo — Follow one request, step by step

Press Play. Each box will light up one by one as your request reaches it, and the line below explains in plain words what is happening at that moment. This "boxes, arrows, and flow" thinking is exactly what HLD is about. Use Step to move manually at your own pace.

Appyour phone
→
Load Balancertraffic police
→
Serverbusiness logic
→
Cachefast memory
→
Databasereal data
Press  Play  to send a request through the system…

What an HLD document usually contains

When a senior engineer writes an HLD, it normally includes these things. Do not worry if some words are new — we have a full topic on each one later. For now, just see the kind of decisions HLD covers:

  • Architecture diagram — the boxes-and-arrows picture of the whole system.
  • Major components — load balancers, servers, databases, caches, queues, CDNs.
  • Data flow — how a request travels in, and how the response comes back.
  • Database choice — SQL or NoSQL, and roughly what data is stored where.
  • Scaling plan — how the system handles more users (add more servers, etc.).
  • Reliability plan — what happens if a server or database fails.
  • APIs between services — which service talks to which, at a high level.

Low-Level Design (LLD) — The Inside Details

LLD zooms into one single box from the HLD (for example, the "Backend Server" box) and designs exactly how it works inside, in real code. It answers the question "How is this one piece actually built?".

When you do LLD, you are making decisions like these:

  • Which classes and functions to create, and how they call each other.
  • The exact database tables: their columns, data types, and relationships.
  • The precise step-by-step logic inside each feature.
  • Which design patterns to use (Factory, Observer, Strategy, etc.).
  • How to keep the code clean, reusable, and easy to test.
Example: A "User" in LLD

For the same app, the HLD just said "we need a database". The LLD goes much deeper and defines the exact table:

TABLE users (
  user_id      BIGINT       PRIMARY KEY,   -- unique id for each user
  name         VARCHAR(100),               -- user's full name
  email        VARCHAR(150) UNIQUE,        -- no two users share an email
  created_at   TIMESTAMP                   -- when the account was made
)

This level of exact detail — column names, data types, and keys — is LLD. The HLD did not care about any of this; it only cared that a database exists.

How HLD and LLD Work Together (The Real Workflow)

In a real company, the two are done in order. You almost always go HLD first, then LLD:

1. HLDdraw the big boxes
→
2. Pick a boxe.g. "Order Service"
→
3. LLDdesign its classes & tables
→
4. Code itdevelopers build it
First the architect draws the city (HLD). Then, building by building, the detailed blueprints are made (LLD). Then construction (coding) begins.

Why Split the Work into Two Levels at All?

A fair question: why not just start coding directly? Why waste time on HLD and LLD? Here is why every serious team does it:

  • You catch expensive mistakes early. Fixing a wrong decision on paper takes 5 minutes. Fixing it after writing 50,000 lines of code takes months. HLD lets you find big problems before they cost money.
  • Many people can work together. Once the HLD shows 5 boxes, 5 different teams can each take one box and design its LLD in parallel. Without a shared big picture, everyone would clash.
  • It separates two kinds of thinking. HLD needs "will this scale and survive failures?" thinking. LLD needs "is this code clean and correct?" thinking. Mixing both at once overwhelms the brain. Splitting them keeps each step clear.
  • It becomes documentation. New engineers who join later can read the HLD to understand the whole system in 10 minutes, instead of reading lakhs of lines of code.
Simple way to remember

HLD answers "Will this system work at scale?" — LLD answers "Is this piece of code correct and clean?" Two different questions, so two different levels.

What Kind of Diagrams Are Used at Each Level?

This is a detail many beginners miss, and it instantly makes things clearer. HLD and LLD literally use different types of diagrams:

HLD diagrams

  • Block / architecture diagrams (boxes and arrows).
  • Data flow diagrams (how data moves around).
  • Deployment diagrams (which server sits where).

LLD diagrams

  • Class diagrams (classes and how they relate — UML).
  • ER diagrams (database tables and their links).
  • Sequence diagrams (exact order of function calls).

HLD vs LLD: Side-by-Side Comparison

PointHigh-Level Design (HLD)Low-Level Design (LLD)
Zoom levelBird's-eye / big pictureClose-up / fine detail
Question it answersWhat are the parts & how connectedHow is each part built inside
ShowsServers, DBs, load balancers, cachesClasses, functions, tables, logic
Level of detailBoxes and arrowsActual code structure
AudienceArchitects, team leads, managersDevelopers who write the code
Usually made bySenior engineers / architectsDevelopers
Also calledMacro / architecture designMicro / detailed design
Interview round name"System Design" round (scalability)"LLD / OOP / Machine Coding" round

So Where Does "System Design" Fit?

System Design is the umbrella term that covers both HLD and LLD together. But here is the important real-world fact: in everyday talk and in most interviews, when people say "System Design", they usually mean HLD (the big-picture, scalability-focused design), because that is the harder and more discussed part.

System Designthe full umbrella
↓
HLDbig picture
LLDinside details
System Design = HLD + LLD together. But "System Design interview" almost always means HLD.

Real Example: Designing Instagram

Let us take one real app and clearly split which decisions are HLD and which are LLD. Click the tabs to switch.

  • Use a CDN so photos load fast for users in every country.
  • Use object storage (like Amazon S3) to hold billions of images.
  • Use a cache for the home feed so it appears instantly.
  • Split the app into separate services: feed, upload, notifications, search.
  • Use a message queue so notifications are sent in the background.
  • Design the posts table: its columns, types, and indexes.
  • Write the exact step-by-step logic of the "generate home feed" function.
  • Create the Post, User, and Comment classes and their methods.
  • Decide the exact API request and response format (what JSON goes in and out).
  • Choose the right design pattern to handle different post types (photo, reel, story).

See the pattern? The HLD tab never mentions a class or a table. The LLD tab never mentions a CDN or scaling. Each lives at its own zoom level — but both describe the same Instagram.

Full Walkthrough: "Place Order" on Swiggy

Let us trace one single feature — tapping "Place Order" on Swiggy — through both levels, so you see exactly where HLD ends and LLD begins.

Step 1 — The HLD (big picture)

At the HLD level, we only decide the major pieces involved and how the request flows. We do not write any code yet:

Swiggy App
→
API Gateway
→
Order Service
→
Database
↓
Payment Service
Notification Servicetells the restaurant
HLD: which services handle "Place Order" and how they connect. Still just boxes and arrows.

Step 2 — The LLD (inside the Order Service)

Now we zoom into the single Order Service box and design its actual code and data:

// The classes inside the Order Service
class OrderController {
    placeOrder(request) { ... }     // entry point for the web request
}
class OrderService {
    validateCart(cart) { ... }      // are items still available?
    createOrder(cart, user) { ... } // build the order object
}
class OrderRepository {
    save(order) { ... }             // write the order to the database
}

// The exact database table it writes to
TABLE orders (
    order_id     BIGINT      PRIMARY KEY,
    user_id      BIGINT,
    restaurant_id BIGINT,
    total_amount DECIMAL,
    status       VARCHAR(20),   -- PLACED, COOKING, DELIVERED
    created_at   TIMESTAMP
)

Notice the clean split: the HLD said "there is an Order Service". The LLD said "here are the exact classes and the exact table inside it". Same feature, two zoom levels.

Who Works on What in a Real Team?

RoleMostly works onTypical question they ask
Architect / Staff EngineerHLD"Will this handle 10x more users next year?"
Senior EngineerHLD + reviews LLD"Is this the right database for this data?"
Software DeveloperLLD + writing code"How should these classes be structured?"
The whole teamBoth, together"Does our detailed design match the big picture?"

Common Misconceptions (Cleared)

Myth 1: "HLD and LLD are completely separate skills."

Reality: They are two zoom levels of the same design work. A great engineer moves smoothly between them — first the big picture, then the details.

Myth 2: "LLD is only for juniors, HLD is only for seniors."

Reality: Both matter at every level. Juniors should understand HLD too, and seniors must still get LLD right. A bad LLD can ruin a perfect HLD — just like bad wiring can ruin a beautifully designed house.

Myth 3: "You must finish all HLD before any LLD."

Reality: In real projects it is iterative. You may do HLD, start LLD on one service, learn something, and go back to adjust the HLD. It is a back-and-forth, not a one-way street.

Interview Tips

  • If the round is called "System Design" or "Design Twitter / Uber / Netflix", they almost always mean HLD — focus on scale, databases, caching, and load balancing.
  • If the round is called "LLD", "Machine Coding", or "OOP Design" (e.g. "Design a parking lot" or "Design a chess game"), they want classes, objects, and clean code.
  • In an HLD interview, start with the big boxes first. Do NOT jump into table columns early — that is LLD and will waste your limited time.
  • If you are unsure which type the round is, just ask the interviewer: "Should I focus on the high-level architecture or the detailed class design?" This is a smart, professional question.

Key Takeaways

  • System Design = HLD + LLD. They are zoom levels of the same thing, not three separate subjects.
  • HLD = big picture (servers, DBs, caches, and how they connect). Answers "what & how connected".
  • LLD = inside details of one box (classes, tables, logic). Answers "how built in code".
  • Real workflow: HLD first, then LLD, then coding — and it is iterative (you go back and forth).
  • When people say "System Design interview", they almost always mean HLD.
  • This whole course mostly teaches HLD, which is what scaling and interviews need most.
Previous← What is System Design? NextFunctional vs Non-Functional →

#3Functional vs Non-Functional Requirements

One-line definition

Functional Requirements describe what the system should do (its features and actions). Non-Functional Requirements describe how well the system should do it (its speed, reliability, security, and overall quality).

Before you design or build anything, you must first know what you are being asked to build. That list of "what is needed" is called the requirements. Every requirement falls into one of two buckets, and understanding the difference is the true starting point of all system design.

Let us learn both with one simple sentence to keep in mind:

The one-line trick to never get confused

Functional = the "WHAT" (what features the system has).
Non-Functional = the "HOW WELL" (how fast, how safe, how reliable those features are).

Real-life Analogy: Ordering Food at a Restaurant

The menu vs the experience

Imagine you walk into a restaurant.

Functional requirements are the things on the menu — what the restaurant can do for you: serve pizza, serve cold drinks, take your order, accept card payment. These are the actions and features.

Non-functional requirements are about the quality of the experience — the food arrives in 10 minutes (fast), the plates are clean (safe), the restaurant is open 24/7 (always available), and it can seat 200 people at once (handles load).

A restaurant can have a great menu (functional) but still be terrible if the food takes 2 hours and the kitchen is dirty (bad non-functional). Both matter.

Functional Requirements — The "WHAT"

A functional requirement is a specific action, feature, or behaviour that the system must provide to its users. In other words, it answers the question: "What should this system be able to do?" Every button you can click, every action you can take, and every result the system gives back is described by a functional requirement.

Think of functional requirements as a promise list. Each item is a promise the software makes to the user: "If you do this, I will do that." For example, WhatsApp promises: "If you type a message and press send, I will deliver it to your friend." That single promise is one functional requirement.

The simple test to spot a functional requirement

Whenever you are unsure, try to fit the requirement into this sentence: "The system should allow the user to ______" or "The system should be able to ______." If the sentence makes sense, it is almost certainly functional. For example, "The system should allow the user to reset their password" fits perfectly — so it is functional. But "The system should allow the user to load pages in 2 seconds" sounds wrong — because speed is a quality, not an action. That tells you it is non-functional instead.

Functional requirements usually fall into three groups

To make sure you never miss any, it helps to think of them in three buckets:

  • User actions — things the user directly does, like "post a photo", "search for a product", or "send a message". These are the most obvious ones.
  • System actions — things the system does on its own, often in the background, like "send an OTP email", "automatically log out after 30 minutes", or "calculate the delivery fee".
  • Data handling — what data the system stores and manages, like "store the user's order history" or "keep a record of all transactions".

Examples across real apps (and why each is functional)

Below, notice that every single one describes an action or behaviour — something the system does. That is the signature of a functional requirement:

  • Instagram: "A user can upload a photo" is an action the user performs. "A user can like a post" and "follow another user" are also clear actions. Each one is a feature the app must build.
  • Swiggy: "A user can search for restaurants", "add items to a cart", and "pay for an order" — each is a concrete step in the ordering journey, so each is functional.
  • WhatsApp: "Send a text message", "create a group", "make a voice call" — these are the core things the app lets you do.
  • YouTube: "Upload a video", "search videos", "subscribe to a channel" — again, all clear actions and features.
Key insight: functional requirements define the "scope"

The list of functional requirements decides how big the project is — its scope. If you add "video calling" to WhatsApp's list, you have just added months of work. This is why, in interviews, you first agree on a small core set of functional requirements, so you do not try to design an impossibly large system in 45 minutes.

Non-Functional Requirements — The "HOW WELL"

A non-functional requirement does not add any new feature. Instead, it describes a quality — how good, how fast, how safe, or how reliable the existing features must be. If functional requirements are what the system does, non-functional requirements are the standard to which it must do it.

Here is the crucial point that beginners miss: two apps can have the exact same features (functional) but feel completely different because of their non-functional quality. Imagine two messaging apps that both let you send messages. In one, messages arrive instantly and never get lost. In the other, messages take 10 seconds and sometimes vanish. Same feature, totally different quality. The difference is entirely non-functional — and it is what decides whether users love or hate the app.

These qualities are often nicknamed the "-ilities" because so many of them end in "-ility" (scalability, availability, reliability, and so on). Let us understand each one properly, because these are the true subject matter of system design.

1. Performance / Latency — "How fast does it respond?"

Latency is the time between you doing something and the system responding. When you tap a video and it starts in 1 second, that 1 second is the latency. Good performance means low latency — the system feels instant. A real target might be: "the search results page must appear in under 200 milliseconds." Users are extremely impatient: studies show that even a 1-second delay can make people abandon a website. So performance directly affects whether a business makes money.

2. Scalability — "Can it grow without breaking?"

Scalability is the system's ability to keep working well as the load increases — more users, more data, more requests. A system that runs perfectly for 1,000 users but crashes at 1,00,000 users is not scalable. Think of a small tea stall: it serves 10 customers easily, but if 1,000 people arrive at once, it collapses. A scalable design is one you can grow (by adding more servers) to handle the crowd. This is one of the most discussed topics in all of system design, and we have entire chapters on it later.

3. Availability — "Is it always up and running?"

Availability is the percentage of time the system is working and reachable. We measure it in "nines": 99.9% availability means the system can be down for about 8.7 hours a year, while 99.99% means only about 52 minutes a year. For something like a bank or a hospital system, even a few minutes of downtime is unacceptable. High availability usually means having backup servers ready, so if one fails, another instantly takes over and users never notice.

4. Reliability — "Does it work correctly every time?"

Reliability means the system gives the correct result and does not lose data, even when things go wrong. Availability says "the system is up"; reliability says "and it is behaving correctly." For example, when you transfer money, reliability guarantees that the money is never lost or duplicated — even if a server crashes halfway through. An app can be available (online) but unreliable (giving wrong balances). You need both.

5. Security — "Is user data safe?"

Security is about protecting the system and its data from attackers, leaks, and misuse. This includes making sure only the right people can access data (authentication and authorization), keeping data encrypted so hackers cannot read it even if they steal it, and defending against attacks. For example, WhatsApp uses end-to-end encryption so that not even WhatsApp itself can read your private messages. We will cover the full security topics later in the course.

6. Maintainability — "Is it easy to fix and improve later?"

Software is never "finished" — engineers constantly fix bugs and add features. Maintainability is how easy and cheap it is to do that. A well-designed, clean, modular system can be updated quickly by any engineer. A messy one becomes a nightmare where changing one thing breaks five others. Good maintainability saves enormous time and money over the years a product lives.

7. Consistency — "Do all users see the same correct data?"

In big systems, data is copied across many servers around the world. Consistency means that everyone sees the same, up-to-date value. For example, if you change your profile photo, consistency decides whether your friend in another country sees the new photo immediately or a few seconds later. Sometimes systems deliberately trade a little consistency to gain more speed and availability — a famous trade-off we will study in depth in the CAP Theorem topic.

Here is a quick reference table summarising all seven, so you can revise them at a glance:

QualityThe question it answersExample target
PerformanceHow fast does it respond?Page loads in under 2 seconds
ScalabilityCan it grow without breaking?Handle 1 crore users
AvailabilityIs it always up?Up 99.99% of the time
ReliabilityIs it always correct?Never lose a payment
SecurityIs data safe?All data encrypted
MaintainabilityEasy to change later?Add a feature in days, not months
ConsistencySame data everywhere?All users see the latest value
Why these matter so much

In a system design interview, almost all the hard and interesting work is about non-functional requirements. Anyone can say "users can send messages" (functional — one line). The real challenge that takes real engineering skill is "send 100 billion messages a day, instantly, without ever losing one, securely, even if servers fail" (that is performance + scalability + reliability + security + availability, all at once). This is exactly where system design lives, and why we will spend most of this course here.

Interactive Quiz — Can you classify them?

Read each requirement and decide: is it Functional (a feature/action) or Non-Functional (a quality)? Click your answer to get instant feedback and an explanation. This is exactly the skill interviewers test.

Functional vs Non-Functional: Side-by-Side

PointFunctionalNon-Functional
Answers the questionWhat does it do?How well does it do it?
DescribesFeatures and actionsQualities and constraints
Example"User can send a message""Message is delivered in under 1 second"
If missingThe feature simply does not existThe feature exists but works badly
Easy to test?Yes (does it work: yes/no)Harder (needs measuring: how fast, how many)
Interview focusListed quickly at the startDiscussed deeply (the main event)

Real Example: Requirements for WhatsApp

Let us split a real app's requirements into the two buckets. Click the tabs.

  • A user can send and receive text messages.
  • A user can send photos, videos, and voice notes.
  • A user can create a group chat.
  • A user can see "last seen" and "online" status.
  • A user can make voice and video calls.
  • Messages must be delivered almost instantly (low latency).
  • The system must handle billions of messages per day (scalability).
  • No message should ever be lost (reliability).
  • Chats must be end-to-end encrypted (security).
  • The app must be available 24/7 across the world (availability).

How to Gather Requirements in an Interview

When an interviewer says "Design Instagram", they are deliberately being vague. There is no way to design a system if you do not know exactly what it must do and how well it must do it. So the interviewer is secretly testing whether you have the maturity to ask questions first instead of blindly jumping into a solution. Gathering requirements is therefore the very first phase of any design, and it usually goes like this:

Step 1 — Pin down the functional scope. Start by agreeing on which features you will actually design. Real apps have hundreds of features, but you only have 45 minutes, so you must narrow it down. You might ask: "Which features should I focus on — just posting photos and viewing the feed, or also stories, reels, and direct messages?" This prevents you from accidentally trying to build the entire app and running out of time.

Step 2 — Understand the scale. Scale completely changes the design. A system for 1,000 users is simple; a system for 100 crore users needs caching, sharding, and load balancing. So ask about numbers: "Roughly how many total users? How many are active daily? How many photos are uploaded per second?" These numbers drive almost every later decision, which is why we have a whole topic on "back-of-the-envelope calculations" near the end.

Step 3 — Clarify the quality priorities. You usually cannot make everything perfect at once, so find out what matters most. For example: "Is it more important that the feed is always available, or always perfectly up to date?" The answer tells you which trade-offs to make. Knowing the top 2-3 non-functional priorities lets you design intelligently instead of trying to optimise everything.

Step 4 — Write it all down as two clear lists. Summarise everything into a Functional list and a Non-Functional list, and say them out loud to confirm with the interviewer. This shows structure, makes sure you both agree, and gives you a checklist to design against for the rest of the interview.

Critical interview habit

Never start drawing boxes before clarifying requirements. Jumping straight into a solution without asking questions is the number one reason candidates fail system design interviews.

Common Misconceptions (Cleared)

Myth 1: "Non-functional requirements are less important."

Reality: The word "non-functional" makes them sound optional, but they are usually the hardest and most important part. A slow, insecure, or crashing app fails no matter how many features it has.

Myth 2: "Requirements are fixed and never change."

Reality: Requirements often change as a product grows. Good design leaves room to adjust. That is exactly why maintainability is itself a non-functional requirement.

Myth 3: "I should list every possible requirement."

Reality: Especially in an interview, focus on the core few functional features and the key non-functional qualities. Trying to list everything wastes time and shows poor prioritisation.

Interview Tips

  • Always begin a system design answer by stating the functional and non-functional requirements out loud. It shows structure and maturity.
  • For non-functional requirements, try to put numbers on them: "under 200 milliseconds", "99.99% available", "10 million daily users". Numbers impress interviewers far more than vague words.
  • Pick the 2-3 most important non-functional requirements and design around them. You cannot perfectly optimise everything at once — choosing priorities shows real engineering judgement.
  • A classic question: "What is the difference between functional and non-functional requirements?" — you can now answer it in one clean sentence.

Key Takeaways

  • Functional = WHAT the system does (features/actions). Non-Functional = HOW WELL it does it (qualities).
  • Functional test: "The system should let the user ___". If it fits, it is functional.
  • Key non-functional qualities: performance, scalability, availability, reliability, security, maintainability, consistency.
  • System design interviews focus mostly on the non-functional side — that is where the hard problems are.
  • Always clarify requirements first (ask questions, put numbers on them) before designing anything.
Previous← HLD vs LLD vs System Design NextClient-Server Architecture →

#4Client-Server Architecture

One-line definition

Client-Server Architecture is a way of building software where one computer (the client) asks for something, and another computer (the server) provides it. The client sends a request, the server sends back a response. Almost every app and website you use works this way.

This is the most fundamental pattern in all of system design. Before we can talk about load balancers, databases, or scaling, we must deeply understand this one simple relationship: someone asks, and someone answers. Let us build that understanding from absolute zero.

What Problem Does This Solve?

Imagine you want to watch a YouTube video. The video file is huge — maybe hundreds of megabytes. It would be impossible to store every video in the world on your phone. So instead, the videos live on YouTube's powerful computers, and your phone simply asks for the one video you want, when you want it. Your phone is the client; YouTube's computer is the server.

This split exists because of a simple truth: the device in your hand is small and limited, but the data and computing power needed are enormous. So we keep the heavy stuff (data, processing) on big shared computers (servers) and let many small devices (clients) request only what they need. This is the core idea behind client-server architecture.

Real-life Analogy: A Restaurant

You, the waiter, and the kitchen

Think of eating at a restaurant:

You are the client. You decide what you want and place an order. You do not go into the kitchen or cook anything yourself — you just ask.

The kitchen is the server. It has all the ingredients, the chefs, and the equipment (the "resources"). It receives your order, does the hard work of cooking, and produces the dish.

The waiter and the path between tables and kitchen is the network. The waiter carries your request to the kitchen and carries the finished food back to you.

Notice: many customers (clients) can be served by one kitchen (server). You do not need your own kitchen at home for every meal — you share the restaurant's. That sharing is exactly why client-server is so powerful and efficient.

Who Is the "Client"?

The client is the device or program that starts the conversation by asking for something. It is usually the thing in front of the user. Common examples of clients include:

  • A web browser (Chrome, Safari) when you open a website.
  • A mobile app (the Instagram or Swiggy app on your phone).
  • A desktop application (the Spotify app on your laptop).

The client's main jobs are: take input from the user, send a request to the server, wait for the response, and then display the result nicely on the screen. The client usually does not store the main data or do the heavy processing — it relies on the server for that.

Who Is the "Server"?

The server is a powerful computer (often many computers working together) that waits for requests and responds to them. It holds the important data and does the heavy work. When you hear "server", do not imagine something magical — it is just a computer, usually sitting in a large building called a data center, that is always switched on and connected to the internet, ready to answer requests 24/7.

The server's main jobs are: listen for incoming requests, process each one (which may involve reading or writing to a database, running calculations, checking permissions), and send back a response. One server can handle requests from thousands of clients, which is why this model scales so well.

The Request-Response Cycle (The Heart of It All)

The entire interaction is built on one repeating loop called the request-response cycle. It always follows the same four steps:

  1. The client sends a request. For example, your browser sends "Please give me the home page of youtube.com."
  2. The request travels over the network (the internet) to reach the correct server.
  3. The server processes the request. It figures out what is being asked, fetches the needed data (maybe from a database), and prepares an answer.
  4. The server sends back a response, which travels back over the network to the client, and the client displays it.

One very important detail: in classic client-server, the client always speaks first. The server never randomly sends data to a client out of nowhere — it only answers when asked. (There are special technologies like WebSockets that change this, and we will study them later, but the basic model is "client asks, server answers.")

Interactive Demo — Send a real request

Press "Send a Request" and watch the full cycle happen: the request packet travels from the client to the server, the server processes it, and the response travels back. Read the live log below to follow each step in plain words.

💻Clientyour browser
🖥️ServerYouTube data center
Click the button to begin…

Full Walkthrough: What Happens When You Open youtube.com

Let us connect this to something you do every day. When you type youtube.com and press Enter, here is the client-server story behind it:

  1. Your browser (the client) needs to find YouTube's server. It first asks a special "phone book of the internet" called DNS to convert youtube.com into a numerical address (an IP address). We cover DNS fully in a later topic.
  2. Now knowing the address, the client sends an HTTP request to YouTube's server: "Give me the home page."
  3. YouTube's server processes the request — it checks who you are, fetches video recommendations from its database, and builds a personalised page.
  4. The server sends back an HTTP response containing the page (HTML, images, video links).
  5. Your browser receives this and renders the YouTube home page you see. Every thumbnail you then click starts a brand new request-response cycle.
Connecting the dots

Notice how many future topics already appear here: DNS, IP addresses, HTTP, databases. Client-server is the skeleton; everything else in this course hangs off it. That is why we learn it first.

Client-Server vs Peer-to-Peer (P2P)

There is another, different model worth knowing so you appreciate the contrast. In Peer-to-Peer (P2P), there is no central server — every computer is both a client and a server at the same time, sharing directly with each other. Old file-sharing apps like BitTorrent and some crypto networks work this way.

PointClient-ServerPeer-to-Peer (P2P)
Who has the data?A central serverEvery participant shares it
ControlCentral, easy to manageNo central control
If the center failsEveryone is affectedOthers keep working
ExamplesYouTube, Instagram, banking appsBitTorrent, blockchains

For almost all modern apps (and everything in this course), the client-server model is what we use, because the central control makes data, security, and updates far easier to manage.

Strengths and Weaknesses

Strengths

  • Central control: data lives in one place, so it is easy to secure, back up, and update.
  • Sharing: one server serves many clients efficiently.
  • Lightweight clients: your phone does not need huge storage or power.
  • Easy updates: fix something on the server and every client instantly benefits.

Weaknesses

  • Single point of failure: if the one server goes down, every client is stuck.
  • Bottleneck: too many clients at once can overload a single server.
  • Cost: running powerful, always-on servers is expensive.
This weakness is the doorway to the rest of the course

The big weakness — "one server can be overloaded or can fail" — is exactly the problem that load balancers, multiple servers, caching, and replication exist to solve. Every advanced topic you will learn is, in some way, fixing a limitation of the basic client-server model.

Common Misconceptions (Cleared)

Myth 1: "A server is a special, magical machine."

Reality: A server is just an ordinary computer running software that listens for and answers requests. Any computer — even your laptop — can act as a server if it runs server software. The word "server" describes a role, not a magical device.

Myth 2: "One server means one physical computer."

Reality: Behind a single website, "the server" is often hundreds or thousands of computers working together, hidden behind one address. From the client's point of view it looks like one server, but it is usually many.

Myth 3: "The client is always a phone or browser."

Reality: A client is anything that makes a request. A server can itself be a client when it asks another server for something. The roles are about who is asking versus who is answering in a given conversation.

Interview Tips

  • Be able to clearly explain the request-response cycle in your own words — it is a common warm-up question.
  • When you draw any system diagram, the client (user) on the left and the server on the right is the standard starting point. Interviewers expect you to begin here.
  • If asked about the weakness of a single server, immediately mention "single point of failure" and "bottleneck" — then you can naturally lead into load balancing and replication.

Key Takeaways

  • Client = asks (sends a request). Server = answers (sends a response). The network carries messages between them.
  • It exists because small devices should not hold all the data and power — big shared servers do the heavy lifting.
  • The request-response cycle is the core loop: client requests → network → server processes → response travels back.
  • In classic client-server, the client always speaks first; the server only answers when asked.
  • Its main weakness (overload and single point of failure) is the reason most advanced system design topics exist.
Previous← Functional vs Non-Functional NextIP, DNS & Domain Name →

#5IP Address, DNS & Domain Name

One-line definition

An IP Address is the unique number that identifies a computer on the internet. A Domain Name (like youtube.com) is the easy, human-friendly name for that computer. DNS is the system that translates the human-friendly domain name into the machine-friendly IP address.

In the last topic we said the client sends a request to "the server". But how does your phone actually find the right server out of the billions of computers on the internet? That is the question this topic answers. These three ideas — IP, Domain Name, and DNS — work together to make that possible.

The Big Idea: Computers Use Numbers, Humans Use Names

Here is the core problem. Computers on the internet find each other using numbers called IP addresses, such as 142.250.72.206. Numbers are perfect for machines, but terrible for humans — nobody wants to memorise a string of numbers for every website. Humans prefer names like youtube.com.

So we have a mismatch: humans type names, but computers need numbers. Something must translate between the two. That translator is DNS. This single idea explains the whole topic — everything else is just detail about how the translation works.

Real-life Analogy: The Contacts App on Your Phone

Saving "Mummy" instead of a 10-digit number

Think about your phone's contacts. To call your mother, you do not remember and dial her 10-digit number every time. Instead, you saved her as "Mummy" and just tap that name. Behind the scenes, your phone looks up "Mummy" in your contacts and finds the actual number to dial.

The internet works the same way:

  • "Mummy" (the saved name) = the Domain Name (youtube.com).
  • The phone number = the IP Address (142.250.72.206).
  • Your contacts app that does the lookup = DNS.

IP Address — The Computer's Number

Every device connected to the internet — your phone, a server, a smart TV — gets a unique IP (Internet Protocol) address. It works like a postal address: it tells data exactly where to be delivered. When the server sends back a response, it uses your IP address to know where to send it.

IPv4 and IPv6 (two versions)

There are two formats of IP address you should know:

  • IPv4: the older, most common format. It looks like 142.250.72.206 — four numbers (0 to 255) separated by dots. The problem: IPv4 allows only about 4.3 billion unique addresses, and the world has far more devices than that now. We are literally running out.
  • IPv6: the newer format, created to solve the shortage. It is much longer, like 2401:4900:1c30:8b9d::1, and provides a practically unlimited number of addresses (enough for every grain of sand on Earth to have many).

Public vs Private IP (a quick but important distinction)

A public IP is visible to the whole internet (like your home's street address that anyone can post mail to). A private IP is used only inside a local network, like your home Wi-Fi, where your phone, laptop, and TV each get a private IP (such as 192.168.1.5) that only makes sense within that home. Your router has one public IP that the whole house shares to reach the outside world.

Domain Name — The Human-Friendly Name

A domain name is the readable address you type, like www.youtube.com. It has a structure, and reading it from right to left reveals its parts:

wwwsubdomain
.
youtubedomain name
.
comTLD (top-level domain)
Read right to left: the TLD is the broadest category, then the specific name, then the subdomain.
  • TLD (Top-Level Domain): the ending, like .com, .org, .in, .gov. It is the broadest grouping.
  • Domain Name: the unique name a company registers, like youtube or google.
  • Subdomain: an optional prefix to point to a specific part, like www, mail (as in mail.google.com), or maps.

DNS — The Phone Book of the Internet

DNS (Domain Name System) is the giant, distributed system that stores the mapping between domain names and IP addresses, and answers lookup questions. When you type a domain name, DNS is what finds the matching IP so your browser can connect. It is called the "phone book of the internet" because, just like a phone book turns a name into a number, DNS turns a domain into an IP.

But DNS is not one single computer — that would be impossible to manage for the entire planet. Instead, the work is split across a hierarchy of different servers, each responsible for one part of the answer. Let us see how they cooperate.

How a DNS Lookup Actually Works (Step by Step)

When you request a domain and your computer does not already know its IP, the lookup travels through a chain of helpers. Here are the players:

  • DNS Resolver: the helper (usually run by your internet provider like Jio or Airtel) whose job is to do the searching for you and return the final answer.
  • Root Server: the top of the hierarchy. It does not know the IP, but it knows who handles each TLD (who handles .com, .in, etc.).
  • TLD Server: handles one ending, e.g. the .com server. It knows which server is the final authority for each .com domain.
  • Authoritative Server: the final source of truth for a specific domain. It actually holds the real IP address for youtube.com.

Use the demo below to watch them work together. Then read the recap underneath.

Interactive Demo — Resolve a domain into an IP

Press Play to watch youtube.com get turned into an IP address, hop by hop, through the DNS hierarchy. Use Step to go at your own pace.

Browseryou
→
ResolverISP helper
→
Roottop level
→
TLD.com server
→
Authoritativeowns youtube.com
Press  Play  to resolve youtube.com…

DNS Caching — Why It Is Not Slow Every Time

You might worry: does this whole multi-step journey happen every single time you open a website? That would be slow. The answer is no, thanks to caching. Once an IP is found, it is remembered (cached) at several levels — in your browser, in your operating system, and in the resolver — for a set period of time called the TTL (Time To Live).

So the very first lookup does the full journey, but for a while afterwards, your browser already knows the IP and connects instantly without asking anyone. This is why a website you visit often feels faster to connect to. When the TTL expires, the cache is cleared and the next lookup refreshes it — this lets websites change their IP when needed.

Common DNS Record Types (Good to Know)

DNS stores different kinds of entries called records. You do not need to master these now, but recognising them is useful:

RecordWhat it maps
A recordA domain name to an IPv4 address.
AAAA recordA domain name to an IPv6 address.
CNAMEOne domain name to another domain name (an alias).
MX recordA domain to its mail (email) server.

Common Misconceptions (Cleared)

Myth 1: "DNS stores or delivers the website."

Reality: DNS only gives you the address (the IP). It does not store the website's content at all. After DNS hands over the IP, your browser connects to that server separately to get the actual page. DNS is the phone book, not the website.

Myth 2: "An IP address never changes."

Reality: A website can change its IP address (for example, when it moves servers). DNS is exactly what makes this painless — the domain name stays the same, only the IP it points to is updated, so users notice nothing.

Myth 3: "DNS is one big central server."

Reality: DNS is a distributed hierarchy of many servers (root, TLD, authoritative) spread worldwide. No single machine holds the whole internet's phone book — that would be a massive single point of failure.

Interview Tips

  • Be ready to explain the famous question "What happens when you type a URL and press Enter?" — the DNS lookup is the first major step of that answer.
  • Mention DNS caching and TTL — it shows you understand why the internet is fast and how load is reduced.
  • Knowing that DNS is a distributed hierarchy (not one server) demonstrates that you think about reliability and avoiding single points of failure.

Key Takeaways

  • IP address = the computer's number (e.g. 142.250.72.206). Domain name = the human-friendly name (e.g. youtube.com).
  • DNS is the "phone book" that translates a domain name into an IP address.
  • A DNS lookup travels a hierarchy: Resolver → Root → TLD → Authoritative server, which finally gives the IP.
  • Caching with TTL means most lookups are instant; only the first one does the full journey.
  • DNS gives only the address — your browser then connects to that server separately to fetch the actual website.
Previous← Client-Server Architecture NextHTTP / HTTPS Protocol →

#6HTTP / HTTPS Protocol

One-line definition

HTTP is the set of rules (a "language") that a client and a server use to talk to each other on the web — how to ask for something and how to reply. HTTPS is the exact same thing but secure: the conversation is encrypted so no one in between can read or tamper with it.

In the previous topics, the client sent a "request" and the server sent a "response". But what language do they speak? What exact format does a request take? The answer is HTTP. It is the foundation of the entire web, so let us understand it thoroughly.

First, What Is a "Protocol"?

A protocol is simply an agreed set of rules that two sides follow so they can understand each other. Humans use protocols all the time without thinking. For example, when you answer a phone call, there is an unspoken protocol: the caller waits, you say "Hello?", then they speak. If both people spoke at once or stayed silent, communication would fail.

Computers need the same kind of agreed rules, but written out very precisely. HTTP (HyperText Transfer Protocol) is the specific protocol for the web. Because both the browser and the server follow the exact same HTTP rules, a browser made by Google (Chrome) can talk perfectly to a server running software written by anyone, anywhere. That shared agreement is what makes the web universal.

Real-life Analogy: Sending a Formal Letter

A letter with a fixed format

Think of an HTTP request like posting a formal letter that must follow a fixed format:

  • The action you want ("I want to read" or "I want to submit") = the HTTP method.
  • The address on the envelope = the URL (which resource on which server).
  • Extra notes on the envelope (your name, language, "fragile") = the headers.
  • The actual contents inside = the body (the data you are sending).

The server reads your "letter", does what you asked, and posts a reply letter back — also in a fixed format. That reply is the HTTP response.

The Anatomy of an HTTP Request

Every HTTP request your browser sends has the same parts. Here is a real example of what one looks like behind the scenes when you log in:

POST /login HTTP/1.1            ← method + path + HTTP version
Host: www.example.com           ← which server
Content-Type: application/json  ← what kind of data is in the body
Authorization: Bearer abc123    ← (a header, e.g. who you are)

{                               ← the body (the actual data)
  "email": "raj@gmail.com",
  "password": "secret123"
}
  • Method (POST): what action you want (explained below).
  • Path (/login): which resource on the server.
  • Headers: extra information about the request (who you are, what format, etc.).
  • Body: the actual data being sent (not all requests have one).

HTTP Methods — The "Verbs"

The method tells the server what kind of action you want to perform. The four most important ones map neatly to everyday actions:

MethodMeansEveryday example
GETRead / fetch dataOpening your Instagram feed
POSTCreate new dataPosting a new photo
PUT / PATCHUpdate existing dataEditing your bio
DELETERemove dataDeleting a post

We will go deeper into how these are used to build clean APIs in the REST API topic. For now, just know that the method communicates intent.

HTTP Status Codes — The Server's Reply Code

When the server replies, it always includes a 3-digit status code that summarises what happened. You have definitely seen 404 before. The first digit tells you the category:

  • 1xx — Informational (rarely seen).
  • 2xx — Success (everything worked).
  • 3xx — Redirection (go look somewhere else).
  • 4xx — Client error (you, the client, did something wrong).
  • 5xx — Server error (the server messed up).
Interactive — Tap a status code to learn it

Click any code to see exactly what it means and when you would see it.

👆 Click a code above to see what it means.

An Important Property: HTTP Is "Stateless"

This is a concept that confuses many beginners, so let us be very clear. Stateless means the server does not remember anything about previous requests. Each HTTP request is treated as brand new, as if it is the first time it has ever seen you.

Imagine a shopkeeper with zero memory: every time you walk up to the counter — even if it is the tenth time in five minutes — he greets you like a total stranger and asks "Who are you?". That is HTTP. The server forgets you the moment it sends the response.

So how do websites "remember" that you are logged in? Because the client re-sends proof of identity (like a token or cookie) with every single request. The server does not remember you; you keep reminding it. We will explore exactly how this works in the topics on sessions and authentication. For now, just lock in this fact: plain HTTP has no memory.

HTTPS — The Secure Version

Plain HTTP has one giant problem: everything is sent as plain, readable text. Anyone who can intercept the traffic — a hacker on the same public Wi-Fi, your internet provider, etc. — can read it all: your passwords, your messages, your card numbers. This is obviously dangerous.

HTTPS (HTTP Secure) fixes this. The "S" stands for Secure. It is the same HTTP, but the entire conversation is encrypted using a technology called TLS (you may also hear the older name SSL). Encryption scrambles the data into meaningless gibberish that only the intended server can unscramble. Even if a hacker captures it, they see useless noise.

Interactive — See what a hacker sees

Imagine you send your password over public Wi-Fi. Toggle between HTTP and HTTPS to see what a hacker watching the network would actually capture.

What travels across the network:

email=raj@gmail.com&password=secret123
🦹 Hacker: "Perfect, I can read your password in plain text!"

What travels across the network:

a8F2$kL9@xPq#7vR!2nW0zYt^bE4mJ6hT*cQsD1gU5oN8...
🦹 Hacker: "It is all scrambled gibberish. Useless to me!"

How HTTPS Works (The Simple Version)

You do not need deep cryptography yet, but here is the simple flow when your browser connects to an HTTPS website:

  1. Your browser asks the server to prove its identity. The server sends an SSL/TLS certificate — like a verified ID card issued by a trusted authority — proving it really is, say, the real youtube.com and not an imposter.
  2. Your browser checks the certificate is valid and trusted. (This is why you see a padlock icon in the address bar.)
  3. The two sides then agree on a secret key (this exchange is called the TLS handshake).
  4. From then on, all data is encrypted with that key. Only these two parties can read it.
The padlock means two things

The padlock icon in your browser confirms (1) the data is encrypted, and (2) the website's identity is verified. That is why you should never enter a password on a site without it.

HTTP vs HTTPS: Side-by-Side

PointHTTPHTTPS
SecurityPlain text, readable by anyoneEncrypted, unreadable to outsiders
Identity checkNoneVerified by a certificate
Default port80443
Address barhttp://, often "Not secure"https:// with a padlock 🔒
Use todayUnsafe, being phased outThe standard for all sites

A Note on HTTP Versions

HTTP has improved over the years, mainly to get faster. You do not need details now, but recognise the names: HTTP/1.1 (the old reliable one), HTTP/2 (allows many requests over one connection at once, much faster), and HTTP/3 (the newest, built for even lower latency). The rules of requests and responses stay the same; these versions just change how efficiently the data travels.

Common Misconceptions (Cleared)

Myth 1: "HTTPS makes my whole website 100% safe."

Reality: HTTPS only secures data while it travels between client and server. It does not protect against weak passwords, bugs in your code, or a hacked server. It is essential, but it is one layer of security, not all of it.

Myth 2: "The server remembers me between requests."

Reality: HTTP is stateless — the server forgets you after each response. The feeling of "being remembered" comes from the client re-sending a token or cookie with every request.

Myth 3: "HTTP and HTTPS are completely different protocols."

Reality: They are the same HTTP. HTTPS is just HTTP wrapped in an encryption layer (TLS). The request/response rules are identical underneath.

Interview Tips

  • Always remember and state that HTTP is stateless — it comes up constantly and leads naturally into discussions of sessions, cookies, and tokens.
  • Know the status code categories (2xx, 4xx, 5xx). Being able to say "that should return a 404" or "a 500 means our server crashed" sounds professional.
  • Be able to explain in one line why HTTPS matters: "it encrypts data in transit and verifies server identity."
  • If asked to design any web system, assume HTTPS everywhere — it is the modern default.

Key Takeaways

  • HTTP is the agreed language (protocol) of rules for client-server communication on the web.
  • A request has a method, path, headers, and body; a response includes a status code (2xx success, 4xx your error, 5xx server error).
  • HTTP methods: GET (read), POST (create), PUT/PATCH (update), DELETE (remove).
  • HTTP is stateless — the server remembers nothing between requests; the client re-sends identity each time.
  • HTTPS = HTTP + encryption (TLS). It scrambles data so outsiders cannot read it and verifies the server's identity (the padlock 🔒).
Previous← IP, DNS & Domain Name NextTCP vs UDP →

#7TCP vs UDP

One-line definition

TCP and UDP are two different methods for sending data across a network. TCP is reliable but slower — it guarantees every piece arrives, correctly and in order. UDP is fast but unreliable — it just fires the data off quickly without checking if it all arrived.

This topic sounds technical, but the idea behind it is actually very simple. Let us start with an everyday situation and build up slowly. By the end, TCP and UDP will feel obvious.

Start Here: Two Ways to Tell Your Friend Something

Imagine you want to tell your friend a 5-word sentence, one word at a time, from across a noisy room. There are two possible styles you could use:

Style 1 — The careful way

After every word, you wait for your friend to shout back "got it!". If they did not hear a word, you say it again. You even check "can you hear me?" before you start.

Result: the full sentence always reaches them, correct and complete — but it takes more time.

Style 2 — The fast way

You just say all 5 words quickly, one after another, without waiting for any reply. If your friend misses a word, oh well — you do not repeat it.

Result: super fast — but a word or two might get missed.

That is the whole idea

The careful way is TCP. The fast way is UDP. Everything else in this topic is just detail added on top of this one simple picture. Keep this in your head and you cannot get confused.

Quick context: what are these two, really?

TCP and UDP are the two main ways data actually travels across the internet (their formal name is "transport protocols" — the things that carry your data). One nice fact: the web (HTTP) uses TCP, because web pages must arrive complete. So TCP is what delivered this very page to your screen.

TCP — The Careful Way (Reliable)

TCP is the "careful friend" from our example. Its one job is to make sure every piece of data arrives, correctly, and in the right order. It does four simple things to guarantee this. You do not need to memorise the technical names — just understand the plain meaning:

What TCP doesWhat it actually means (plain words)
Connects first
(the "handshake")		Before sending anything, both sides quickly say "ready?" / "ready!" to confirm they are connected. Like saying "hello, can you hear me?" before you start talking.
Waits for "got it!"
(an "acknowledgement")		After each piece, the receiver replies "got it!". This is exactly like the double blue ticks on WhatsApp telling you the message was received.
Resends lost pieces
(a "retransmission")		If a "got it!" never comes back, TCP knows that piece was lost — so it simply sends it again. This is why nothing is ever lost.
Fixes the orderEach piece is numbered, so even if they arrive jumbled up, TCP arranges them back into the correct order before handing them over.

All these checks make TCP completely reliable — nothing is ever lost or out of order. But the checks take a little extra time. That is simply the price of being careful.

UDP — The Fast Way (Speedy)

UDP is the "fast friend". It just sends the data as quickly as possible and skips all of TCP's careful checks:

  • It does not connect first — it starts sending right away.
  • It does not wait for any "got it!" reply.
  • If a piece gets lost, UDP does not resend it — that piece is simply gone.
  • It does not bother fixing the order either.

This might sound careless, but skipping all those checks makes UDP much faster. And for some apps, that speed matters far more than catching every single piece — as you will see in the demo below.

The one question that decides which to use

Whenever you must choose, just ask yourself: "If one piece gets lost, is it worth stopping to resend it?"

• If YES (a file, a web page, a payment — every piece matters) → use TCP.
• If NO (a live video call — an old lost frame is useless by the time it could arrive) → use UDP.

Interactive Demo — The same problem, two real apps

Press Start. On the left, UDP is powering a live video call (like a WhatsApp call). On the right, TCP is powering a file download. In both, packet 3 gets lost on the way — watch how each one reacts, and why that reaction is perfect for its job.

🎥 UDP — powering a LIVE VIDEO CALL (video frames)
📥 TCP — powering a FILE DOWNLOAD (file chunks)

TCP vs UDP: Side-by-Side

PointTCPUDP
ReliabilityGuaranteed deliveryNo guarantee (can drop)
OrderingAlways in orderMay arrive out of order
SpeedSlower (more checks)Faster (no checks)
ConnectionNeeds a handshake firstNo setup, sends instantly
ConfirmationYes (acknowledgements)None
Best forAccuracy mattersSpeed matters

When to Use Which?

Use TCP when accuracy is essential

If losing even one byte would break things, use TCP. Examples:

  • Web pages (HTTP/HTTPS) — a half-loaded page is useless.
  • Email — you cannot drop words from a message.
  • File downloads — a missing chunk corrupts the file.
  • Banking / payments — every bit must be correct.

Use UDP when speed beats perfection

If being fast and live matters more than catching every packet, use UDP. Examples:

  • Video / voice calls (WhatsApp, Zoom) — a tiny glitch is better than a delay.
  • Live streaming — keep the stream moving.
  • Online games — the latest position matters, not an old lost one.
  • DNS lookups — small, quick, just retry if needed.

Common Misconceptions (Cleared)

Myth 1: "UDP is broken or bad because it loses data."

Reality: UDP is not broken — it is designed to skip reliability for speed. For live video and gaming, that is a feature, not a bug. The "right" choice depends entirely on the use case.

Myth 2: "TCP is always the better, safer choice."

Reality: TCP's guarantees add delay. For real-time applications, that delay is worse than a little data loss. There is no universally "better" one — only the better fit for the job.

Myth 3: "Video calls use TCP because video is important."

Reality: Most real-time video and voice calls use UDP. Resending a lost video frame from a second ago is pointless — the call has already moved on. Staying live matters more than perfection.

Interview Tips

  • Frame your answer around the trade-off: TCP = reliability over speed, UDP = speed over reliability. Interviewers love hearing trade-off thinking.
  • Have ready examples for each: "HTTP and file transfer use TCP; video calls, gaming, and DNS use UDP."
  • Mention that HTTP runs on TCP — it connects this topic to the previous one and shows depth.
  • If asked to design something real-time (like a video call system), proposing UDP and explaining why instantly shows strong understanding.

Key Takeaways

  • TCP = reliable but slower: handshake, acknowledgements, retransmission, and ordering guarantee perfect delivery.
  • UDP = fast but unreliable: no setup, no confirmation, no resending — lost data stays lost.
  • It is a classic trade-off: reliability (TCP) versus speed (UDP).
  • Use TCP for web, email, files, payments. Use UDP for video/voice calls, live streaming, gaming, DNS.
  • HTTP rides on TCP, which is why web pages always arrive complete.
Previous← HTTP / HTTPS Protocol NextREST API Basics →

#8REST API Basics

One-line definition

An API is a way for two pieces of software to talk to each other. A REST API is the most popular style of building that conversation over the web — using clear web addresses (URLs) for things, and standard HTTP methods (GET, POST, etc.) to act on them.

This topic ties together everything from the last few chapters. The client and server (Topic 4) talk using HTTP (Topic 6). But how should we organise that talking so it is clean, predictable, and easy for any developer to use? The answer the whole industry settled on is REST. Let us build it up from the very beginning.

First, What Is an API?

API stands for Application Programming Interface. That sounds complex, but the idea is simple: it is a defined way to ask one program to do something for you, without needing to know how it works inside.

You use APIs in real life constantly. A power socket is an API: you plug in any device using the standard shape, and you get electricity. You do not need to understand the power plant. The socket is a clean, agreed "interface" between you and the electricity system. A software API is the same: a clean, agreed way for your app to request something (data, an action) from another system.

Analogy: The waiter is the API

At a restaurant, you (the client) do not walk into the kitchen (the server) and start cooking. Instead, you talk to the waiter. The waiter is the API: a defined messenger who knows how to take your order, carry it to the kitchen, and bring back the food. You only need to know how to talk to the waiter — not how the kitchen works. The menu is like the API documentation: it tells you exactly what you are allowed to ask for.

What Does "REST" Actually Mean? (Kept Simple)

Forget the scary full form ("Representational State Transfer") — honestly, the name does not matter at all. Here is all REST really is: a set of simple, tidy rules for naming things and asking for them, so that every developer builds their API the same, predictable way.

Think of it like a well-organised library. In a good library, every book has a clear address (a shelf and a number), and there are clear actions you can take: borrow, return, reserve. Because everything is tidy and consistent, anyone can walk in and use it without confusion. REST does exactly this, but for data on the web.

And the best news: REST is built on just two simple ideas. Genuinely, that is all you need to get started:

Idea 1: Name the "thing" in the URL

Every thing your app deals with (a user, a photo, an order) gets its own web address. That address is always a noun — the name of a thing.

/users = "all users".
/users/2 = "the user with id 2".

Idea 2: Say the "action" with the method

What you want to do with that thing comes from the HTTP method (from Topic 6), not from the URL.

GET = read it.
POST = create.
DELETE = remove.

Now put the two ideas together and you get a complete instruction. GET /users/2 simply reads as: "GET (read) the user with id 2." Notice how it reads almost like plain English — action + thing. That readability is the entire point of REST. An API built this way is called RESTful.

Two smaller rules, just so you have heard them

REST also likes the conversation to be stateless (the server remembers nothing between requests — exactly like HTTP in Topic 6, so the client simply re-sends its identity each time), and data is usually sent as JSON (a simple text format we cover in the very next topic). Do not worry about these yet — the two ideas above are the real heart of REST.

Resources and Endpoints

A resource is a thing. An endpoint is the URL (address) that points to that resource. The beauty of REST is how logically these are organised. Look at this pattern for "users":

EndpointWhat it refers to
/usersThe whole collection of all users
/users/2One specific user (the one with id 2)
/users/2/postsAll posts belonging to user 2

Notice these are all nouns. You never see /getUser or /deleteUser in good REST design. The URL only names the thing; the action comes from the HTTP method. This separation is the heart of REST.

Mapping Methods to Actions (CRUD)

Almost everything an app does to data falls into four actions, nicknamed CRUD: Create, Read, Update, Delete. REST maps each to an HTTP method. This is the single most important table in this topic:

Action (CRUD)HTTP MethodExampleMeaning
ReadGETGET /users/2Fetch user 2's details
CreatePOSTPOST /usersAdd a new user
UpdatePUT / PATCHPUT /users/2Update user 2
DeleteDELETEDELETE /users/2Remove user 2

See the elegance? The same URL /users/2 can be read, updated, or deleted — just by changing the method. The address stays the same; the verb changes the action.

Interactive Demo — Try a REST API

Click any API call below to see the exact request the client sends and the response the server returns (with its status code). This is a realistic "users" API.

Request
Click an API call above →
Response 
…

Anatomy of a REST Call

Putting it together, here is a full REST request and response for creating a new user. Notice how it uses the HTTP knowledge from Topic 6:

Request:
POST /users HTTP/1.1
Host: api.example.com
Content-Type: application/json

{ "name": "Amit", "email": "amit@gmail.com" }

Response:
201 Created
Content-Type: application/json

{ "id": 3, "name": "Amit", "email": "amit@gmail.com" }

The client said "create a user" (POST to the /users collection) with the data in the body. The server created it and replied 201 Created along with the new user, including the id it assigned. Clean and predictable.

Good REST Design Practices

These small rules are what separate a clean API from a messy one, and interviewers notice them:

  • Use nouns, not verbs, in URLs: /users, not /getUsers. The method already says the action.
  • Use plural nouns for collections: /users and /users/2, consistently.
  • Use the right status codes: 200/201 for success, 404 when not found, 400 for bad input, 500 for server errors.
  • Version your API: put /v1/ in the path (like /v1/users) so you can change things later without breaking old apps.
  • Keep it stateless: every request carries its own authentication (like a token), since the server remembers nothing.

Why REST Became So Popular

  • Simple and predictable: once you learn the style, every REST API feels familiar.
  • Uses plain HTTP: no special tools needed — it works in any browser, language, or device.
  • Stateless and scalable: because the server holds no memory of clients, you can add more servers easily (a big deal for scaling, as we will see later).
  • Human-readable: the URLs and JSON are easy to read and debug.

Common Misconceptions (Cleared)

Myth 1: "REST is a protocol or a technology."

Reality: REST is a style / set of guidelines, not a protocol. It uses the existing HTTP protocol. You cannot "install REST" — you just design your API to follow REST principles.
