Myth 2: "REST must always use JSON."

Reality: JSON is the most common format, but REST can technically use others (like XML). JSON just won because it is simple and lightweight (more on this next topic).

Myth 3: "The URL should describe the action, like /createUser."

Reality: In REST, the URL names the resource (a noun), and the HTTP method describes the action. So it is POST /users, never /createUser.

Interview Tips

  • Be able to instantly map CRUD to HTTP methods (Create=POST, Read=GET, Update=PUT/PATCH, Delete=DELETE). This comes up very often.
  • Emphasise that REST is stateless — this directly enables horizontal scaling, which interviewers love to connect.
  • When designing any system, you will define the API endpoints early. Showing clean, noun-based, RESTful endpoints signals strong fundamentals.
  • Know one alternative exists (GraphQL, gRPC) — we compare them in a later topic — so you can mention REST is one choice among a few.

Key Takeaways

  • An API is a defined way for software to talk to software; the waiter between client and server.
  • REST is a popular style of web API: resources have noun-based URLs, and HTTP methods act on them.
  • CRUD → HTTP: Create=POST, Read=GET, Update=PUT/PATCH, Delete=DELETE.
  • Same URL (/users/2), different method = different action. URLs are nouns; methods are verbs.
  • REST is stateless, uses plain HTTP, usually exchanges JSON, and scales easily — which is why it dominates the web.
Previous← TCP vs UDP NextJSON vs XML →

#9JSON vs XML

One-line definition

JSON and XML are two different ways of writing down data as text so that it can be sent between a client and a server. They both carry the same information — they just look different. JSON is short and clean; XML is longer and uses tags.

In the last topic, our REST API sent back data like { "name": "Priya" }. That format is called JSON. But why that format? Could it be written another way? Yes — and the main alternative is XML. Let us understand both in the simplest possible way.

First: Why Do We Even Need a "Data Format"?

Here is the basic problem. A server wants to send some data — say, a user's name, age, and city — to a phone. But data can only travel across the internet as plain text. So the server must write the data down as text in a way that the phone can read back and understand perfectly.

This is exactly like filling a form. If everyone writes their details in a clear, agreed structure, anyone can read it back correctly. A data format is just that agreed structure for writing data as text. JSON and XML are two such agreed structures.

Analogy: Writing down an address

Imagine telling someone your address so they can write it down. One person writes it as a short, neat note. Another writes it as a full labelled form ("House Name: ...", "Street: ...", "City: ..."). Both contain the same address — one is just shorter, the other more labelled and longer. JSON is the short neat note; XML is the fully labelled form.

JSON — The Short, Clean One

JSON stands for JavaScript Object Notation, but you do not need to know JavaScript to use it. The idea is beautifully simple: data is written as key-value pairs — a label, then its value. A "key" is the name of the thing (like name), and the "value" is the actual data (like "Priya").

{
  "name": "Priya",
  "age": 24,
  "city": "Mumbai"
}

Read it like plain language: the name is "Priya", the age is 24, the city is "Mumbai". The curly braces { } simply mean "here is one object (one thing) with these details". It is easy for both humans and computers to read — which is exactly why it became so popular.

XML — The Tag-Based One

XML stands for eXtensible Markup Language. If you have seen HTML, XML looks very familiar: every piece of data is wrapped inside an opening tag and a closing tag, like <name>Priya</name>. The same user data looks like this in XML:

<user>
  <name>Priya</name>
  <age>24</age>
  <city>Mumbai</city>
</user>

It carries the exact same information as the JSON above. But notice the difference: each piece of data is written twice in tags (once to open <name>, once to close </name>). This makes XML more descriptive, but also much longer and heavier.

Interactive — Same data, two formats

This is the exact same information about one user. Toggle between the two formats and watch how much shorter and cleaner JSON is compared to XML.

{
  "name": "Priya",
  "age": 24,
  "city": "Mumbai",
  "premium": true
}
📏 About 70 characters — short, clean, easy to read.
<user>
  <name>Priya</name>
  <age>24</age>
  <city>Mumbai</city>
  <premium>true</premium>
</user>
📏 About 130 characters — nearly double, because every tag is repeated.

Side-by-Side Comparison

PointJSONXML
StyleKey-value pairs { }Opening & closing tags
SizeShorter, lighterLonger, heavier
Easy to read?Very easyHarder (more clutter)
Speed to processFasterSlower
Best friendWeb & mobile apps, REST APIsOlder / enterprise systems
Popularity todayThe default choiceLess common, still around

Why JSON Won (Mostly)

Today, JSON is the default for almost all modern web and mobile APIs. Here is why it took over:

  • It is smaller: less text means less data to send, so it travels faster and is cheaper — a big deal when serving millions of users.
  • It is easier to read: for both humans (debugging) and computers (processing).
  • It fits the web naturally: JSON came from JavaScript, the language of web browsers, so it plugs in effortlessly.

So Is XML Dead? No.

XML is older, but it is still used in plenty of places, and it has some genuine strengths. It is more descriptive and can carry extra information (like attributes and comments), and many large, older "enterprise" systems — banks, government systems, and a style of API called SOAP — were built on XML and still run on it. Configuration files for some tools are also written in XML. So you will still meet it; it is just no longer the first choice for new web apps.

Common Misconceptions (Cleared)

Myth 1: "JSON and XML are programming languages."

Reality: Neither is a programming language. You cannot write logic or loops in them. They are just formats for writing down data as text — nothing more.

Myth 2: "You need to know JavaScript to use JSON."

Reality: JSON only borrowed its look from JavaScript. Every programming language (Python, Java, C++, etc.) can read and write JSON easily. The name is just history.

Myth 3: "JSON is always better than XML."

Reality: For modern web apps, JSON is usually the better fit. But XML is not "worse" — it is more descriptive and is the right choice in certain enterprise and document-heavy systems. It depends on the use case.

Interview Tips

  • If asked "which format would your API use?", the safe modern answer is JSON — and you can explain why (lighter, faster, easier, web-native).
  • Be able to write a small example of both from memory. It shows you genuinely understand the difference, not just the buzzwords.
  • Mention that the smaller size of JSON matters at scale — saving a few bytes per response adds up massively across millions of requests. This connects to performance, which interviewers love.

Key Takeaways

  • JSON and XML are both just ways to write data as text for sending between client and server — not programming languages.
  • JSON uses simple key-value pairs — short, clean, fast, and the modern default.
  • XML uses opening and closing tags (like HTML) — more descriptive but longer and heavier.
  • JSON won for the web because it is smaller, easier, and faster; XML still lives on in older and enterprise systems.
  • They carry the same information — the choice is about size, readability, and fit, not about what data they can hold.
Previous← REST API Basics NextLatency, Throughput & Bandwidth →

#10Latency, Throughput & Bandwidth

One-line definition

Latency = the delay before data starts arriving (how long you wait). Bandwidth = the maximum amount of data that could flow at once (the capacity). Throughput = the actual amount of data that does flow per second (real-world speed).

These three words measure the "speed" of a system, and they are constantly mixed up — even by experienced people. By the end of this topic you will tell them apart instantly. The secret is one simple analogy that makes all three obvious at once.

The Master Analogy: A Highway

Cars travelling on a highway

Imagine cars (your data) driving from City A to City B on a highway:

  • Latency = how long one car takes to travel from A to B. It is a time (measured in milliseconds). Less is better.
  • Bandwidth = how many lanes the highway has. More lanes means more cars can travel side by side. It is the maximum capacity.
  • Throughput = how many cars actually reach City B each second in reality (after traffic jams, slow cars, etc.). It is the real achieved rate.

Hold this highway picture in your mind. Now each term becomes easy.

Latency — The Waiting Time

Latency is the delay between asking for something and starting to get it. It answers: "How long do I have to wait?" It is measured in milliseconds (ms), and lower is better.

You feel latency every day. When a video call has "lag" and the other person's reply comes a second late — that is high latency. When an online game feels laggy — high latency. The famous "ping" number gamers care about is latency. A big cause of latency is simply distance: data sent to a server on the other side of the planet takes longer than to one in your own city (which is exactly why we later use CDNs to place servers near users).

Bandwidth — The Maximum Capacity

Bandwidth is the most data that could travel through the connection in one second, if conditions were perfect. It is the highway's number of lanes — the theoretical maximum. It is measured in things like Mbps (megabits per second). When your internet plan says "100 Mbps", that is bandwidth: the maximum it promises.

Throughput — The Real Speed You Actually Get

Throughput is how much data actually gets through per second in the real world. Bandwidth is the promise; throughput is the reality. Just like a 4-lane highway rarely carries the maximum number of cars (because of traffic, accidents, slow vehicles), your real throughput is usually less than your bandwidth, because of network congestion, errors, and other slowdowns.

The key relationship

Bandwidth is the maximum possible; throughput is what you really get. Throughput can never be higher than bandwidth, and is usually a bit lower. Think: bandwidth = the size of the pipe, throughput = the water actually flowing through it right now.

Interactive — Download on slow vs fast internet

Click a connection to download the same 10 MB file. Watch the latency (the short wait before anything happens) and the throughput (how fast the bar then fills). Notice the fast connection has both lower latency and higher throughput.

Latency (wait)
—
Throughput (speed)
—
Total time
—
Pick a connection above to start the download…

The Big Insight: High Bandwidth Does NOT Mean Low Latency

This is the point that confuses everyone, so let us nail it. Bandwidth and latency are completely separate things. You can have one without the other:

  • High bandwidth but high latency: imagine a 100-lane highway, but City B is on another continent. Tons of cars can flow (high bandwidth), but each one still takes a long time to arrive (high latency). Like a satellite internet connection: lots of capacity, but a noticeable delay.
  • Low bandwidth but low latency: a 1-lane road between two neighbouring houses. Few cars at a time (low bandwidth), but each arrives almost instantly (low latency).
A famous way to picture it

A truck full of hard drives driving across the country has enormous bandwidth (it carries a massive amount of data) but terrible latency (it takes two days to arrive). A single text message over fibre has tiny bandwidth but amazing latency (it arrives in milliseconds). Different jobs need different priorities.

Which One Matters When?

When latency matters most

  • Online gaming — you need instant reactions.
  • Video/voice calls — delay makes talking awkward.
  • Stock trading — milliseconds mean money.
  • Any "click and it should respond now" action.

When bandwidth/throughput matters most

  • Streaming HD/4K video — lots of data per second.
  • Downloading big files — you want it all fast.
  • Backing up data to the cloud.
  • Any "move a lot of data" task.

Side-by-Side Comparison

TermMeasuresUnitHighway meaning
LatencyDelay / waiting timemilliseconds (ms)Time for one car A→B
BandwidthMaximum capacityMbpsNumber of lanes
ThroughputActual data per secondMbpsCars actually arriving/sec

Common Misconceptions (Cleared)

Myth 1: "More bandwidth always means a faster experience."

Reality: If your problem is latency (delay), adding bandwidth will not help. A video call does not get smoother by buying a bigger internet plan if the lag comes from distance. Match the fix to the real problem.

Myth 2: "Bandwidth and throughput are the same thing."

Reality: Bandwidth is the maximum possible; throughput is the actual achieved. Real throughput is almost always lower than the advertised bandwidth due to congestion and overhead.

Myth 3: "Latency can be reduced to zero."

Reality: Latency can be made very small, but never zero — data cannot travel faster than the speed of light. That physical limit is why placing servers closer to users (via CDNs) is the main way to cut latency.

Interview Tips

  • Keep the one-liners ready: latency = delay, bandwidth = max capacity, throughput = actual rate. Crisp definitions impress.
  • Always state your targets with numbers: "we want latency under 100 ms" sounds far more professional than "we want it fast".
  • If a system feels slow, diagnose which of the three is the bottleneck before proposing a fix — this shows real engineering thinking.
  • Connect latency to CDNs and caching (upcoming topics): putting data closer to users is the classic way to reduce it.

Key Takeaways

  • Latency = the waiting delay (in ms). Lower is better. Caused largely by distance.
  • Bandwidth = the maximum data capacity (the number of highway lanes).
  • Throughput = the actual data delivered per second (cars that really arrive). Usually a bit less than bandwidth.
  • High bandwidth does not mean low latency — they are separate. A 100-lane road to another continent is still slow per car.
  • Latency matters for real-time things (gaming, calls); bandwidth matters for large data things (streaming, downloads).
Previous← JSON vs XML NextMonolithic Architecture →

#11Monolithic Architecture

One-line definition

A Monolithic Architecture means the entire application is built as one single, large unit. All the features — login, payments, search, everything — live together in one codebase and run as one program.

The word "monolith" means "one giant stone". A monolithic app is exactly that: one big block of software where all the parts are tightly joined together and shipped as a single deployable program. This is how almost every successful application starts — including giants like Amazon, Netflix, Twitter, and Instagram, which all began life as monoliths — because it is by far the simplest and fastest way to build something that works.

Let us slow down and really understand what "one single unit" means, because this is the foundation for the next two topics. In a monolith, your entire product — every feature your users can see and use — lives inside one project, compiled or packaged into one artifact, and run as one process. When you start the app, all of it starts together. When you stop it, all of it stops together.

Real-life Analogy: A One-Room Shop

Everything under one roof

Imagine a small shop where one person handles everything in a single room — billing, storage, customer service, and cleaning, all in the same space. When the shop is small, this is wonderful: there is no overhead, no coordination, and the owner can do anything instantly. But two problems appear as it grows. First, if that one room catches fire, the entire shop stops — billing, storage, everything goes down at once. Second, if you hire 5 people, they all crowd into the same little room, bumping into each other and arguing over the same shelves. That crowding-and-fragility is exactly what happens to a monolithic codebase as the product and the team grow.

What Actually Lives Inside a Monolith

To make this concrete, picture a typical e-commerce app built as a monolith. Inside that one codebase you would find all of these, calling each other directly as ordinary function calls:

  • User module — signup, login, profiles.
  • Product catalog module — listings, search, categories.
  • Cart & checkout module — adding items, calculating totals.
  • Payment module — charging cards.
  • Order module — placing and tracking orders.
  • Notification module — sending emails and SMS.

Crucially, all of these talk to one shared database, and they communicate with each other simply by calling functions in memory — for example, the checkout code just calls paymentModule.charge(order) directly. There is no network between them, which is why a monolith is so fast internally.

How It Works: The Build and Deploy Cycle

In a monolith, all the code is written, built, tested, and deployed as one piece. This single fact drives almost every pro and con. When you make even a tiny change — say, fixing the colour of the login button — you must rebuild and redeploy the entire application, including payments, search, and everything else, even though none of those changed.

Users
→
Single Applicationlogin + search + payments + orders all together
→
One Database
One big unit holds every feature and talks to one shared database.

Why a Single Bug Can Take Everything Down

Because everything runs in one process and shares the same memory, a serious problem in any one part can crash the whole thing. If the search feature has a memory leak, or the notification code goes into an infinite loop, it consumes the resources of the single process — and your payments and checkout go down with it, even though they were written perfectly. There is no wall between the modules. This is the flip side of the speed advantage: tight coupling means tight blast radius.

Signs Your Monolith Is Starting to Hurt

A monolith is not "bad" — but there are clear warning signs that tell you it has outgrown its usefulness:

  • Deploys become scary and slow — a 30-second change requires redeploying a huge app, and everyone is afraid to ship.
  • Teams block each other — 50 engineers editing one codebase causes constant merge conflicts and coordination overhead.
  • You can't scale the hot part — only search is overloaded, but you are forced to run more copies of the entire app to cope.
  • One module's failure keeps taking down unrelated features.
  • The codebase is so large that builds and tests take forever.

These exact pains are what pushed companies like Amazon and Netflix to eventually split into microservices — which we cover next.

Bonus concept: the "Modular Monolith"

There is a smart middle path. A modular monolith is still deployed as one unit (keeping the simplicity), but the code inside is organised into clean, well-separated modules with clear boundaries. This gives you most of the organisational benefits of microservices without the operational complexity — and makes it much easier to split into microservices later if you ever need to. Many experienced teams now recommend starting here.

Strengths and Weaknesses

Strengths

  • Simple to build at the start — one codebase, one place.
  • Easy to test and deploy — just one app to ship.
  • Fast internally — features call each other directly, no network in between.
  • Great for small teams and new products.

Weaknesses

  • Hard to scale parts — you must scale the whole thing, even if only search is busy.
  • One bug can crash everything.
  • Slow to update as it grows — every change redeploys the whole app.
  • Teams step on each other in one giant codebase.
Important: monolith is NOT "bad"

Starting with a monolith is often the smartest choice for a new app. It is simple and fast to build, easy to reason about, and lets a small team move incredibly quickly. The problems only appear when the app and team grow very large — which is when people consider microservices (next topic). Reaching for microservices on day one is one of the most common beginner mistakes.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Monolith means messy/spaghetti code." False — a monolith can be beautifully organised (a modular monolith). "Monolith" describes how it is deployed (one unit), not how clean the code is.

"Big companies don't use monoliths." False — many still run large, successful monoliths, and almost all started as one. Even where microservices exist, individual services are often small monoliths internally.

"Monolith can't scale." Misleading — a monolith scales fine horizontally (run many copies behind a load balancer); what it can't do is scale individual features independently.

Interview tip

If asked "monolith or microservices for a new startup?", the strong answer is: "Start with a monolith (or modular monolith) for speed and simplicity, and only split into microservices when you feel real pain — team size, deploy friction, or independent scaling needs." This shows mature judgement instead of chasing trends.

Key Takeaways

  • A monolith = the whole app as one single unit: one codebase, one build, one deployment, one shared database.
  • Modules talk via direct in-memory function calls — no network — which is why it is fast internally.
  • Pros: simple to build, test, and deploy; fast internally; great for small teams and early-stage products.
  • Cons: can't scale parts independently, one bug can take down everything, deploys get slow and risky, teams collide as it grows.
  • It is the right starting point for most projects — not a mistake. A modular monolith is a great middle ground.
Previous← Latency, Throughput & Bandwidth NextMicroservices Architecture →

#12Microservices Architecture

One-line definition

Microservices Architecture means breaking the application into many small, independent services, where each service handles one specific job (like payments, or search) and runs on its own. They talk to each other over the network (usually via APIs).

If a monolith is "one giant stone", microservices are "many small bricks". Instead of one huge program, you build lots of small programs, each focused on a single business capability, each able to be developed, deployed, and scaled on its own. This is not just a coding style — it is an architectural philosophy that reshapes how teams are organised and how the whole system behaves under load and failure.

The core promise of microservices is independence. Each service is owned by a team, lives in its own repository, has its own database, runs as its own process (or many copies), and can be released without waiting for or affecting any other service. That independence is the prize — and almost everything good and bad about microservices flows from it.

Real-life Analogy: A Food Court

Many independent stalls

A monolith is one giant kitchen cooking every cuisine. Microservices are a food court: separate stalls for pizza, dosa, Chinese, and juice. Each stall runs independently — if the pizza stall closes for cleaning, you can still buy dosa. If the juice stall gets crowded in summer, you can add more juice staff without touching the pizza stall. Each stall has its own counter, its own staff, and its own cash box (its own database). But there is a cost: the stalls now need a shared map and signage so customers can find them, and if the dosa stall needs an ingredient from the pizza stall, they have to physically walk over and ask — slower than reaching across one kitchen. That "walking over" is the network call between services.

The Golden Rule: Database Per Service

This is the single most important — and most misunderstood — principle of microservices. Each service owns its own private database, and no other service is allowed to touch it directly. If the Order service needs user data, it must ask the User service through its API; it can never reach into the User service's database tables.

Why be so strict? Because if many services shared one database, they would become secretly coupled through it — changing one table could break five services, and you would lose the independence that was the whole point. A private database keeps each service a true black box that can evolve freely behind its API.

How Services Talk to Each Other

Since services are separate programs (often on separate machines), they communicate over the network, in two main styles:

  • Synchronous (request/response): one service directly calls another and waits for the reply — using REST or gRPC (Topics 8 and 45). Simple, but creates a temporary dependency: if the called service is slow or down, the caller is stuck.
  • Asynchronous (events/messages): a service publishes an event to a queue or message bus and moves on; interested services react later (Topics 38–40). More resilient and decoupled, but harder to trace.
Users
→
API Gateway
↓
User Serviceown DB
Order Serviceown DB
Payment Serviceown DB
Notification Serviceown DB
Many small services, each owning its private database, talking over the network through an API gateway.

Full Walkthrough: Placing an Order

Let us trace one real request through a microservices e-commerce app, so the abstract idea becomes concrete:

  • 1. You tap "Place Order". The request hits the API Gateway (Topic 50), the single front door.
  • 2. The gateway routes it to the Order service, which creates an order record in its own database.
  • 3. The Order service calls the Payment service over the network to charge your card.
  • 4. On success, the Order service publishes an "order_placed" event. The Notification service reacts by emailing you, and the Inventory service reacts by reducing stock — all independently.
  • 5. Each of these services can be scaled, deployed, and even rewritten separately without the others knowing.

Notice how a single user action becomes a small orchestra of independent services cooperating over the network.

How the Real Giants Use Microservices

Netflix, Amazon, Uber

Netflix famously runs hundreds of microservices — separate services for recommendations, playback, billing, search, and more — so that, for example, if the recommendations service has a bad day, you can still hit play on a movie. Amazon moved from a monolith to microservices so its thousands of engineers could ship independently; the rule "every team exposes its data only through service APIs" became legendary. Uber split its early monolith into services for trips, pricing, matching, and payments to scale each independently as it grew across cities. In every case, the move happened because of scale and team size — not because microservices are inherently "better".

The Hidden Cost: You Are Now Building a Distributed System

This is the part beginners underestimate. The moment your features talk over a network instead of in-memory function calls, you inherit the hard problems of distributed systems (which Part 8 covers in depth):

  • The network is unreliable — calls can fail, time out, or arrive twice. You need retries, timeouts, and circuit breakers (Part 7).
  • No more easy transactions — you can't wrap a change across services in one database transaction; you need patterns like Saga (Topic 63).
  • Debugging is harder — one request spans many services, so you need distributed tracing (Topic 83).
  • Operational overhead — many services to deploy, monitor, and secure, which demands strong DevOps, containers, and orchestration (Part 12).

Strengths and Weaknesses

Strengths

  • Scale each part separately — add more Payment servers without touching others.
  • One service failing does not crash the whole app.
  • Teams work independently on their own service.
  • Each service can use a different technology if it suits.

Weaknesses

  • Much more complex — many moving parts to manage.
  • Network calls between services add latency and can fail.
  • Harder to test and debug across services.
  • Needs strong DevOps (monitoring, deployment automation).
Common misconceptions, cleared

"Microservices are always better than monoliths." False — for a small app or team, they add huge complexity for little benefit and will slow you down. They pay off at scale.

"Microservices means tiny services." Not exactly — "micro" refers to a focused business capability, not lines of code. A service should be as small as makes sense, not artificially tiny.

"Each service must have its own database server." It needs its own data ownership (its own schema/tables that others can't touch) — it doesn't necessarily need a separate physical server.

Interview tip

When proposing microservices, always justify it with scale and team independence, and immediately acknowledge the costs you will pay (network failures, distributed transactions, observability). Then mention the mitigations: API gateway, async events, sagas, circuit breakers, and tracing. Showing you know the downsides is what separates a senior answer from a buzzword answer.

Key Takeaways

  • Microservices = the app split into many small, independent services, each owning one business capability and its own private database.
  • Services communicate over the network — synchronously (REST/gRPC) or asynchronously (events).
  • Pros: independent scaling, fault isolation, independent teams, freedom to choose tech per service.
  • Cons: you inherit all distributed-system problems — network failures, no easy transactions, harder debugging, heavy ops.
  • Best for large apps and large teams (Netflix, Amazon, Uber) — and only after a monolith starts to hurt.
Previous← Monolithic Architecture NextMonolith vs Microservices →

#13Monolith vs Microservices

One-line definition

This topic directly compares the two architectures so you know which to choose and when. In short: start with a monolith for simplicity, and move to microservices only when scale and team size make the monolith painful.

Now that you understand both (Topics 11 and 12), let us put them head to head. This is one of the most common system design discussion points, so it is worth being crystal clear.

Interactive — See both at a glance

Toggle to compare how each one handles the same situations.

  • Deploy: ship the whole app at once.
  • Scaling: scale the entire app together.
  • A bug: can crash the whole application.
  • Team: everyone works in one codebase.
  • Best when: small app, small team, early stage.
  • Deploy: ship each service separately.
  • Scaling: scale only the busy service.
  • A bug: usually contained to one service.
  • Team: each team owns its own service.
  • Best when: large app, many teams, high scale.

Full Comparison Table

PointMonolithMicroservices
StructureOne single unitMany small services
ComplexityLow (simple)High (many parts)
DeploymentWhole app togetherEach service alone
ScalingScale everythingScale parts independently
Fault isolationOne bug can crash allFailures stay contained
Speed to build (early)FastSlow (lots of setup)
Best team sizeSmallLarge

How to Actually Decide (a practical checklist)

Instead of guessing, ask yourself these concrete questions. The more you answer "yes", the more microservices make sense:

  • Is your team large? If 50+ engineers keep colliding in one codebase, splitting into service-owning teams helps. If you are a team of 5, a monolith is almost certainly right.
  • Do different parts have very different scaling needs? If search gets 100× the traffic of billing, being able to scale them separately is valuable.
  • Are deploys slow and scary? If shipping a one-line fix means a risky redeploy of everything, independent deployment is a real win.
  • Do you have strong DevOps maturity? Microservices require automation, monitoring, and orchestration. Without it, you will drown.
  • Is fault isolation critical? If one feature crashing must not take down the rest, separate processes help.

If most answers are "no", a monolith (or modular monolith) is the better engineering choice — not the "lesser" one.

The Worst of Both Worlds: The "Distributed Monolith"

A trap to avoid

If you split into microservices but they are still tightly coupled — they must all be deployed together, share a database, or every request fans out across many of them — you get a distributed monolith: all the complexity and network pain of microservices, with none of the independence. This is a very common and painful failure. The fix is clean boundaries: each service truly independent, with its own data and the ability to deploy alone.

How Companies Migrate: The Strangler Fig Pattern

You rarely rewrite a monolith into microservices in one big bang — that is far too risky. Instead, teams use the Strangler Fig pattern (named after a vine that slowly grows around a tree until it replaces it):

  • 1. Keep the monolith running and serving traffic.
  • 2. Pick one capability (say, notifications) and build it as a new, separate microservice.
  • 3. Route just that capability's traffic to the new service (often via the API gateway), leaving everything else in the monolith.
  • 4. Repeat, peeling off one piece at a time, until the monolith is small or gone.

This lets you migrate safely and incrementally, with the ability to roll back any single piece. It is exactly how Amazon and others did it.

The Golden Rule

Start monolith, split later

Most successful companies (including Amazon and Netflix) started as monoliths and moved to microservices only when growth demanded it — gradually, using the strangler pattern. Do not start with microservices for a brand-new small app — the complexity will slow you down for no benefit. Add complexity only when you actually feel the pain.

Common interview trap

If asked "monolith or microservices?", never blurt "microservices because it is modern". The smart answer is: "It depends on scale and team size" — then explain the trade-offs. That shows real judgement.

Key Takeaways

  • Monolith: simple, fast to start, but hard to scale and risky as it grows.
  • Microservices: scalable and resilient, but complex to build and operate.
  • The right answer depends on scale and team size — there is no universal winner.
  • Golden rule: start with a monolith, split into microservices only when the pain is real.
Previous← Microservices Architecture NextVertical vs Horizontal Scaling →

#14Vertical vs Horizontal Scaling

One-line definition

Scaling means making your system handle more users/load. Vertical scaling = make one server more powerful (bigger machine). Horizontal scaling = add more servers that share the work.

When your app gets popular and one server can no longer cope, you have exactly two ways to grow. Understanding both — and why horizontal usually wins at large scale — is core to system design.

Real-life Analogy: A Busy Restaurant Kitchen

Bigger chef vs more chefs

Your kitchen cannot keep up with orders. You have two choices:

Vertical scaling: hire one super-chef with more hands, faster knives, a bigger stove. One person, more powerful. But there is a limit — no chef can have 50 hands.

Horizontal scaling: hire more normal chefs and split the orders among them. Need more capacity? Just add another chef. There is almost no limit.

Vertical Scaling (Scale Up)

You upgrade the single server — add more CPU, more RAM, faster disks. The app does not change at all; it just runs on a beefier machine.

  • Pro: very simple — no code changes, nothing to coordinate.
  • Con: there is a hard ceiling (a machine can only get so big), and a powerful machine is very expensive.
  • Con: it is still one server — if it dies, everything goes down (single point of failure).

Horizontal Scaling (Scale Out)

You add more servers and put a load balancer (next topic) in front to share the work between them.

  • Pro: almost unlimited growth — just keep adding servers.
  • Pro: if one server dies, the others keep serving (no single point of failure).
  • Con: more complex — you need load balancing and your app should be "stateless" (covered later).
Vertical:
Small Server
→
One BIG Server
 
Horizontal:
Server 1
Server 2
Server 3
+ more…
Vertical = one bigger machine. Horizontal = many machines working together.
PointVertical (Scale Up)Horizontal (Scale Out)
HowBigger single machineMore machines
LimitHard ceilingAlmost unlimited
Failure riskSingle point of failureOthers survive
ComplexitySimpleMore complex
Cost curveGets very expensiveUses cheap commodity servers

The Hidden Requirement of Horizontal Scaling: Statelessness

Here is the catch that trips people up. When you have many servers behind a load balancer, a single user's requests may land on different servers each time. Request 1 hits Server A, request 2 hits Server B. If Server A stored "this user is logged in" in its own memory, then Server B has no idea who the user is — and the user appears randomly logged out.

So horizontal scaling requires your servers to be stateless: they must not keep important per-user information in their own memory. Instead, that state is pushed to a shared store (a database or Redis) that all servers can read (this is Topics 47–48). Vertical scaling never has this problem — with one server, all state is naturally in one place. This is a big part of why horizontal scaling is "more complex".

When Each One Wins (with numbers)

Imagine one server can handle 1,000 requests/sec, and you suddenly need 10,000.

  • Vertical: buy a machine ~10× more powerful. Easy — until you need 1,000,000 req/sec, where no single machine on Earth can cope, and the top-end machines cost a fortune. The cost curve is steep and the ceiling is hard.
  • Horizontal: run 10 ordinary servers, each handling 1,000. Need a million? Run 1,000 servers. The growth is essentially unlimited, using cheap "commodity" machines, and you get fault tolerance for free.

A Key Nuance: Databases Often Scale Vertically First

Stateless app servers vs stateful databases

Stateless app servers are easy to scale horizontally. But databases hold state (the data!), so scaling them out is much harder — you need replication and sharding (Topics 26–27). For this reason, teams frequently scale the database vertically (a bigger DB machine) for as long as possible, while scaling the app tier horizontally. Recognising this split is a sign of real understanding.

Which do big companies use?

Horizontal scaling is how all large systems (Google, Netflix, Amazon) handle massive scale, because it has no real ceiling and survives failures. Vertical scaling is a quick early fix, but you eventually hit its limits. In practice, real systems use both: scale up to a sensible machine size, then scale out with many of them.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Horizontal is always better." Not for everything — for stateful systems like databases, scaling out is genuinely hard, and vertical may be the right first move.

"Scaling means just adding servers." Adding servers does nothing without a load balancer to distribute traffic and stateless design so any server can serve any request.

Interview tip

When asked to scale a system, say: "First I'd scale vertically as a quick win, but the long-term answer is horizontal scaling behind a load balancer, which means making the app tier stateless and pushing session/state to a shared store like Redis. The database I'd scale with replicas and, if needed, sharding." That single sentence ties five concepts together.

Key Takeaways

  • Vertical scaling = one more powerful server. Simple (no code change), but has a hard ceiling, gets expensive, and is a single point of failure.
  • Horizontal scaling = more servers sharing load. Nearly unlimited and fault-tolerant, but more complex.
  • Horizontal scaling requires stateless servers + a load balancer; state moves to a shared store.
  • Databases are stateful, so they often scale vertically first, then via replication/sharding.
  • Real systems use both: scale up to a point, then scale out.
Previous← Monolith vs Microservices NextLoad Balancer →

#15Load Balancer

One-line definition

A Load Balancer is a "traffic police" that sits in front of your servers and spreads incoming requests evenly across them, so no single server gets overloaded and the system stays fast and available.

The moment you have more than one server (horizontal scaling), you need something to decide which server each request should go to. That something is the load balancer. It is one of the most important building blocks in all of system design.

Real-life Analogy: Queues at a Supermarket

The staff member directing you to a free counter

At a busy supermarket, imagine a staff member standing at the front who watches all the billing counters and tells each customer "Counter 3 is free, go there!". This keeps all counters evenly busy — no huge line at one while another sits empty. A load balancer does exactly this for servers.

What a Load Balancer Does

  • Distributes requests across multiple servers so the load is shared.
  • Health checks: it constantly pings servers, and if one is dead, it stops sending traffic there — users never hit a broken server.
  • Enables scaling: you can add or remove servers behind it without users noticing.
  • Improves availability: if one server fails, traffic just goes to the healthy ones.
Users
→
Load Balancer
→
Server 1
spacer
→
spacer
→
Server 2 & 3
All requests hit the load balancer first, which forwards each to a healthy, free server.

What Actually Happens on a Single Request

Let us trace one request to make the role crystal clear. You type myapp.com and hit enter:

  • 1. DNS resolves myapp.com to the load balancer's public IP (not to any individual server — users never know the real servers exist).
  • 2. Your request arrives at the load balancer.
  • 3. The load balancer picks a healthy server using its algorithm (Topic 16) — say Server 2.
  • 4. It forwards your request to Server 2, gets the response, and sends it back to you.
  • 5. Your next request might go to Server 5 instead — which is exactly why the servers must be stateless (Topic 47).

Health Checks in Detail

The "magic" that keeps users away from broken servers is the health check. The load balancer repeatedly asks each server "are you OK?" (e.g. it calls a /health endpoint every few seconds, Topic 59):

  • If a server responds healthy → it stays in the rotation and receives traffic.
  • If a server fails several checks in a row → it is temporarily removed, so no user request is sent to it.
  • When it recovers and passes checks again → it is automatically added back.

This is automatic and invisible to users — a server can crash and most people never notice.

Hardware vs Software Load Balancers

Load balancers come in a few forms you should be able to name:

  • Software: Nginx and HAProxy are hugely popular, running on ordinary servers. Flexible and cost-effective.
  • Cloud / managed: AWS ELB/ALB, Google Cloud Load Balancer — the cloud runs and scales it for you.
  • Hardware: dedicated physical appliances (e.g. F5) — very fast, used in large enterprises, but expensive.

Layer 4 vs Layer 7 Load Balancers

There are two types you should know by name, based on how deeply they look at traffic (the "layer" refers to the OSI networking model):

  • Layer 4 (L4 — transport level): forwards traffic based only on IP address and port, without looking inside the request. It is like a postal sorter who routes by the address on the envelope without opening it. Extremely fast and efficient, but "dumb" — it cannot make content-based decisions.
  • Layer 7 (L7 — application level): can look inside the request (the URL path, headers, cookies) and make smarter decisions — e.g. send /images/* to image servers and /api/* to API servers, or route by user. It is like a receptionist who reads your letter and sends it to exactly the right department. Slightly slower, but far more capable. Most modern web load balancers (Nginx, ALB) are L7.

The Load Balancer Must Not Become the Single Point of Failure

Who balances the balancer?

You added a load balancer for reliability — but now if it dies, your whole app dies, because all traffic goes through it. So the load balancer itself must be made redundant. The common solution is active-passive: run two load balancers, with a standby that instantly takes over the shared "floating" IP if the active one fails (a failover, Topic 54). Cloud load balancers handle this redundancy for you automatically.

Why it also boosts reliability

The load balancer is not just about speed — it is a key part of high availability. Because it routes around dead servers, your app can lose a server and keep running with zero user impact. It is the component that turns "many servers" into "one reliable, scalable service".

Interview tip

Whenever you draw a system with multiple app servers, always put a load balancer in front of them — and mention health checks and that the LB itself is made redundant. Forgetting the load balancer (or making it a single point of failure) is a classic interview slip.

Key Takeaways

  • A load balancer is the "traffic police" that spreads requests across many servers.
  • It does health checks and routes traffic away from dead servers — boosting availability.
  • It is what makes horizontal scaling actually work.
  • L4 = fast, routes by IP/port. L7 = smarter, can read the request content.
  • The load balancer itself should be redundant so it is not a single point of failure.
Previous← Vertical vs Horizontal Scaling NextLoad Balancing Algorithms →

#16Load Balancing Algorithms

One-line definition

A Load Balancing Algorithm is the rule a load balancer uses to decide which server gets the next request. Different rules suit different situations.

We know the load balancer spreads requests across servers (Topic 15). But how exactly does it pick which server? That choice is made by an algorithm. Here are the main ones, in simple terms.

The Main Algorithms

AlgorithmHow it decidesGood for
Round RobinSend to each server in turn: 1, 2, 3, 1, 2, 3…Servers of equal power; simple and fair
Weighted Round RobinStronger servers get more requests (a bigger "weight")When some servers are more powerful
Least ConnectionsSend to the server currently handling the fewest active requestsWhen requests take different amounts of time
Least Response TimeSend to the server that is responding fastest right nowSqueezing out best performance
IP HashUse the user's IP to always send them to the same serverWhen a user should "stick" to one server

Understanding the Two You Will Use Most

Round Robin is the simplest: just go around the servers in a circle, one after another. It works great when all servers are equally powerful and all requests are similar. Its weakness: it does not care if one server is already busy with a slow request.

Least Connections is smarter: it looks at how busy each server currently is and sends the new request to the least busy one. This is better when some requests take much longer than others, because it avoids piling work onto an already-busy server.

Analogy: assigning customers to bank tellers

Round Robin = send each new customer to the next teller in order, regardless of how busy they are. Least Connections = look at all tellers and send the customer to whichever one has the shortest line right now. The second is obviously smarter when some customers take a long time.

Weighted & Least-Response-Time, Explained

Two more you should understand beyond the basics:

  • Weighted Round Robin: servers are not always equal. If Server A is twice as powerful as Server B, you give A a weight of 2 and B a weight of 1, so A receives roughly twice the requests. It is round robin that respects each server's capacity — useful when your fleet is a mix of machine sizes.
  • Least Response Time: goes a step beyond Least Connections by also considering how fast each server is currently responding. It sends the next request to the server that is both lightly loaded and answering quickly. This squeezes out the best real-world performance, at the cost of more monitoring overhead.

Why IP Hash Is Special (and Its Trap)

IP Hash always sends the same user to the same server (by hashing their IP address). This is useful when a server needs to "remember" a user between requests — a sticky session. For example, if Server 3 is holding your shopping cart in its own memory, IP Hash keeps sending you back to Server 3 so your cart is there.

The sticky-session problem

Sticky sessions are fragile: if "your" server dies, you lose your session (your cart vanishes). They also cause uneven load — if a few heavy users all stick to one server, it gets overloaded while others sit idle. The modern fix is to make servers stateless (Topic 47) and store sessions in a shared store like Redis (Topic 48). Then any server can serve any user, and you can use a simpler algorithm like round robin or least connections without stickiness at all.

How to Choose in Practice

  • Equal servers, similar requests → Round Robin (simple, fair).
  • Mixed machine sizes → Weighted Round Robin.
  • Requests vary a lot in duration → Least Connections (avoids overloading a busy server).
  • Performance-critical → Least Response Time.
  • Must keep a user on one server → IP Hash — but prefer fixing it with stateless design instead.
Interview tip

A great answer is: "I'd start with Round Robin or Least Connections, keep the servers stateless so I don't need sticky sessions, and use Weighted if the machines differ in size." Mentioning that you avoid sticky sessions by design shows maturity.

Key Takeaways

  • The algorithm is the rule for choosing which server gets the next request.
  • Round Robin = take turns; simple and fair for equal servers.
  • Least Connections = pick the least busy server; better for uneven request times.
  • Weighted = give stronger servers more traffic. IP Hash = stick each user to one server.
  • The best algorithm depends on your situation — another classic trade-off.
Previous← Load Balancer NextForward vs Reverse Proxy →

#17Forward Proxy vs Reverse Proxy

One-line definition

A proxy is a middleman that sits between a client and a server. A Forward Proxy sits in front of the client (it represents you). A Reverse Proxy sits in front of the server (it represents the website).

The word "proxy" just means "stand-in" or "middleman". The only thing to learn here is which side the middleman stands on. That single difference decides whether it is "forward" or "reverse".

Forward Proxy — Stands in Front of You (the Client)

A forward proxy is a middleman you send your requests through. The destination server sees the proxy, not you, so your identity is hidden behind it.

  • Example uses: a company proxy that filters which websites employees can visit; a VPN that hides your real location; bypassing regional blocks.
  • Who it serves: the client. It acts on your behalf.

Reverse Proxy — Stands in Front of the Server

A reverse proxy is a middleman that receives requests on behalf of the servers. The client thinks it is talking to the real server, but it is actually talking to the reverse proxy, which then forwards the request to the right server behind it.

  • Example uses: load balancing, caching, hiding/protecting the real servers, handling HTTPS encryption. A load balancer is a type of reverse proxy.
  • Who it serves: the server. It protects and represents the backend.
You
→
Forward Proxyrepresents you
→
Reverse Proxyrepresents server
→
Server
Forward proxy hides the client side; reverse proxy hides and protects the server side.
PointForward ProxyReverse Proxy
Sits in front ofThe client (you)The server
HidesThe client's identityThe servers behind it
Works forThe userThe website owner
Common usesVPN, content filteringLoad balancing, caching, security

What a Reverse Proxy Actually Does for You

The reverse proxy is the more important one in system design, because so many key components are reverse proxies (load balancers, API gateways, CDNs). Sitting in front of your servers, it can take over many jobs so your application code does not have to:

  • Load balancing — spread requests across servers (Topic 15).
  • SSL/TLS termination — handle the expensive HTTPS encryption/decryption at the proxy, so your backend servers do simpler, faster plain work behind it.
  • Caching — store common responses and serve them directly, sparing the servers.
  • Compression — shrink responses (gzip) before sending to clients.
  • Security & hiding — the real servers' addresses are never exposed; the proxy can block bad traffic and absorb attacks.
  • Routing — send /api to one set of servers and /images to another.

How They Relate to Things You Already Know

It all connects

A load balancer is a reverse proxy focused on distributing load. An API gateway (Topic 50) is a reverse proxy focused on routing + auth + rate limiting. A CDN (next topic) is a globally-distributed reverse proxy + cache. A VPN is essentially a forward proxy. So this one simple idea — a middleman, and which side it serves — underlies a huge amount of system design.

Easy way to remember

Forward proxy = for the client (hides you from the server). Reverse proxy = for the server (hides the servers from you). Just ask: "whose side is the middleman on?" If it protects the user, it is forward; if it protects the website, it is reverse.

Common misconception

"A proxy and a VPN are different things entirely." Not really — a VPN is a kind of forward proxy with encryption. And "a reverse proxy and a load balancer are different categories" — actually a load balancer is one type of reverse proxy. The labels describe roles, and one piece of software (like Nginx) can play several of them at once.

Key Takeaways

  • A proxy is a middleman between client and server; the only question is which side it represents.
  • Forward proxy sits in front of the client and hides the user (VPN, content filtering, bypassing blocks).
  • Reverse proxy sits in front of the server and does load balancing, SSL termination, caching, compression, and security.
  • Load balancers, API gateways, and CDNs are all reverse proxies. Remember: forward = for client, reverse = for server.
Previous← Load Balancing Algorithms NextCDN (Content Delivery Network) →

#18CDN (Content Delivery Network)

One-line definition

A CDN is a network of servers spread around the world that store copies of your content (images, videos, files) close to users, so it loads fast no matter where they are.

Remember from Topic 10 that distance causes latency — data from a faraway server is slow. A CDN solves this by keeping copies of your content in many locations worldwide, so users get it from a server near them instead of from one distant origin server.

Real-life Analogy: Local Warehouses

Amazon delivery from a nearby warehouse

When you order on Amazon, it does not ship from one giant warehouse on the other side of the country. It ships from a local warehouse near your city, so it arrives in a day. A CDN does the same with data: instead of every user fetching a video from one distant server, copies are kept in local "warehouses" (called edge servers) around the world, so it arrives fast.

How It Works

Your main server is called the origin. The CDN copies your static content (images, CSS, videos) onto many edge servers placed in cities worldwide. When a user requests an image, the CDN serves it from the nearest edge server. The user gets it quickly, and your origin server is spared the work.

User in India
→
Edge Server (Mumbai)nearby copy
↓
Origin Server (USA)only contacted if edge has no copy
The user is served from a nearby edge server, not the distant origin.

Edge Cache Hit vs Miss (how content gets there)

Edge servers do not magically have your content — they fill up on demand, just like any cache (Topic 31):

  • First request in a region (a "miss"): the edge server in, say, Mumbai does not have the file yet, so it fetches it once from the origin and stores a copy.
  • Every later request (a "hit"): the Mumbai edge already has the file and serves it instantly — the origin is never touched again.

So the very first user "warms" the cache, and millions after them get a fast, local copy.

Push vs Pull CDNs

There are two ways content ends up on the edge servers:

  • Pull CDN (most common): the CDN automatically pulls content from your origin the first time it is requested, then caches it. You do almost nothing — just point your site at the CDN. Best for frequently-changing or large catalogs.
  • Push CDN: you actively upload (push) content to the CDN ahead of time. You control exactly what is stored and when. Best for large files that you know will be popular (like a big software release or a launch video).

Controlling Freshness: TTL and Invalidation

Since edges hold copies, those copies can go stale. Two controls manage this (covered more in Topic 35):

  • TTL (Time To Live): each cached file has an expiry. After it expires, the edge fetches a fresh copy from the origin.
  • Invalidation / purge: if you change a file before its TTL (e.g. fix an image), you tell the CDN to clear it so users get the new version. A common trick is cache busting — renaming the file (logo.v2.png) so it is treated as brand-new.

Beyond Speed: CDNs as a Shield

  • Massive traffic absorption: CDNs are enormous global networks that soak up traffic spikes (a viral video) before they ever reach your origin.
  • DDoS protection: because attack traffic hits the huge CDN first, it can be absorbed and filtered (Topic 74).
  • Security features: many CDNs include a Web Application Firewall (WAF) and bot protection.
  • Modern CDNs even accelerate dynamic content by keeping fast, optimized connections between edges and the origin — so it is not only for static files anymore.

This is exactly how Netflix and YouTube stream video smoothly worldwide — the video chunks are cached on edge servers near you (Topic 94). It is also why your favourite websites load their images and scripts so quickly. Popular CDNs include Cloudflare, Akamai, AWS CloudFront, and Google Cloud CDN.

What CDNs are best for

CDNs are ideal for static content that does not change per user — images, videos, CSS, JavaScript files. They are traditionally not used for highly personalised, always-changing data (like your live bank balance), which must come fresh from the origin — though modern CDNs and edge computing are increasingly handling some dynamic content too.

Interview tip

In almost any design that serves images, video, or static assets globally, mention a CDN early. State that it cuts latency (content is near the user), offloads the origin, absorbs spikes, and adds DDoS protection. It is one of the highest-impact, lowest-effort additions you can propose.

Key Takeaways

  • A CDN keeps copies of your content close to users on edge servers worldwide.
  • It cuts latency (content travels a shorter distance) and reduces load on your origin server.
  • Best for static content: images, videos, CSS, JS.
  • It is how Netflix, YouTube, and most big sites deliver content fast and handle huge traffic.
  • Bonus: CDNs add reliability and security (absorbing spikes and attacks).
Previous← Forward vs Reverse Proxy NextSQL vs NoSQL Databases →

#19SQL vs NoSQL Databases

One-line definition

A database is where an app stores its data permanently. SQL databases store data in neat tables (like spreadsheets) with a strict structure. NoSQL databases store data in flexible formats (documents, key-values, etc.) without a fixed structure.

Every app needs to store data — users, orders, messages. The big first decision is which type of database to use. The two families are SQL and NoSQL, and each fits different needs.

SQL (Relational) Databases

SQL databases store data in tables made of rows and columns, exactly like an Excel sheet. Before storing anything, you must define a schema — the exact columns and their types. Tables can be linked ("related") to each other, which is why they are called relational. Examples: MySQL, PostgreSQL, Oracle.

  • Strict structure — every row must follow the defined columns.
  • Powerful queries using the SQL language (joins across tables).
  • Strong consistency & ACID (next topic) — great for data that must be exactly correct, like money.

NoSQL (Non-Relational) Databases

NoSQL databases skip the rigid table structure. Data can be stored as flexible documents (like JSON), key-value pairs, and more. There is usually no fixed schema, so different records can have different fields. Examples: MongoDB, Cassandra, Redis, DynamoDB.

  • Flexible structure — add new fields anytime, no migration needed.
  • Scales horizontally very easily — built to spread across many servers.
  • Great for huge volumes and fast-changing data, but often trades some strict consistency for speed.
Analogy: A strict form vs a blank notebook

SQL is like a printed government form: every box is fixed, you must fill exactly the right fields in the right format. Very organised and reliable. NoSQL is like a blank notebook: you can write anything, in any shape, on any page. Very flexible and fast, but less strictly organised.

PointSQLNoSQL
StructureFixed tables (schema)Flexible (no fixed schema)
ScalingUsually verticalEasily horizontal
ConsistencyStrong (ACID)Often eventual (BASE)
Best forStructured data, transactions (banking)Huge scale, flexible data (feeds, logs)
ExamplesMySQL, PostgreSQLMongoDB, Cassandra, Redis

The Same Data, Two Different Worlds

Let us make the difference concrete. Suppose we store a user and their orders.

In SQL, you would normally split this into two tables — a users table and an orders table — and link them by a user_id. To get "Priya and all her orders", you run a JOIN that stitches the two tables together. This avoids duplicating Priya's details and keeps everything consistent (this is normalization, Topic 24).

In a document NoSQL database (like MongoDB), you would often store the whole thing as one document — Priya's profile with her orders nested right inside it. One read gives you everything, no join needed. The trade-off: if Priya's address is duplicated across documents, updating it is harder.

This single difference — relate-and-join vs nest-it-together — explains most of the practical contrast between the two families.

Why NoSQL Scales Horizontally So Easily

This is the deeper "why" behind the scaling row in the table. In SQL, the power comes from relationships and joins across tables. But joins are very hard to perform when the data is split across many machines (sharded) — the database would have to gather rows from many servers to answer one query. So traditional SQL prefers to live on one strong machine (vertical scaling).

NoSQL databases deliberately give up complex joins and relationships. Each record is self-contained, so it can live on any machine independently. That makes spreading data across hundreds of servers (sharding, Topic 27) natural — which is exactly why NoSQL handles internet-scale data so well. The flexibility is not free; you trade away joins and often strict consistency to get it.

Schema-on-Write vs Schema-on-Read

  • SQL is "schema-on-write": you define the structure first, and the database rejects any data that does not fit. This guarantees clean, predictable data — great when correctness matters.
  • NoSQL is often "schema-on-read": you can store almost anything, and your application makes sense of it when reading. This gives flexibility and fast iteration — great when your data shape is still changing — but the discipline now lives in your code, not the database.
Real-world: who uses what

Banks & payment systems lean SQL (PostgreSQL/Oracle) because money demands ACID correctness. Instagram's feed, Netflix's viewing history, and logging pipelines lean NoSQL (Cassandra/DynamoDB) for massive scale and flexibility. Most large companies practice polyglot persistence — using SQL for orders/payments and NoSQL for feeds/sessions/search within the same product.

How to choose

Need strict correctness, relationships, and complex queries (money, orders, reporting)? Lean SQL. Need massive scale, flexible/changing data, and simple access patterns (social feeds, logs, real-time, sessions)? Lean NoSQL. Many big systems use both, each for what it is best at.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"NoSQL is newer and therefore better." False — it is a different tool with different trade-offs, not an upgrade. SQL databases are battle-tested and often the right default.

"SQL can't scale." Misleading — SQL scales very well vertically and with read replicas; modern "NewSQL" databases (like CockroachDB, Spanner) even scale horizontally with SQL and ACID.

"NoSQL means no consistency." False — many NoSQL stores can be configured for strong consistency (e.g. DynamoDB strong reads); they simply default to favouring availability and speed.

Interview tip

Never start with "I'll use NoSQL because it scales". Instead reason from the data: "This data is highly relational and needs transactions, so SQL. This part is huge-volume, simple-access, and write-heavy, so a NoSQL store like Cassandra." Justifying from access patterns and consistency needs is what interviewers want.

Key Takeaways

  • SQL = structured tables, strict schema (schema-on-write), strong consistency, powerful joins (MySQL, PostgreSQL).
  • NoSQL = flexible formats, no fixed schema, self-contained records that shard easily (MongoDB, Cassandra, Redis).
  • NoSQL scales horizontally because it gives up joins and relationships — a deliberate trade-off.
  • SQL suits transactions and correctness; NoSQL suits scale and flexibility. Choose from access patterns.
  • Big systems use both (polyglot persistence); "NewSQL" even offers SQL + horizontal scale.
Previous← CDN NextTypes of NoSQL Databases →

#20Types of NoSQL Databases

One-line definition

"NoSQL" is not one thing — it is a family with four main types: Key-Value, Document, Column-family, and Graph. Each stores data in a different shape and is good for different jobs.

Saying "NoSQL" is like saying "vehicle" — there are many kinds for different purposes. Let us look at the four main types, simply.

1. Key-Value Stores

The simplest type. Data is stored as a key and its value, exactly like a dictionary: you look up a key, you get its value. Super fast. Examples: Redis, DynamoDB.

Best for: caching, user sessions, shopping carts — anything you fetch by a single key.

2. Document Databases

Store data as documents (usually JSON-like), where each document can hold nested, flexible fields. Examples: MongoDB, CouchDB.

Best for: flexible data like user profiles, product catalogs, content — where each item may have different fields.

3. Column-Family Databases

Store data in columns grouped together rather than rows, which makes them very fast for reading huge amounts of specific columns. Examples: Cassandra, HBase.

Best for: massive write-heavy workloads and analytics — like time-series data, logs, and big-data systems.

4. Graph Databases

Store data as nodes and the connections (relationships) between them. Built to answer "how are these things connected?" extremely fast. Examples: Neo4j, Amazon Neptune.

Best for: social networks (friends-of-friends), recommendation engines, fraud detection — anything about relationships.

TypeStores data asGreat forExamples
Key-Valuekey → value pairsCaching, sessionsRedis, DynamoDB
DocumentJSON-like documentsProfiles, catalogsMongoDB
Column-familyGrouped columnsBig data, logsCassandra
GraphNodes & relationshipsSocial networksNeo4j

Seeing Each Type in Action

To make the four types stick, here is a concrete picture of each, plus when not to use it:

  • Key-Value: imagine a giant dictionary — session:abc123 → {userId: 42}. You can only look things up by the key; you cannot ask "find all sessions for user 42" efficiently. Avoid when you need to query by the contents of the value.
  • Document: a whole user profile (name, address, list of orders) stored as one JSON document you fetch in a single read. You can query inside documents. Avoid when data is highly relational with many cross-references — you'd duplicate a lot.
  • Column-family: think of a massive table optimised for writing and reading specific columns across billions of rows — perfect for "all sensor readings for device X over time". Avoid when you need flexible ad-hoc queries or joins.
  • Graph: data is people (nodes) connected by "follows"/"friends" (edges). Asking "friends of friends who like cricket" is one fast graph traversal — in SQL it would be a nightmare of joins. Avoid when your data is not really about relationships.

The Deeper Point: NoSQL Means "Designed Around Your Queries"

Query-first, not data-first

In SQL you model the data cleanly first, then query it however you like later. In NoSQL it is the opposite: you decide your access patterns first, then choose the type and shape your data so those exact queries are blazing fast. That is why "which NoSQL type?" always comes back to "how will you read and write this data?"

Common misconceptions, cleared

"NoSQL is one kind of database." No — these four families are as different from each other as a bicycle is from a boat. "NoSQL" just means "not the traditional relational table model".

"Redis is only a cache." Redis is a key-value store often used as a cache, but it also does queues, leaderboards (sorted sets), pub/sub, and can persist to disk.

"A graph DB is just for social networks." It shines anywhere relationships matter — fraud detection, recommendations, knowledge graphs, network/IT maps.

Interview tip

If you propose NoSQL, name the specific type and justify it by the access pattern: "Sessions are simple lookups by key, so a key-value store like Redis; the social graph needs friends-of-friends traversal, so a graph database." Saying just "NoSQL" is vague; naming the right family shows depth.

Key Takeaways

  • Key-Value (Redis): simplest, fastest, fetch by key — caching, sessions. Can't query by value.
  • Document (MongoDB): flexible JSON-like records — profiles, catalogs. Can query inside docs.
  • Column-family (Cassandra): huge write-heavy and analytics workloads across billions of rows.
  • Graph (Neo4j): relationships and connections — social networks, fraud, recommendations.
  • NoSQL is query-first: choose the type and data shape from how you will read and write.
Previous← SQL vs NoSQL NextACID Properties →

#21ACID Properties

One-line definition

ACID is a set of four guarantees (Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation, Durability) that make database transactions safe and reliable — especially important for things like money, where mistakes are unacceptable.

A "transaction" is a group of database operations that should happen together as one unit. ACID is the promise that this unit behaves correctly even if things go wrong. SQL databases are famous for strong ACID guarantees.

The Classic Example: Bank Transfer

You transfer ₹500 from your account to a friend. This involves two steps: (1) subtract ₹500 from you, (2) add ₹500 to your friend. ACID makes sure this is safe.

The Four Letters

  • A — Atomicity: "all or nothing". Either both steps happen, or neither does. If the system crashes after step 1, the ₹500 is not lost — the whole transaction is undone.
  • C — Consistency: the database always moves from one valid state to another. Rules are never broken (e.g. total money in the system stays correct; no negative balances if not allowed).
  • I — Isolation: if many transactions happen at once, they do not mess each other up. Each behaves as if it ran alone. Two people transferring at the same time will not corrupt each other's data.
  • D — Durability: once a transaction is confirmed, it is saved permanently — even if the power goes out one second later, the data survives.
Atomicity in one picture

Imagine the bank deducts ₹500 from you, then crashes before adding it to your friend. Without atomicity, your ₹500 vanishes! With atomicity, the system rolls back the deduction — as if nothing happened. You try again safely.

Going Deeper on Isolation (the trickiest letter)

Atomicity and durability are fairly intuitive, but isolation is where most real bugs hide, because many transactions run at the same time. If isolation is weak, concurrent transactions can interfere and produce classic anomalies:

  • Dirty read: you read data another transaction wrote but has not committed yet — and then it rolls back, so you read something that "never happened".
  • Non-repeatable read: you read a row twice in one transaction and get different values, because someone changed it in between.
  • Phantom read: you run the same query twice and get extra rows the second time, because someone inserted new matching rows.

To control this trade-off between safety and speed, databases offer isolation levels:

Isolation levelPreventsTrade-off
Read UncommittedAlmost nothingFastest, least safe
Read CommittedDirty readsCommon default
Repeatable Read+ non-repeatable readsStronger
SerializableEverything (acts as if one-at-a-time)Safest, slowest

Higher isolation = more correctness but more locking and lower throughput. This is itself a tunable trade-off, much like consistency choices elsewhere.

How Durability Actually Works

How can a database promise your data survives a crash one millisecond after "success"? The trick is a write-ahead log (WAL): before applying a change, the database first writes it to an append-only log on durable disk. Even if the server crashes immediately after, on restart it replays the log to recover every confirmed transaction. So "confirmed" really means "safely written to the log on disk".

Common misconceptions, cleared

"The C in ACID is the same as the C in CAP." No! ACID's Consistency means "the database's rules/constraints are never violated". CAP's Consistency means "all nodes see the same latest data". They are different concepts that unfortunately share a letter.

"NoSQL has no ACID." Increasingly false — MongoDB now supports multi-document ACID transactions, and many modern databases offer it. ACID vs BASE is a spectrum, not a wall.

Interview tip

When money or inventory is involved, explicitly say "this needs ACID transactions" and mention you'd be careful about the isolation level (e.g. serializable for a critical balance check). Naming isolation levels signals genuine database depth.

Key Takeaways

  • ACID = four guarantees that make transactions safe: Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation, Durability.
  • Atomicity: all-or-nothing. Consistency: the DB's rules are never broken.
  • Isolation: concurrent transactions don't corrupt each other — tuned via isolation levels (Read Committed → Serializable).
  • Durability: confirmed data survives crashes, thanks to a write-ahead log.
  • Essential for money, orders, and critical data. (Note: ACID's "C" ≠ CAP's "C".)
Previous← Types of NoSQL Databases NextBASE Properties →

#22BASE Properties

One-line definition

BASE is the relaxed alternative to ACID, used by many NoSQL databases. It trades strict, instant correctness for high availability and scale. The data becomes correct eventually, not necessarily the same instant.

ACID is strict and safe, but being that strict is hard to do across thousands of servers worldwide. So many large NoSQL systems use a more relaxed model called BASE, which favours staying available and fast over being perfectly consistent every millisecond.

What BASE Stands For

  • BA — Basically Available: the system stays up and responds, even if some parts are having trouble.
  • S — Soft state: the data might be "in flux" for a short time — different copies may temporarily disagree.
  • E — Eventually consistent: given a little time, all copies of the data will sync up and become the same.

Real Example: A Like Count

Instagram likes

When a post gets a like, you might see "1,000 likes" while your friend briefly sees "999". For a moment the copies disagree (soft state), but within a second or two they sync to the same number (eventually consistent). And the app never goes down to fix it (basically available). For a like count, this tiny delay is totally fine — and it lets the system serve billions of users.

ACID vs BASE

PointACIDBASE
PriorityCorrectness & safetyAvailability & scale
ConsistencyImmediate & strictEventual
Typical DBSQLNoSQL
Best forMoney, ordersFeeds, likes, logs, huge scale
The trade-off behind it

You usually cannot have perfect instant consistency AND huge scale AND constant availability all at once (the CAP theorem, Topic 29, explains why). ACID picks correctness; BASE picks availability and scale. Choose based on whether a brief disagreement in data is acceptable for your use case.

How "Eventually Consistent" Actually Catches Up

"Eventually" sounds vague, so here is what really happens under the hood. When you write data, it is accepted by one node and the system says "done" immediately (basically available). That node then copies the change to the other replicas in the background. During that gap, replicas disagree (soft state). They reconcile through mechanisms like:

  • Read repair: when a read notices two replicas disagree, it fixes the stale one on the spot.
  • Anti-entropy / gossip: nodes periodically compare data and patch differences in the background.
  • Hinted handoff: if a node was down during a write, another node holds a "hint" and delivers it once that node returns.

"Eventually" in healthy systems usually means milliseconds to a couple of seconds — not minutes.

The Question to Ask: "Is a Brief Disagreement Dangerous?"

This is the heart of choosing ACID vs BASE. Ask: if two users saw slightly different values for a second, would anything bad happen?

  • Like counts, view counts, follower numbers, feeds: a brief mismatch is harmless → BASE is perfect.
  • Bank balance, seat booking, inventory, payments: a brief mismatch could mean double-spending or overselling → you need ACID.
Common misconceptions, cleared

"Eventually consistent means unreliable / data can be lost." No — the data is safe and will become consistent; it is just not instantaneous across all copies. It is a timing property, not a "might lose your data" property.

"BASE is always faster and better for scale, so always use it." Only when your use case tolerates brief inconsistency. Using BASE for money is a classic, costly mistake.

Interview tip

Show you can mix both in one system: "I'll use ACID (SQL) for payments and order state, and BASE (Cassandra/DynamoDB) for the activity feed and like counts." Demonstrating that consistency is a per-feature decision is exactly the senior-level instinct interviewers reward.

Key Takeaways

  • BASE = a relaxed model favouring availability and scale over strict instant correctness.
  • Basically Available, Soft state, Eventually consistent.
  • Replicas sync via read repair, anti-entropy, and hinted handoff — usually within milliseconds.
  • Decide with one question: is a brief disagreement dangerous? If yes → ACID; if no → BASE.
  • ACID (SQL) = strict correctness; BASE (NoSQL) = scale and availability. Mix per feature.
Previous← ACID Properties NextDatabase Indexing →

#23Database Indexing

One-line definition

A database index is a special lookup structure that makes searching for data dramatically faster — just like the index at the back of a book helps you find a topic without reading every page.

As tables grow to millions of rows, finding a specific row by scanning every single one becomes painfully slow. An index solves this by keeping a sorted "shortcut" to the data.
