Pair it with circuit breakers (open → serve fallback) and load shedding under overload. This shows you optimise for user/business value during failures, not just uptime.

Key Takeaways

  • Graceful degradation = drop non-essential features to keep the core running under failure/load.
  • Like a car's limp mode — reduced, but still functional.
  • Load shedding = deliberately reject excess/low-priority work so the important traffic survives.
  • Must be designed (fallbacks, toggles); it doesn't happen by default. Pairs with circuit breakers.
  • Decide your essential "core path" in advance and protect it — serve 80% well over crashing for all.
Previous← Health Checks & Heartbeats NextStrong vs Eventual Consistency →

#61Strong vs Eventual Consistency

One-line definition

When data is copied across many servers, consistency is about whether everyone sees the same value. Strong consistency = everyone always sees the latest value immediately. Eventual consistency = copies may briefly differ but will all agree soon.

This builds directly on ACID/BASE and CAP. When you replicate data, you face a choice: guarantee everyone sees the newest value right away (slower), or let copies catch up over time (faster, more available).

Strong Consistency

The moment a write succeeds, every read anywhere returns that new value. The system waits until all (or enough) copies are updated before confirming. Correct and predictable, but slower and less available (it may block during network issues).

  • Use for: bank balances, inventory, anything where a stale value is dangerous.

Eventual Consistency

A write updates one copy and confirms quickly; other copies catch up shortly after. For a brief window, different users might see different values, but eventually all copies converge to the same value.

  • Use for: like counts, social feeds, view counts — where a slightly old value is harmless.
Analogy: telling friends news

Strong: you gather all your friends in one room and announce the news at once — everyone knows simultaneously, but it took effort to assemble them. Eventual: you tell one friend, who tells another, and so on — fast and easy, but for a while some know and some do not. Soon, everyone knows.

PointStrongEventual
ReadsAlways latestMay be briefly stale
SpeedSlowerFaster
AvailabilityLowerHigher
Best forMoney, inventoryLikes, feeds, counts

There are also middle grounds like read-your-own-writes (you always see your own changes immediately, even if others see them slightly later).

The Consistency Spectrum (it's not just two options)

"Strong" and "eventual" are the two ends, but real systems offer a whole spectrum of useful in-between guarantees. Knowing the named ones makes you sound precise:

  • Read-your-own-writes: you always see your own updates immediately (others may lag). Fixes the "I edited my profile but it shows the old one" bug from Topic 26.
  • Monotonic reads: once you've seen a value, you'll never see an older one later (no "time travel" backwards).
  • Causal consistency: things that are cause-and-effect appear in the right order everywhere (you never see a reply before the message it replies to), while unrelated events can be in any order.

These give a much better user experience than raw eventual consistency, without the full cost of strong consistency — which is why apps like social networks lean on them.

Why Strong Consistency Costs Latency (and availability)

The reason strong consistency is "slower" is concrete: to guarantee every reader sees the latest write, the system must coordinate — the write has to reach a majority of replicas (a quorum, Topic 68) before it's confirmed, and that coordination takes round-trips, especially across regions. Worse, during a network partition (CAP, Topic 29), a strongly-consistent system may have to reject requests rather than risk serving stale data — trading availability for correctness. Eventual consistency skips the wait: confirm immediately, reconcile later.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Eventual consistency = your data is wrong / will be lost." No — it's a timing property; data is durable and converges, usually within milliseconds.

"Strong consistency means the system is always correct." It means reads are always current — but it can become unavailable during partitions, and it's slower. Correctness of reads, not a free lunch.

"It's a global setting." Often it's chosen per operation (e.g. DynamoDB lets you request a strong or eventual read on each call).

Interview tip

Pick consistency per feature and name the level: "strong for the wallet balance; read-your-own-writes for profile edits; eventual for the like counter." Mentioning intermediate levels (read-your-own-writes, causal) instead of only the two extremes signals genuine distributed-systems depth.

Key Takeaways

  • Strong consistency: everyone sees the latest value immediately — correct but slower and less available.
  • Eventual consistency: copies briefly differ, then converge — faster and more available.
  • There's a spectrum: read-your-own-writes, monotonic reads, causal consistency sit usefully in between.
  • Strong consistency costs latency because it must coordinate a quorum; often chosen per operation.
  • Choose based on whether a brief stale value is acceptable — the practical face of CAP/PACELC.
Previous← Graceful Degradation NextDistributed Transactions (2PC) →

#62Distributed Transactions (Two-Phase Commit)

One-line definition

A distributed transaction spans multiple services/databases that must all succeed or all fail together. Two-Phase Commit (2PC) is a protocol to coordinate this: first everyone agrees they can do it, then everyone actually does it.

ACID transactions are easy within one database. But what if a single operation must update several separate databases (e.g. deduct stock in one, charge payment in another)? You need them all to commit together — that is a distributed transaction.

Real-life Analogy: Friends Agreeing on a Plan

"Can everyone make it?" then "OK, it's on!"

To plan an outing, a coordinator first asks everyone "Can you come Saturday?" (phase 1). Only if everyone says yes does the coordinator announce "OK, confirmed!" (phase 2). If even one says no, the plan is cancelled for all. That is exactly 2PC.

The Two Phases

  • Phase 1 — Prepare (voting): a coordinator asks every participant "are you ready to commit?" Each one does the work tentatively and replies Yes (ready) or No.
  • Phase 2 — Commit/Abort: if all said Yes, the coordinator tells everyone "Commit!" and they finalize. If any said No, it tells everyone "Abort!" and they roll back.
Coordinator
"ready?" →
All vote Yes
"commit!" →
All commit
Everyone must agree in phase 1 before anyone commits in phase 2.

The Big Problem with 2PC

It blocks, and the coordinator is a SPOF

2PC guarantees correctness but is slow and blocking: participants must lock resources and wait. Worse, if the coordinator crashes at the wrong moment, participants can be stuck waiting indefinitely. Because of these issues, large modern systems often avoid 2PC and use the Saga pattern (next topic) instead.

The Exact Moment 2PC Gets Stuck

Let's pinpoint why 2PC is feared. After phase 1, every participant has voted "Yes" and is now holding locks, waiting to be told commit or abort. If the coordinator crashes right then, the participants are stuck in limbo: they can't commit (maybe someone else voted No) and can't abort (maybe everyone voted Yes) — so they keep their locks and block, sometimes forever, until the coordinator returns. Meanwhile those locked rows can't be used by anyone else. This "blocking" property is 2PC's fundamental flaw, and it's why holding locks across network boundaries is so dangerous compared to a single-database transaction.

So When Is 2PC Still Used?

It hasn't vanished — 2PC is fine when you need true atomic correctness across a small number of tightly-coupled, reliable participants in one data center (e.g. a couple of databases, or a DB + a message broker via XA transactions). It's avoided for high-scale, geographically-spread microservices, where its locking and blocking would cripple throughput. Rule of thumb: 2PC for a few local resources that must be perfectly atomic; sagas for many distributed services.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"2PC guarantees you can never get stuck." The opposite — its known weakness is exactly that participants can block indefinitely if the coordinator fails after voting.

"2PC scales fine." No — locks held across the network plus the coordinator round-trips make it slow and poorly-scaling; it's a small-scale, strong-correctness tool.

Interview tip

If asked to keep multiple services consistent, say: "2PC gives atomicity but blocks and doesn't scale, so for microservices I'd use a Saga with compensating actions and accept eventual consistency." Knowing why 2PC is avoided (blocking + coordinator SPOF) is the key insight interviewers want.

Key Takeaways

  • A distributed transaction must succeed or fail across multiple databases together.
  • 2PC = Phase 1 "everyone prepare & vote", Phase 2 "all commit or all abort".
  • It guarantees correctness but is slow, blocking (locks held across the network), and the coordinator is a SPOF.
  • Still fine for a few reliable local resources; avoided for high-scale distributed microservices.
  • Modern large systems often prefer the Saga pattern instead.
Previous← Strong vs Eventual Consistency NextSaga Pattern →

#63Saga Pattern

One-line definition

The Saga Pattern handles transactions across microservices by breaking them into a series of small local steps, each with a matching "undo" (compensating) action. If a later step fails, the saga runs the undos to reverse the earlier steps.

2PC locks everything and is fragile. The saga takes a different approach: do each step, and if something fails midway, undo the completed steps one by one. No global locks — great for microservices.

Real-life Analogy: Booking a Trip

Book flight, hotel, car — undo if one fails

You book a flight ✓, then a hotel ✓, then try a rental car ✗ (sold out). You cannot leave the trip half-booked. So you undo: cancel the hotel, cancel the flight. Each booking has a matching cancellation. The saga pattern automates exactly this "do, and undo if needed".

How It Works

Each service does its own local transaction and then triggers the next step. If any step fails, compensating transactions run in reverse to undo all the previous successful steps, returning the system to a consistent state.

Charge ✓
→
Reserve stock ✓
→
Ship ✗ fails
↓ compensate (undo)
Release stock
→
Refund charge
On failure, run compensating actions to undo completed steps.

Two Ways to Coordinate a Saga

  • Choreography: each service listens for events and reacts, triggering the next step. No central boss — simple for few steps, but can get tangled.
  • Orchestration: a central "orchestrator" tells each service what to do and when. Easier to understand and manage for complex flows.
Saga vs 2PC

2PC: strong consistency, locks resources, blocking. Saga: eventual consistency, no locks, resilient — better for microservices. The trade-off: with sagas you accept brief inconsistency and must carefully design the undo steps.

Choreography vs Orchestration: When to Use Which

  • Choreography (event-driven): no central boss — each service emits events and others react. Great for simple, few-step flows; fully decoupled. Downside: with many steps the logic is scattered everywhere and becomes hard to follow ("where does this flow even go next?").
  • Orchestration (central controller): one orchestrator explicitly drives the steps and the undos. Easier to understand, monitor, and debug complex flows; the trade-off is the orchestrator is a component you must build and keep available. Most non-trivial sagas use orchestration.

The Hard Parts of Designing Sagas

Sagas trade locking complexity for design complexity. Things you must handle:

  • Compensations must exist and be reliable: every step needs a sensible undo — and some things can't truly be undone (you can't un-send an email; you send an apology/cancellation instead).
  • Idempotency: steps and compensations may be retried, so they must be safe to run twice (Topic 57).
  • Intermediate visibility: because there are no locks, others can see the half-finished state (e.g. an order briefly "pending"). You design the UX around this eventual consistency.
Common misconceptions, cleared

"A saga rolls back like a database transaction." No — there's no automatic rollback. You write explicit compensating actions that semantically undo each step.

"Sagas give the same guarantees as ACID." No — you get eventual consistency with a temporarily-visible in-between state, not atomic isolation.

Interview tip

For multi-service workflows (order → payment → inventory → shipping), propose a saga, pick orchestration for clarity, and explicitly list compensating actions for each step ("if shipping fails, release stock and refund payment"). Mentioning idempotent steps and the visible intermediate state shows you've thought it through.

Key Takeaways

  • The saga pattern = a sequence of local steps, each with a compensating (undo) action.
  • If a step fails, earlier steps are undone to restore consistency — no global locks.
  • Choreography (events) suits simple flows; orchestration (central controller) suits complex ones.
  • You must design reliable, idempotent compensations and accept a visible intermediate state.
  • Preferred over 2PC for microservices; trade-off is eventual consistency.
Previous← Distributed Transactions (2PC) NextConsensus Algorithms →

#64Consensus Algorithms (Paxos, Raft)

One-line definition

A consensus algorithm lets a group of distributed servers agree on a single value or decision, even when some servers fail or messages are delayed. Paxos and Raft are the two famous algorithms that solve this.

In a distributed system, many servers must agree on things — like "who is the leader?" or "what is the correct order of these writes?". Getting separate machines to reliably agree, despite failures, is surprisingly hard. Consensus algorithms make it possible.

Real-life Analogy: A Group Deciding Where to Eat

Reaching one decision everyone honors

A group of friends must pick one restaurant. Some are not answering their phones, messages arrive late, opinions differ. Yet they need a single, final decision everyone will follow. Consensus algorithms are a rigorous way to reach that one agreed decision despite the chaos.

The Core Idea: Majority Agreement

Consensus works by majority vote (quorum). A decision is final once more than half the servers agree. Because any two majorities overlap, you can never have two conflicting decisions accepted — this is the key trick that keeps everyone consistent.

Raft — The Understandable One

Paxos works but is famously hard to understand. Raft was designed to do the same job in a clearer way, and is widely used today. Raft breaks consensus into:

  • Leader election: the servers elect one leader (next topic).
  • Log replication: the leader receives changes and copies them to followers.
  • Safety: a change is committed once a majority of followers have stored it.
Where consensus is used

Systems like etcd, ZooKeeper, Consul, and many distributed databases use consensus (often Raft) to stay consistent — for leader election, configuration, and agreeing on the order of operations. Kubernetes stores its cluster state in etcd, which uses Raft.

Why a Majority? The Overlap Trick

The genius of quorum-based consensus is simple once you see it: any two majorities of the same group must share at least one member. With 5 servers, any 3 (a majority) and any other 3 always overlap in at least one server. That shared member can't agree to two conflicting decisions, so two contradictory values can never both be accepted. This also explains why you typically run an odd number of nodes (3, 5, 7): 5 nodes tolerate 2 failures (3 still form a majority), while 6 nodes also only tolerate 2 — the extra node buys nothing but costs more. And if too many nodes are down to form a majority, the system stops accepting writes rather than risk inconsistency (choosing consistency over availability, CAP-style).

The Cost: Consensus Is Expensive, So Use It Sparingly

Every consensus decision needs multiple network round-trips to a majority, so it's much slower than a normal write. That's why systems don't run all their data through Raft. Instead they use consensus for the small but critical things — "who is the leader?", cluster configuration, lock ownership, metadata — and let the elected leader handle the high-volume data path more cheaply. Consensus is the trustworthy-but-slow committee; you don't ask it about every tiny thing.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Consensus needs all servers to agree." No — only a majority. That's the whole point: it keeps working even when a minority is down or unreachable.

"Raft and Paxos do different jobs." They solve the same problem (agreement under failures); Raft is just designed to be far easier to understand and implement correctly.

"More nodes = more fault tolerance always." Going from 5 to 6 nodes does not increase failures tolerated (still 2). Use odd numbers.

Interview tip

You rarely implement Raft yourself — the win is knowing where it lives: "I'll store leader/config/lock state in a consensus system like etcd/ZooKeeper (Raft), with an odd number of nodes for majority quorum." Mentioning the odd-node rule and that consensus is reserved for critical metadata (not bulk data) shows real understanding.

Key Takeaways

  • Consensus algorithms let distributed servers agree on one decision despite failures.
  • They rely on majority (quorum) agreement — any two majorities overlap, so conflicting decisions can't both win.
  • Run an odd number of nodes (3/5/7); the system stops writing rather than risk inconsistency without a majority.
  • Consensus is slow — reserve it for critical metadata (leader, config, locks), not bulk data.
  • Paxos is the classic (hard); Raft is the modern, understandable standard. Used by etcd, ZooKeeper, Consul.
Previous← Saga Pattern NextLeader Election →

#65Leader Election

One-line definition

Leader election is the process by which a group of servers chooses one of them to be the "leader" (the one in charge of coordinating), and automatically picks a new leader if the current one fails.

Many distributed tasks need a single coordinator to avoid chaos — one server to make decisions, assign work, or accept writes. Leader election is how the group fairly and reliably designates that one server.

Why You Need a Leader

Analogy: a team needs a captain

If every player on a team made independent decisions, there would be confusion. A single captain coordinates the strategy. But if the captain leaves the field, the team quickly picks a new one so play continues. Servers do the same: one leader coordinates; if it dies, a new election happens.

What the Leader Typically Does

  • Accepts all writes and decides their order (then replicates to followers).
  • Coordinates tasks so two servers do not do the same job twice.
  • Acts as the single source of truth for decisions.

How Election Works (Simply)

Followers expect regular heartbeats from the leader. If the leader's heartbeats stop, a follower suspects it is dead and starts an election — asking others to vote for it. Whoever gets a majority of votes becomes the new leader. (This is exactly how Raft does it.)

Leader dies 💥
→
Followers notice (no heartbeat)
→
Vote → new leader
Missing heartbeats trigger an election; a majority vote picks the new leader.
The "split-brain" danger

A risk in leader election is split-brain: two servers both think they are the leader (e.g. after a network split), causing conflicts. Requiring a majority to elect a leader prevents this — only one side of a split can have a majority.

Split-Brain, Made Concrete

Picture 5 nodes with leader L. A network partition splits them into a group of 2 (with L) and a group of 3. The group of 2 can't reach the others — but old L is still happily acting as leader on its side. Meanwhile the group of 3 sees no heartbeats, holds an election, and (having a majority) elects a new leader. Now there are two leaders accepting writes — split-brain — and their data diverges, which is a nightmare to reconcile. The majority rule is the cure: the minority side (2 nodes) can never get 3 votes, so it must step down and stop accepting writes. Only the majority side stays operational. This is why you need an odd number of nodes and majority quorum — it guarantees at most one leader.

Fencing: Stopping a "Zombie" Old Leader

One more subtlety: an old leader might be frozen (e.g. a long pause) and "wake up" still believing it's leader, after a new one was elected. To stop this zombie from corrupting data, systems use fencing tokens — each leadership term gets an increasing number, and downstream systems reject any request carrying an older token. So even if the old leader acts, its stale token is refused.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"A leader makes the system a single point of failure." No — the role is single, but it's automatically re-elected on failure, so the system keeps going. The leader is a logical role, not a fixed machine.

"Just pick the lowest-ID server as leader." Naive schemes break under partitions (split-brain). Real election needs majority quorum (and often fencing) to be safe.

Interview tip

Whenever your design has a "primary" (DB primary, coordinator, scheduler), mention "leader election via a majority quorum (e.g. Raft/ZooKeeper) with automatic failover, and fencing tokens to avoid split-brain." Naming split-brain and how majority quorum prevents it is a classic distributed-systems checkpoint.

Key Takeaways

  • Leader election picks one server to coordinate, and re-elects if it fails.
  • The leader typically orders writes and acts as the single source of truth.
  • Triggered by missing heartbeats; the winner needs a majority of votes.
  • Majority voting prevents split-brain (the minority side must stop accepting writes).
  • Fencing tokens stop a stale "zombie" old leader from corrupting data after a new election.
Previous← Consensus Algorithms NextConsistent Hashing →

#66Consistent Hashing

One-line definition

Consistent hashing is a clever technique for spreading data across servers so that when you add or remove a server, only a small fraction of data needs to move — instead of almost everything. It is the backbone of distributed caches and databases.

This is a famous, must-know system design topic. It solves a real headache in sharding: what happens to your data when the number of servers changes?

The Problem with Simple Hashing

Why "hash % N" breaks

A simple way to assign data to servers is server = hash(key) % N (N = number of servers). The disaster: if N changes (you add/remove a server), the % N result changes for almost every key, so nearly all data must move to new servers at once. That causes a massive reshuffle and cache misses everywhere.

The Solution: A Hash Ring

Consistent hashing places both servers and data keys on an imaginary circle (a "ring") using a hash. Each key is stored on the next server clockwise from it. The magic: when a server is added or removed, only the keys in its immediate neighborhood move — everything else stays put.

Hash Ring 🔵servers & keys placed around a circle
Add a server
→
Only nearby keys moverest unaffected
On the ring, a key goes to the next server clockwise; changes affect only a small slice.

Virtual Nodes (the practical refinement)

To keep the load even (so one server does not accidentally get a huge slice of the ring), each physical server is placed at many points on the ring as "virtual nodes". This spreads data more uniformly and makes rebalancing smoother.

Where it is used

Consistent hashing powers distributed caches and databases like Cassandra, DynamoDB, and large-scale caching layers. Whenever you need to scale a cluster up or down with minimal disruption, this is the technique.

The Numbers That Make It Click

Compare the two approaches concretely with 4 servers becoming 5. With hash % N, changing N from 4 to 5 changes the bucket for roughly 80% of all keys — a near-total reshuffle, and for a cache that means almost everything becomes a miss at once (a stampede onto your database). With consistent hashing, adding one server to a 4-server ring only moves about 1/5 of the keys (the slice now owned by the newcomer); the other ~80% stay exactly where they were. In general, adding the Nth server moves only ~1/N of the data. That difference — "move almost everything" vs "move a small slice" — is the entire reason the technique exists.

Why Virtual Nodes Are Essential, Not Optional

With only a few servers placed once each on the ring, the gaps between them are uneven by luck of hashing — one server might own a huge arc (and get hammered) while another owns a tiny one. Virtual nodes fix this by placing each physical server at many points (say 100–200) around the ring. The law of averages then makes each server's total share roughly equal, and when a server dies its load is spread across many others rather than dumped entirely on its single clockwise neighbour. Real systems always use virtual nodes for this reason.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Consistent hashing perfectly balances load." Not by itself — plain consistent hashing can be lumpy; it's virtual nodes that smooth the distribution.

"It eliminates all data movement." No — it minimises it to ~1/N. Some keys still move when membership changes; that's unavoidable and fine.

"It's only for caches." It also underpins data placement in Cassandra/DynamoDB and request routing in many load balancers.

Interview tip

This is a top-tier favourite. When sharding a cache/DB, say: "I'll use consistent hashing with virtual nodes so scaling the cluster only remaps ~1/N of keys instead of nearly all of them, avoiding a cache-miss stampede." Contrasting it explicitly with hash % N is exactly the answer interviewers are listening for.

Key Takeaways

  • Consistent hashing spreads data so adding/removing a server moves only a small fraction (~1/N) of keys.
  • It avoids the ~80% reshuffle and cache-miss stampede caused by simple hash % N.
  • Uses a hash ring: each key goes to the next server clockwise.
  • Virtual nodes are essential for even load and smooth rebalancing on failure.
  • Used by Cassandra, DynamoDB, distributed caches, and some load balancers.
Previous← Leader Election NextVector Clocks & Logical Clocks →

#67Vector Clocks & Logical Clocks

One-line definition

Logical clocks (and vector clocks) are ways to figure out the order of events across different servers without relying on real-world time, because clocks on different machines are never perfectly in sync.

In a distributed system you often need to know "did event A happen before event B?". You cannot trust wall-clock timestamps, because each server's clock drifts slightly. Logical clocks solve ordering using counters instead of time.

The Problem with Real Clocks

Clocks disagree

Server A's clock might say 10:00:01 and Server B's might say 10:00:00 for an event that actually happened later. Relying on these timestamps to order events leads to wrong conclusions. We need a method that does not depend on synchronized time.

Lamport (Logical) Clocks

Each server keeps a simple counter. Every time something happens, it increments. When servers send messages, they include their counter, and the receiver bumps its own counter to stay ahead. This guarantees: if A causes B, then A's number is smaller than B's. It captures "happened-before" ordering.

Limitation: Lamport clocks can tell you a possible order, but cannot always tell whether two events were truly concurrent (unrelated) or one really caused the other.

Vector Clocks

Vector clocks fix that. Instead of one counter, each server keeps a vector (a list) of counters — one slot per server. By comparing two vectors, you can tell precisely whether one event happened before another, or whether they were concurrent (a conflict that needs resolving).

Where it is used

Vector clocks help detect conflicting concurrent updates in systems like DynamoDB and Cassandra. For example, if two users edit the same item at the same time on different replicas, vector clocks reveal the conflict so the system can resolve it (e.g. keep both versions or merge them).

Why "Just Use Timestamps" Fails — the LWW Trap

A tempting shortcut is Last-Write-Wins (LWW): attach a wall-clock timestamp to each write and keep the "latest". The problem: clocks drift between servers (clock skew). So a write that truly happened later might carry an earlier timestamp and get thrown away — meaning you can silently lose data that should have won. LWW is simple and sometimes acceptable (Cassandra uses it by default), but it can quietly drop updates. Logical/vector clocks avoid this by ordering events on causality, not unreliable time.

What "Concurrent" Really Means Here

The key concept is "happened-before". If event A could have influenced B (e.g. B read A's result, or they're on the same machine in sequence), then A happened-before B. If neither could have influenced the other, they are concurrent — and concurrent writes to the same data are a genuine conflict that no ordering can resolve on its own. Vector clocks' superpower is detecting exactly this: comparing two vectors tells you "A before B", "B before A", or "concurrent — you must decide". The system then resolves it by keeping both versions (siblings, like Dynamo) for the app/user to merge, or applying a merge rule (CRDTs).

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Timestamps are good enough to order events." Not across machines — clock skew makes them unreliable, and naive LWW can lose data.

"Vector clocks resolve conflicts." They only detect conflicts (concurrency). Resolution (merge, keep-both, business rule) is a separate decision you must make.

"This is rarely relevant." Any multi-leader / multi-region writable system (Dynamo-style) hits concurrent writes — this is core to how they stay correct.

Interview tip

If a design allows concurrent writes to the same data across replicas, mention: "I can't rely on timestamps due to clock skew; I'd use vector clocks (or version vectors) to detect concurrent conflicts, then resolve via merge / keep-both / a business rule." Distinguishing detecting from resolving conflicts is the nuance that impresses.

Key Takeaways

  • Real machine clocks drift, so they cannot reliably order distributed events (and naive Last-Write-Wins can lose data).
  • Lamport clocks use a counter to capture "happened-before" ordering.
  • Vector clocks use a counter per server, so they can also detect concurrent (conflicting) events.
  • They detect conflicts; resolving them (merge / keep-both / rule) is a separate choice.
  • Used to detect and resolve conflicts in systems like DynamoDB and Cassandra.
Previous← Consistent Hashing NextQuorum-based Reads/Writes →

#68Quorum-based Reads/Writes

One-line definition

A quorum is the minimum number of servers that must agree for a read or write to be accepted. By requiring a majority for both, a distributed system can stay consistent even with replicated data.

When data is replicated across many nodes, how many copies must respond before you trust a read or confirm a write? Quorums give a precise, tunable answer that balances consistency, speed, and availability.

The Formula: W + R > N

Let:

  • N = total number of replicas holding the data.
  • W = how many replicas must confirm a write before it is considered done.
  • R = how many replicas must respond to a read.

The key rule: if W + R > N, then any read is guaranteed to overlap with the latest write — so you always read fresh data. This is how you get strong consistency from replicas.

Simple example (N = 3)

With 3 replicas, set W = 2 and R = 2. Since 2 + 2 = 4 > 3, any read of 2 replicas must include at least one that has the newest write. So you never miss the latest value. You also tolerate one replica being down for both reads and writes.

Tuning the Trade-off

SettingEffect
High WSafer writes, but slower writes
High RFresher reads, but slower reads
W=1, R=1Very fast, but eventual (may read stale)
W + R > NStrong consistency guaranteed
Why this is powerful

Quorums let you dial the balance between consistency and availability per operation. Systems like Cassandra and DynamoDB let you choose W and R, so you can pick strong consistency where it matters and fast/eventual where it does not.

Picking W and R for Your Workload

The W+R>N rule is the floor for strong consistency, but how you split it tunes performance. With N=3:

  • Write-heavy, want fast writes: use W=1, R=3. Writes confirm instantly (one replica), but reads must check all 3 to be sure they catch the latest. (1+3>3 ✓)
  • Read-heavy, want fast reads: use W=3, R=1. Reads are instant (any one replica is current), but writes are slower (must hit all 3). (3+1>3 ✓)
  • Balanced: W=2, R=2 — both moderately fast, tolerates one node down. The common default.
  • Max availability, accept staleness: W=1, R=1 (1+1≤3, so not strongly consistent) — fastest, but reads can be stale.

The lesson: put the cost on the side you do least. Read-heavy systems make writes pay; write-heavy systems make reads pay.

Quorums + Consistent Hashing + Vector Clocks = "Dynamo-style"

This topic is the capstone of Part 8 because the famous Dynamo design (behind DynamoDB and Cassandra) combines them all: consistent hashing (Topic 66) decides which N replicas hold a key, quorums (this topic) decide how many must respond, and vector clocks (Topic 67) detect conflicts when you relax W/R for availability. Seeing how these three fit together is exactly the kind of synthesis senior interviews reward.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Quorum always means a majority." Often, but not necessarily — W and R are tunable. W+R>N is what guarantees overlap; that can be achieved with non-majority splits like W=1,R=3.

"W+R>N gives full ACID consistency." It guarantees a read overlaps the latest write, but edge cases (failed writes, concurrent writes) still need conflict handling (vector clocks) — it's strong-ish, not free ACID.

Interview tip

Show you can tune it: "N=3 replicas; for this read-heavy path I'll use W=3, R=1 for fast reads; W+R>N keeps reads current." Then connect the trio — consistent hashing places replicas, quorums decide responses, vector clocks resolve conflicts. That end-to-end picture is the goal.

Key Takeaways

  • A quorum = the minimum replicas that must agree for a read/write.
  • N = replicas, W = write confirmations, R = read responses.
  • W + R > N guarantees you always read the latest write (strong consistency).
  • Tune the split (W=1/R=3 vs W=3/R=1) to make the less-frequent operation pay the cost.
  • With consistent hashing + vector clocks, quorums form the Dynamo-style design (Cassandra, DynamoDB).
Previous← Vector Clocks & Logical Clocks NextAuthentication vs Authorization →

#69Authentication vs Authorization

One-line definition

Authentication (AuthN) = proving who you are (login). Authorization (AuthZ) = deciding what you are allowed to do once you are identified. AuthN comes first, then AuthZ.

These two words sound alike and are constantly confused, but they are completely different steps. Getting them straight is fundamental to all security.

Real-life Analogy: Entering an Office

ID badge vs access level

Authentication = showing your ID at the entrance to prove you are really "Priya". Authorization = your badge's access level deciding which rooms you can enter. You might be authenticated (proven to be Priya) but not authorized to enter the server room. First they confirm who you are, then they check what you can access.

Authentication (Who are you?)

The user proves their identity. Common methods:

  • Something you know: password, PIN.
  • Something you have: phone (OTP), security key.
  • Something you are: fingerprint, face.
  • MFA (Multi-Factor): combine two or more of the above for stronger security.

Authorization (What can you do?)

After login, the system checks permissions. Common models:

  • RBAC (Role-Based): permissions based on roles (admin, editor, viewer).
  • ABAC (Attribute-Based): permissions based on attributes (department, location, time).
PointAuthenticationAuthorization
QuestionWho are you?What can you do?
HappensFirstAfter authentication
ExampleLogin with passwordCan you delete this file?

The Most Common Real-World Security Bug: Broken Authorization

Here's a sobering fact: the #1 item on the OWASP Top 10 (the industry's list of worst web vulnerabilities) is Broken Access Control — i.e. authorization done wrong. The classic bug is IDOR (Insecure Direct Object Reference): the API checks that you're logged in (AuthN ✓) but forgets to check that the data belongs to you (AuthZ ✗). So GET /orders/123 returns order 123 even if it's someone else's — just change the number and read everyone's orders. The lesson: authentication is not enough; every request must also verify the user is authorized for that specific resource. "Am I logged in?" and "Am I allowed to touch this?" are different checks, and forgetting the second is how real breaches happen.

RBAC vs ABAC, and Where to Enforce

  • RBAC (roles): simple and common — "admins can delete, viewers can only read". Easy to reason about, but role explosion happens when rules get fine-grained.
  • ABAC (attributes): flexible — "managers can approve expenses under ₹50k in their own department during business hours". Powerful for complex policies, harder to manage.
  • Enforce on the server, always: never trust the client. Hiding a "Delete" button in the UI is not authorization — the attacker just calls the API directly. The server must check every time.
Common misconceptions, cleared

"If a user is logged in, they're authorized." The dangerous myth behind most breaches — AuthN proves who, AuthZ must still check what they can access, per resource.

"Hiding the button is enough." No — client-side hiding is cosmetic; authorization must be enforced server-side on every request.

Interview tip

When designing any API, say it explicitly: "Each request is authenticated (who) and authorized for the specific resource (can this user access this exact object?), enforced server-side — to avoid IDOR/broken access control." This single sentence shows real security awareness.

Key Takeaways

  • Authentication = proving identity (login). Authorization = checking permissions. AuthN first, then AuthZ.
  • AuthN methods: password, OTP, biometrics, MFA. AuthZ models: RBAC (roles), ABAC (attributes).
  • Being authenticated does not mean you are authorized — check access to the specific resource every time.
  • Broken access control / IDOR is the #1 web vulnerability — always enforce AuthZ server-side.
Previous← Quorum-based Reads/Writes NextOAuth 2.0 & OpenID Connect →

#70OAuth 2.0 & OpenID Connect

One-line definition

OAuth 2.0 lets you grant an app limited access to your data on another service without sharing your password (this is "authorization"). OpenID Connect (OIDC) builds on OAuth to also handle login / identity (authentication) — this is "Login with Google".

Ever clicked "Sign in with Google" or let an app access your Google Photos? That is OAuth/OIDC. It lets you safely give apps access without handing over your actual password.

Real-life Analogy: A Hotel Key Card / Valet Key

Limited access, no master key

A valet key lets the parking attendant drive your car but not open the glovebox or trunk — limited access without your full key. OAuth gives apps a "valet key" to part of your data, without your password (the master key). You can also revoke it anytime.

The Key Players

  • Resource Owner: you, the user who owns the data.
  • Client: the app that wants access (e.g. a photo-printing app).
  • Authorization Server: issues tokens after you approve (e.g. Google's login).
  • Resource Server: holds your data and accepts the token (e.g. Google Photos).

How the Flow Works (Simplified)

  • 1. The app redirects you to Google to log in and approve specific permissions ("see your photos").
  • 2. You approve. Google gives the app a short-lived access token (not your password).
  • 3. The app uses that token to access only what you allowed — nothing more.
  • 4. You can revoke the token anytime, cutting off access.
You approve
→
Auth server gives token
→
App uses tokenlimited access
The app gets a limited token, never your password.

OAuth vs OpenID Connect

The key distinction

OAuth 2.0 = authorization (granting access to data). OpenID Connect = authentication (proving identity, i.e. login), added on top of OAuth. "Login with Google" uses OIDC; "let this app read my Google Drive" uses OAuth.

The Problem OAuth Actually Solves

Before OAuth, if an app wanted to access your Gmail contacts, you'd literally give it your Google password — meaning it could read your email, change your password, do anything, forever. OAuth was invented to kill that anti-pattern. It introduces delegated, scoped, revocable access: the app gets a token limited to specific permissions ("read contacts only"), never sees your password, and you can revoke it anytime without changing your password. Three properties to remember: scope (limited permissions), delegation (acting on your behalf without being you), and revocability.

Why "Access Token + Refresh Token"?

OAuth issues two tokens for a reason rooted in earlier topics. The access token is short-lived (minutes) so that if it leaks, the damage window is tiny — but it can't be easily revoked (it's just checked, not looked up). The refresh token is long-lived and stored more securely; when the access token expires, the app quietly exchanges the refresh token for a new one. This gives you both scalability (most requests just validate a token) and a revocation point (revoke the refresh token to cut off access) — exactly the trade-off from session management (Topic 48).

Common misconceptions, cleared

"OAuth logs me in." Not by itself — plain OAuth is about access to resources. Login/identity is OpenID Connect (which adds an "ID token" identifying the user). "Sign in with Google" is OIDC, not bare OAuth.

"The app sees my password." The whole point is that it never does — it only gets a scoped token.

"OAuth encrypts the connection." No — OAuth assumes you're already on HTTPS (Topic 73); it handles authorization, not transport security.

Interview tip

If a design needs "login with Google/GitHub" or third-party data access, say: "OAuth 2.0 for delegated, scoped access with access + refresh tokens; OpenID Connect on top if I need identity/login." Calling out scopes and the token pair shows you understand the why, not just the buzzword.

Key Takeaways

  • OAuth 2.0 grants apps limited (scoped), delegated, revocable access without sharing your password.
  • Uses short-lived access tokens + long-lived refresh tokens for safety plus a revocation point.
  • Players: resource owner (you), client (app), authorization server, resource server.
  • OAuth = authorization; OpenID Connect = authentication (login) built on top of OAuth.
  • It does not provide transport security — always run over HTTPS.
Previous← Authentication vs Authorization NextJWT (JSON Web Tokens) →

#71JWT (JSON Web Tokens)

One-line definition

A JWT is a compact, digitally-signed token that carries user information. After you log in, the server gives you a JWT; you send it with each request to prove who you are — without the server needing to store a session.

JWTs are the most common way to do stateless authentication (recall Topic 47/48). The token itself carries the proof, so any server can verify it without looking anything up.

Real-life Analogy: A Tamper-Proof Wristband

A festival wristband

At a festival, you show ID once and get a sealed wristband. After that, you just flash the wristband to enter areas — staff do not re-check your ID, because the wristband is hard to fake. A JWT is a digital tamper-proof wristband: show it, and any guard (server) can trust it instantly.

The Three Parts of a JWT

A JWT looks like xxxxx.yyyyy.zzzzz — three parts separated by dots:

  • Header: says what type of token and which signing algorithm.
  • Payload: the actual data (claims) — like user id, role, and an expiry time. Note: this is readable, not encrypted — never put secrets here.
  • Signature: a cryptographic signature created with a secret key. It proves the token was not tampered with.

How It Works

  • 1. You log in; the server creates a JWT, signs it with its secret key, and gives it to you.
  • 2. You send the JWT with every request (usually in the Authorization header).
  • 3. The server verifies the signature using its key. If valid and unexpired, it trusts the token — no database lookup needed.
Important cautions

The signature stops tampering, but the payload is not secret (anyone can read it). Also, JWTs are hard to revoke before they expire (since the server stores nothing) — so keep expiry times short and use refresh tokens for longer sessions.

Signed ≠ Encrypted (the #1 JWT misunderstanding)

This trips up almost everyone, so internalise it: a standard JWT is signed, not encrypted. The payload is just Base64-encoded — which is not encryption; anyone can paste the token into a decoder and read every claim. The signature only guarantees the token wasn't altered (integrity), not that it's hidden (confidentiality). So: put a user id and role in a JWT, never a password, credit card, or secret. If you genuinely need the contents hidden, you need JWE (encrypted JWT) — but that's rare.

The Revocation Problem — and Real Fixes

JWT's superpower (statelessness — no server lookup) is also its weakness: if you fire an employee or a token is stolen, you can't instantly invalidate that JWT, because the server isn't tracking it. It stays valid until it expires. Real systems handle this with:

  • Short-lived access tokens (5–15 min) + a refresh token — so the damage window is tiny and you revoke at the refresh step.
  • A token blocklist/denylist in Redis for emergency revocation (reintroduces a small bit of state — a deliberate trade-off).
  • Token versioning — bump a "token version" on the user record so old tokens fail validation.

JWT vs Server-Side Sessions (when to choose which)

Use JWTs when you want statelessness and scale across many services (e.g. microservices, mobile, third-party APIs) and can tolerate the revocation delay. Use server-side sessions (Topic 48) when you need instant revocation and full control (banking, admin panels). It's the same revocation-vs-scalability trade-off seen throughout this part.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"JWTs are encrypted/secure to store secrets in." No — signed, not encrypted; the payload is publicly readable.

"JWTs can be revoked like sessions." Not natively — you need short expiry, a blocklist, or versioning to approximate revocation.

"Use alg: none / trust the header's algorithm." A famous attack — always pin the expected algorithm server-side, or attackers forge tokens with no signature.

Interview tip

Say: "JWTs for stateless auth across services, signed (not encrypted) so no secrets in the payload, short-lived access tokens + refresh tokens, and a Redis blocklist for emergency revocation." Mentioning the signed-vs-encrypted distinction and the revocation strategy is exactly what security-minded interviewers want.

Key Takeaways

  • A JWT is a signed token carrying user info, enabling stateless authentication. Parts: Header.Payload.Signature.
  • Signed ≠ encrypted — the payload is readable (Base64), so never store secrets in it.
  • Hard to revoke early — use short expiry + refresh tokens, a Redis blocklist, or token versioning.
  • Choose JWTs for scale/statelessness; server-side sessions for instant revocation.
  • Always pin the signing algorithm server-side (beware the alg: none attack).
Previous← OAuth 2.0 & OpenID Connect NextAPI Security →

#72API Security

One-line definition

API security is the set of practices that protect your APIs from misuse and attacks — making sure only the right people access them, the right amount, over secure channels, with valid data.

APIs are the doors into your system. If they are not secured, attackers walk right in. This topic gathers the key defenses, most of which build on earlier topics.

The Core Practices

  • Authentication & Authorization: verify identity (JWT/OAuth) and check permissions on every request (Topics 69–71).
  • HTTPS everywhere: always encrypt traffic with TLS so data cannot be read or altered in transit (Topic 6, 73).
  • Rate limiting & throttling: cap requests to prevent abuse and DoS (Topic 49).
  • Input validation: never trust input — validate and sanitize everything to block injection attacks (Topic 75).
  • API keys / tokens: identify and control which apps can call your API.
  • Least privilege: give each client only the minimum access it needs.
  • Use an API Gateway: centralize auth, rate limiting, and logging (Topic 50).

Idempotency (a key safety idea)

Make repeats safe

Design important operations (especially payments) to be idempotent — doing the same request twice has the same effect as doing it once. Clients send an "idempotency key"; if the server sees a repeat, it ignores the duplicate. This prevents double-charges from retries (Topic 57).

More Protections

  • CORS: control which websites are allowed to call your API from browsers.
  • Avoid exposing sensitive data: return only what is needed; never leak internal details in errors.
  • Logging & monitoring: watch for suspicious patterns (Part 11).

The Golden Rule: Never Trust the Client

Almost every API vulnerability traces back to one mistake: trusting input or checks that come from the client. The browser, mobile app, and any "validation" they do can be bypassed entirely — an attacker just talks to your API directly with custom tools. So the server must re-do everything: re-validate all input, re-check authorization for the specific resource, re-enforce limits and prices. Treat every incoming request as potentially hostile, even from "your own" app. Client-side validation is for user convenience; server-side validation is for security.

Defense in Depth: Layers, Not a Wall

Good API security is many overlapping layers so that if one fails, others still protect you: TLS (transport) + gateway auth + per-request authorization + input validation + rate limiting + monitoring. This builds directly on earlier topics — the API gateway (Topic 50) is where you centralise auth, rate limiting (Topic 49), and TLS termination so individual services don't each reinvent them. Also apply least privilege everywhere: each token, service, and DB account gets only the minimum access it needs, so a single compromise has a small blast radius.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"My mobile app validates input, so the API is safe." No — attackers skip your app and call the API directly. Validate on the server.

"HTTPS alone makes my API secure." HTTPS only protects data in transit; you still need auth, authorization, validation, and rate limiting.

"Obscure endpoints won't be found." Security by obscurity fails — scanners and leaked docs find everything. Every endpoint needs real protection.

Interview tip

Summarise API security as layers: "TLS everywhere, authenticate + authorize each request at the gateway, validate all input server-side, rate limit per client, make writes idempotent, and apply least privilege." Leading with "never trust the client" frames it perfectly.

Key Takeaways

  • API security = authenticate + authorize every request, always use HTTPS, and rate limit.
  • Never trust the client — re-validate input and re-check authorization on the server every time.
  • Use defense in depth (overlapping layers) and least privilege to shrink blast radius.
  • Make critical operations idempotent so retries cannot cause double effects.
  • An API gateway helps centralize these protections.
Previous← JWT (JSON Web Tokens) NextEncryption (TLS/SSL) →

#73Encryption (TLS/SSL)

One-line definition

Encryption scrambles data so only someone with the right key can read it. TLS/SSL is the technology that encrypts data in transit over the internet (the "S" in HTTPS).

Without encryption, anyone who intercepts your data (on public WiFi, etc.) can read it. Encryption turns readable data into scrambled nonsense for everyone except the intended recipient.

Two Types of Encryption

  • Symmetric encryption: the same key locks and unlocks the data. Very fast, but both sides must somehow share the key secretly.
  • Asymmetric encryption: uses a key pair — a public key (shared openly, locks data) and a private key (kept secret, unlocks it). Slower, but solves the key-sharing problem.
Analogy: a mailbox

Asymmetric: anyone can drop a letter through your mailbox slot (public key), but only you have the key to open it and read the letters (private key). This is how strangers can send you secret data safely without pre-sharing a key.

How TLS Works (the clever combo)

TLS uses both: it uses slow asymmetric encryption just once at the start (the "handshake") to safely agree on a shared secret key, then switches to fast symmetric encryption for the actual data. Best of both worlds — secure key exchange plus speed.

Handshakeasymmetric, share a secret key
→
Data transferfast symmetric encryption
TLS uses asymmetric to exchange a key, then symmetric for speed.

Encryption in Transit vs at Rest

  • In transit (TLS): protects data while it travels over the network.
  • At rest: protects stored data (databases, disks) so a stolen disk is useless without the key.

The Other Half: Certificates and Trust

Encryption alone isn't enough — you also need to know you're talking to the real server, not an impostor (a "man-in-the-middle"). That's what certificates and Certificate Authorities (CAs) provide. During the TLS handshake, the server presents a certificate (its public key, signed by a trusted CA like Let's Encrypt). Your browser trusts a set of CAs, so it can verify the certificate is genuine and the public key really belongs to that domain. This is why a self-signed or expired certificate triggers scary browser warnings — encryption would still work, but identity can't be trusted. So HTTPS gives you three things at once: confidentiality (encryption), integrity (tamper detection), and authenticity (you're talking to the real site).

Hashing Is Not Encryption

A crucial distinction for passwords: encryption is reversible (with the key you get the data back); hashing is one-way (you can't reverse it). You never encrypt passwords — you hash them (with a slow, salted algorithm like bcrypt/argon2), so even if the database is stolen, the original passwords can't be recovered. Encrypt data you need to read back (card numbers, messages); hash data you only need to verify (passwords).

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Encryption proves who I'm talking to." No — encryption hides data; certificates/CAs prove identity. You need both (that's full HTTPS).

"Passwords should be encrypted in the DB." No — they should be hashed and salted (one-way), never reversibly encrypted.

"Asymmetric is just better than symmetric." They're different tools — asymmetric solves key exchange/identity but is slow; symmetric is fast for bulk data. TLS uses each where it shines.

Interview tip

Say "TLS for data in transit, encryption at rest for stored data, certificates/CAs for server identity, and hashing (bcrypt/argon2) — not encryption — for passwords." Distinguishing encrypt-vs-hash and naming the CA/identity role demonstrates solid security fundamentals.

Key Takeaways

  • Encryption scrambles data so only key-holders can read it.
  • Symmetric = one shared key (fast); Asymmetric = public/private key pair (solves key sharing & identity).
  • TLS combines both: asymmetric handshake to share a key, then fast symmetric transfer; certificates/CAs prove identity.
  • Encrypt data both in transit (TLS) and at rest.
  • Hash passwords (one-way, salted) — never reversibly encrypt them.
Previous← API Security NextDDoS Protection →

#74DDoS Protection

One-line definition

A DDoS (Distributed Denial of Service) attack floods your system with massive fake traffic from many sources at once, trying to overwhelm it so real users cannot get in. DDoS protection is how you absorb or block that flood.

The goal of a DDoS attack is not to steal data but to make your service unavailable by exhausting its resources. "Distributed" means it comes from thousands of machines (often a botnet) at once.

Real-life Analogy: A Fake Crowd Blocking a Shop

A mob that buys nothing

Imagine thousands of fake "customers" crowding into a shop's doorway with no intention of buying — real customers cannot even get in. That is a DDoS: fake traffic packing your system so genuine users are locked out.

How to Defend Against DDoS

  • CDN & scale: a large CDN absorbs huge traffic across its global network, soaking up the flood before it reaches you (Topic 18).
  • Rate limiting: cap requests per IP/user to throttle abusive sources (Topic 49).
  • Web Application Firewall (WAF): filters out malicious traffic patterns before they hit your servers.
  • Traffic filtering / scrubbing: specialized services (Cloudflare, AWS Shield) detect attack traffic and drop it, passing only clean traffic.
  • Auto-scaling: temporarily add capacity to absorb spikes (though attackers may scale too).
  • Blackholing / IP blocking: drop traffic from known-bad sources.
Attack flood 🌊
→
CDN + WAF + scrubbingabsorb & filter
clean →
Your servers
Protection layers absorb and filter attack traffic so only clean traffic reaches you.
Key idea

You cannot always stop a DDoS at the source — the defense is having more capacity and smart filtering than the attacker can overwhelm. That is why companies rely on huge providers (Cloudflare, AWS) whose networks dwarf most attacks.

Three Layers of Attack (and why the layer matters)

"DDoS" isn't one thing — attacks target different layers, and each needs a different defense:

  • Volumetric (network layer): raw flood of traffic (e.g. UDP/amplification) to saturate your bandwidth. Defense: huge-capacity CDN/scrubbing networks that simply have more pipe than the attacker.
  • Protocol attacks: exploit how connections work (e.g. a SYN flood opening half-connections to exhaust your server's connection table). Defense: SYN cookies, firewalls, load balancers that absorb this.
  • Application layer (Layer 7): the sneakiest — looks like real requests (e.g. hammering an expensive search endpoint). Low traffic volume but high server cost, so it slips past volume-based defenses. Defense: WAF, rate limiting, bot detection, CAPTCHAs.

The insight: a giant CDN stops the volumetric flood easily, but L7 attacks need smart filtering because each request looks legitimate.

Why "Distributed" Is the Whole Problem

A plain DoS comes from one source — you just block that IP. A DDoS comes from thousands of hijacked machines (a botnet) across the globe, so there's no single IP to block, and the traffic can be indistinguishable from a real traffic spike. That's exactly why the realistic defense is "out-scale and filter at the edge" rather than "find and block the attacker" — you usually can't pinpoint them.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Auto-scaling solves DDoS." Partly — it can absorb spikes, but it also means you pay for the attacker's fake traffic (a "denial of wallet"), and attackers can scale too. Filtering bad traffic is essential, not just scaling.

"A DDoS steals data." No — its goal is unavailability, not theft (though it's sometimes a smokescreen for another attack).

"Blocking IPs stops it." Rarely — thousands of distributed, rotating IPs make manual blocking futile.

Interview tip

Mention DDoS defense for any public-facing system: "front everything with a CDN/WAF (Cloudflare/AWS Shield) to absorb volumetric attacks, plus rate limiting and bot detection for application-layer attacks." Distinguishing volumetric vs L7 attacks shows you understand it's not one problem.

Key Takeaways

  • A DDoS floods you with fake traffic from a distributed botnet to make you unavailable (not to steal data).
  • Attacks hit different layers: volumetric, protocol, and sneaky application-layer (L7) — each needs different defenses.
  • Defenses: CDN absorption, rate limiting, WAF, traffic scrubbing, bot detection, auto-scaling.
  • Core strategy: more capacity + smart filtering at the edge — you usually can't just block the source.
  • Providers like Cloudflare and AWS Shield specialize in this.
Previous← Encryption (TLS/SSL) NextSQL Injection, XSS, CSRF →

#75SQL Injection, XSS & CSRF

One-line definition

These are three of the most common web attacks. SQL Injection tricks your database. XSS injects malicious scripts into pages. CSRF tricks a logged-in user's browser into making unwanted requests. Knowing them — and their fixes — is essential.

1. SQL Injection (SQLi)

The attacker enters specially crafted text into an input (like a login box) that gets injected into your database query, letting them read, change, or delete data.

Example

If your query is built by gluing user input directly, an attacker typing ' OR '1'='1 can turn a login check into one that is always true — logging in without a password.

Fix: use parameterized queries / prepared statements (never glue user input into SQL), and validate input. This keeps data separate from the query command.

2. Cross-Site Scripting (XSS)

The attacker injects malicious JavaScript into a page (e.g. through a comment field). When other users view the page, the script runs in their browsers — stealing cookies, sessions, or data.

Fix: escape/sanitize all user-generated content before displaying it, use a Content Security Policy (CSP), and mark cookies HttpOnly so scripts cannot read them.

3. Cross-Site Request Forgery (CSRF)

The attacker tricks a logged-in user's browser into silently sending a request to a site they are authenticated on (e.g. a hidden form that transfers money), abusing the user's existing session.

Fix: use CSRF tokens (a secret value the server checks on each sensitive request), SameSite cookies, and verify the request origin.

AttackWhat it doesMain fix
SQL InjectionManipulates DB queriesParameterized queries
XSSRuns malicious scripts in users' browsersEscape/sanitize output, CSP
CSRFForces unwanted actions using a user's sessionCSRF tokens, SameSite cookies
The golden rule

Never trust user input. Validate it, sanitize it, and keep data separate from commands. Most web attacks come down to input that was trusted when it should not have been.

The One Idea Behind SQLi and XSS: Data Treated as Code

SQL Injection and XSS look different but share a single root cause: untrusted data getting interpreted as code/commands. In SQLi, your input is treated as part of the SQL command. In XSS, your input is treated as JavaScript in the page. The fix in both cases is the same principle: keep data and code strictly separate. Parameterized queries send the SQL command and the data on separate channels, so input can never become command. Output escaping turns <script> into harmless text so it can never become executable. Understand this one idea and both attacks (and many others) make sense.

How CSRF Differs (it abuses trust, not input)

CSRF is the odd one out — it doesn't inject anything into your site. Instead it exploits the fact that browsers automatically attach your cookies to requests to a site. So a malicious page can quietly make your browser fire a real, authenticated request to your bank ("transfer money"), and the bank can't tell it wasn't you. The fix works by requiring a secret the attacker can't know or send: a CSRF token (a random value embedded in your real forms) or SameSite cookies (which tell the browser not to send cookies on cross-site requests). Note: XSS can defeat CSRF protections, which is why fixing XSS is foundational.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Hashing/escaping in the frontend is enough." No — SQLi must be prevented at the server/DB layer (parameterized queries); client checks are bypassable.

"An ORM/framework makes me immune." Mostly safe by default, but raw queries, string concatenation, or innerHTML/dangerous rendering still open holes.

"XSS and CSRF are the same." No — XSS injects code into your page; CSRF rides your existing session from another site. Different attacks, different fixes.

Interview tip

Tie them to the root cause: "SQLi and XSS both come from mixing data with code — fix with parameterized queries and output escaping/CSP; CSRF abuses auto-sent cookies — fix with CSRF tokens / SameSite." Referencing the OWASP Top 10 and the data-vs-code principle signals genuine security literacy.

Key Takeaways

  • SQL Injection: malicious input manipulates DB queries — fix with parameterized queries.
  • XSS: injected scripts run in users' browsers — fix by escaping output, CSP, and HttpOnly cookies.
  • SQLi & XSS share one root cause: data treated as code — keep data and commands strictly separate.
  • CSRF: abuses a logged-in session via auto-sent cookies — fix with CSRF tokens and SameSite cookies.
  • The overarching rule: never trust user input (these top the OWASP list).
Previous← DDoS Protection NextBlock vs Object vs File Storage →

#76Block vs Object vs File Storage

One-line definition

There are three fundamental ways to store data: Block (raw chunks, like a hard drive), File (folders and files, like your computer), and Object (data + metadata with a unique id, like cloud storage S3). Each suits different needs.

"Storage" is not one thing. Choosing the right type matters for performance, scale, and cost. Let us understand all three simply.

1. Block Storage

Data is split into fixed-size blocks, each with an address, with no built-in structure on top. It is the lowest-level, fastest storage — like a raw hard drive or SSD. Software (like a database or OS) builds structure on top of it.

  • Best for: databases, virtual machine disks — anything needing fast, low-level access.
  • Example: AWS EBS.

2. File Storage

Data is organized as files inside folders (a hierarchy), exactly like your laptop's file system. Easy for humans and many apps to use, shareable across machines.

  • Best for: shared documents, traditional apps that expect a file system.
  • Example: network drives (NFS), AWS EFS.

3. Object Storage

Data is stored as objects — each object is the data plus metadata plus a unique id, all in a flat space (no folders). It scales almost infinitely and is cheap, accessed via APIs (HTTP).

  • Best for: images, videos, backups, huge amounts of unstructured data.
  • Example: AWS S3, Google Cloud Storage.
TypeStructureBest forExample
BlockRaw blocksDatabases, VM disksAWS EBS
FileFolders & filesShared filesNFS, AWS EFS
ObjectObjects + metadata (flat)Media, backups, big dataAWS S3
Analogy

Block = raw bricks you build anything from. File = a filing cabinet with folders. Object = a valet who takes your item, gives you a ticket (id), and stores it somewhere in a giant warehouse — you just present the ticket to get it back.

The Real Decision Driver: How You Modify Data

The cleanest way to choose is by how you change data. Block lets you modify any small piece in place (overwrite byte 500 without touching the rest) — essential for a database constantly updating rows, which is why databases sit on block storage. Object storage is the opposite: you generally replace the whole object at once (you don't edit byte 500 of a photo on S3 — you re-upload it). That immutability is precisely what lets object storage scale to trillions of items and stay cheap. File sits in between — a familiar folder hierarchy with shared read/write, but it doesn't scale as limitlessly as object storage. So: frequent in-place edits → block; write-once-read-many media → object; shared documents → file.

Why "Folders" in S3 Are an Illusion

A subtle but important point: object storage has a flat namespace — there are no real folders. A key like photos/2024/beach.jpg looks like a path, but the / is just part of the name; the console fakes a folder view for your comfort. This flatness is a feature: with no directory tree to traverse or lock, the system scales horizontally without limit. Real file systems must maintain that tree, which is part of why they don't scale as far.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Object storage is just a file system in the cloud." No — it's flat (no real folders), accessed via HTTP API (not mounted as a drive normally), and objects are typically replaced whole, not edited in place.

"Object storage is slow, so it's worse." It has higher per-request latency than block, but near-infinite scale, durability, and low cost — different tool, not worse. You wouldn't run a database on it, but it's perfect for media.

Interview tip

This appears constantly in design questions involving uploads. Say: "user-uploaded images/videos go to object storage (S3); the database stores only the metadata and the object's URL/key." Choosing object storage for blobs and keeping the DB lean is the expected, correct instinct.

Key Takeaways

  • Block = raw, fast, in-place-editable chunks — for databases and VM disks.
  • File = folders and files (a hierarchy) — for shared documents.
  • Object = data + metadata + id in a flat space, replaced whole — for media, backups, huge unstructured data (S3).
  • Choose by how you modify data: in-place edits → block; write-once media → object; shared docs → file.
  • Store blobs in object storage; keep only their metadata/URL in the database.
Previous← SQL Injection, XSS, CSRF NextObject Storage Internals (S3) →

#77Object Storage Internals (S3)

One-line definition

Object storage (like Amazon S3) stores files as objects in buckets, identified by a unique key. It is built to be virtually unlimited in scale, highly durable, and cheap — the default home for media and backups on the web.

We met object storage in Topic 76. Now let us peek inside how something like S3 achieves its famous scale and durability.

The Building Blocks

  • Bucket: a top-level container for your objects (like a root folder).
  • Object: the actual data (a file) plus metadata (size, type, custom tags).
  • Key: the unique name used to retrieve the object (e.g. photos/2024/beach.jpg). It looks like a path but the storage is actually flat.

How It Achieves Durability & Scale

  • Replication: every object is stored as multiple copies across different machines and data centers, so hardware failures never lose data. (S3 advertises "11 nines" of durability.)
  • Flat namespace: no real folder tree to maintain, so it scales to trillions of objects easily.
  • Distributed across many servers: load and data spread out (often using consistent hashing, Topic 66).
  • Accessed via HTTP API: simple GET/PUT requests — works from anywhere.

Useful Features

  • Versioning: keep old versions of an object so you can recover from mistakes.
  • Storage classes / lifecycle: move rarely-used data to cheaper, colder storage automatically (e.g. S3 Glacier for archives).
  • Access control & encryption: fine-grained permissions and encryption at rest.
  • Static website & CDN integration: often paired with a CDN to serve files fast worldwide.
Where it is used

Netflix stores video files in object storage; Instagram and most apps store user photos there; backups, logs, and data lakes live there too. Whenever you need to store lots of files cheaply and durably, object storage is the answer.

The Killer Pattern: Pre-Signed URLs

A pattern you should know cold, because it appears in nearly every "design a system with uploads" question. When a user uploads a photo, you do not route the file through your servers (that wastes your bandwidth and CPU). Instead, your server generates a pre-signed URL — a temporary, permission-limited link — and the client uploads the file directly to S3 using it. Downloads work the same way. The benefits: your app servers stay lean (they never touch the bytes), it scales effortlessly, and access is still controlled (the URL expires and is scoped). Pair this with a CDN in front of S3 for fast global delivery.

What "11 Nines" of Durability Actually Means

S3's famous "eleven 9s" (99.999999999%) durability means that if you store 10 million objects, you'd expect to lose one roughly every 10,000 years. It achieves this by automatically storing each object redundantly across multiple devices in multiple data centers (availability zones) and constantly verifying integrity. Durability (will my data survive?) is different from availability (can I reach it right now?) — S3's durability is higher than its availability. This is why object storage is the go-to for backups and irreplaceable data.

Common misconceptions, cleared

"Upload files through my server to S3." Wasteful at scale — use pre-signed URLs so clients talk to S3 directly; your server just authorizes.

"Object storage is strongly consistent / good for live editing." It's optimized for whole-object replace and read-many; it's not a database. Don't use it for data that's constantly mutated in place.

"Durable means always available." No — durability is "won't be lost"; availability is "reachable now". They're separate guarantees.

Interview tip

For any upload/media feature, draw it as: "client gets a pre-signed URL → uploads directly to S3 → we store the key + metadata in the DB → serve via CDN." Mentioning pre-signed URLs and lifecycle policies (move cold data to Glacier) shows you know how object storage is used in practice, not just in theory.

Key Takeaways

  • Object storage uses buckets, objects (data + metadata), and unique keys.
  • Huge durability ("11 nines") via cross-data-center replication; scales via a flat namespace.
  • Use pre-signed URLs so clients upload/download directly — your servers never handle the bytes.
  • Supports versioning, lifecycle/cold storage (Glacier), and encryption; pair with a CDN for delivery.
  • Durability ≠ availability. The standard home for media, backups, and big unstructured data.
Previous← Block vs Object vs File Storage NextDistributed File Systems (HDFS) →

#78Distributed File Systems (HDFS)

One-line definition

A Distributed File System stores huge files by splitting them into chunks spread across many machines, while still letting you treat it like one big file system. HDFS (Hadoop Distributed File System) is the classic example, built for big data.

When a file (or dataset) is too big for any single machine — think petabytes of logs — you need to spread it across a cluster. A distributed file system makes that cluster look and behave like one giant disk.

How HDFS Works

  • Files are split into large blocks (e.g. 128 MB each), which are distributed across many machines.
  • Each block is replicated (typically 3 copies) on different machines, so a machine failure never loses data.
  • NameNode (the master): keeps the "map" of which blocks live where (metadata).
  • DataNodes (the workers): actually store the blocks and serve them.
NameNodeknows where every block is
↓
DataNode 1blocks + copies
DataNode 2
DataNode 3
The NameNode tracks metadata; DataNodes store replicated blocks.

Key Design Ideas

  • Built for huge files and throughput, not tiny files or low latency — great for batch processing (e.g. analytics).
  • Move computation to the data: instead of moving giant files to where the code is, run the code on the machine that holds the data (this is how Hadoop/MapReduce works).
  • Fault tolerant by replication — lose a DataNode, the copies elsewhere keep data safe.
Where it fits

Distributed file systems power big data analytics — processing enormous datasets across clusters. HDFS underpins the Hadoop ecosystem; similar ideas appear in Google's GFS and many data platforms.

Why "Move Compute to Data" Is the Whole Point

This is the single most important idea here. Imagine a 10 TB dataset spread across 100 machines, and you want to count something in it. The naive approach — copy all 10 TB over the network to one powerful machine — would take forever, because the network is the bottleneck, far slower than local disk. HDFS flips this: it sends the small piece of code to the 100 machines, each processes its own local block in parallel, and only the tiny results travel back. Moving a few KB of code is trillions of times cheaper than moving terabytes of data. This "data locality" principle is what makes big-data processing (MapReduce, Spark) feasible at all.

Why Big Blocks, and the NameNode Weakness

HDFS uses huge blocks (128 MB+) deliberately: it's built for a few enormous files scanned sequentially, so large blocks mean less metadata to track and efficient streaming reads. The flip side — and a classic exam point — is the "small files problem": millions of tiny files overwhelm the NameNode, because it must hold all block metadata in memory. That also makes the NameNode a single point of failure / bottleneck; production clusters run a standby NameNode (with consensus, Topic 64) for failover. So HDFS is brilliant for "few giant files, batch throughput" and poor for "many tiny files, low latency".

Common misconceptions, cleared

"HDFS is good for lots of small files." The opposite — small files crush the NameNode's memory. It's built for few huge files.

"It's for low-latency, real-time reads." No — it's optimized for high throughput batch processing, not fast individual lookups. Use a database/object store for that.

"HDFS and object storage (S3) are the same." Related but different — HDFS co-locates compute with data for analytics; modern clouds increasingly run compute against data in object storage instead.

Interview tip

Bring up data locality for analytics designs: "for petabyte-scale batch processing I'd use a distributed file system (HDFS) or object storage with a compute engine like Spark, moving compute to the data rather than data to compute." Mentioning the small-files problem and the NameNode SPOF shows real depth.

Key Takeaways

  • A distributed file system splits huge files into replicated blocks across many machines.
  • HDFS: a NameNode tracks block metadata (in memory); DataNodes store them (usually 3 copies).
  • Core idea: move compute to the data — the network, not the disk, is the bottleneck.
  • Built for few huge files + batch throughput; weak at many small files / low latency (NameNode limits).
  • The backbone of big data processing (Hadoop/Spark).
Previous← Object Storage Internals (S3) NextTime-Series Databases →

#79Time-Series Databases

One-line definition

A Time-Series Database (TSDB) is specially built to store and query data stamped with time — measurements that arrive continuously, like CPU usage every second, stock prices, or IoT sensor readings.

Lots of data is naturally about "what happened at this time": metrics, sensor readings, prices. General databases handle this poorly at scale, so TSDBs are optimized just for it.

What Makes Time-Series Data Special

  • Always time-stamped and usually arrives in time order.
  • Write-heavy: huge volumes of new points constantly flowing in.
  • Append-only: you rarely update old points — you just add new ones.
  • Queried by time ranges: "average CPU over the last hour", "price trend this week".

How TSDBs Optimize for This

  • Efficient time-based storage: data is organized by time for fast range queries.
  • Heavy compression: consecutive readings are similar, so they compress extremely well — saving huge space.
