Sync itself is push-based: when the server records a change, it notifies the user's other online devices (long-poll/WebSocket) to pull the new chunks — offline devices reconcile on next connect. This is the consistency/conflict material from Part 8 applied directly.

Interview tip — what they probe

Expect: "How do you avoid re-uploading a whole file for a one-line change?" (chunk hashing + delta sync), "How do you save storage across users?" (dedup by chunk hash), "What if two devices edit offline?" (conflict detection via version vectors, conflicted copies), and "How do other devices learn of changes?" (sync service push). The chunk-hash idea unlocks almost every feature — lead with it.

Key Takeaways

  • Split files into chunks; store chunks in object storage keyed by content hash, map files→chunks in a metadata DB.
  • Chunk hashing enables delta sync, dedup, resumable and parallel transfers (use content-defined chunking).
  • A sync service pushes notifications so other devices pull changed chunks.
  • Handle offline conflicts with version vectors / conflicted copies; metadata is the source of truth.
Previous← Design Swiggy / Zomato NextDesign Notification System →

#98Design a Notification System

The goal

Build a system that sends notifications to users across multiple channels — push (mobile), SMS, email, in-app — reliably and at massive scale.

Step 1: Requirements

  • Functional: send notifications via push/SMS/email/in-app; support templates; respect user preferences (opt-outs); handle priorities.
  • Non-functional: highly scalable (millions of notifications), reliable (do not lose or duplicate), handle third-party provider failures gracefully.

Step 2: The Core Design — Queues + Workers

Decouple sending from generating

Services that want to notify someone just drop a message into a queue (Topic 38) — they do not wait. Dedicated worker services pull from the queue and actually send via the right channel. This decoupling absorbs spikes and lets sending scale independently.

Services
→
Notification queue
→
Channel workers
→
Push / SMS / Email providers
Producers enqueue; workers send via external providers, scaling independently.

Step 3: Key Components

  • Notification service (API): receives requests, validates, applies templates.
  • Preference service: checks if the user has opted in for that channel/type (respect opt-outs and quiet hours).
  • Queues per channel/priority: separate queues so urgent (OTP) is not stuck behind bulk (marketing) — this is a bulkhead idea (Topic 58).
  • Channel workers: integrate with third-party providers (APNs/FCM for push, Twilio for SMS, SES for email).
  • Template service: manages reusable message templates.

Step 4: Reliability Concerns

  • Retries with backoff (Topic 57) when a provider fails; dead-letter queue for messages that keep failing.
  • Idempotency / dedup (Topic 72) so a retry does not send the same SMS twice.
  • Rate limiting to respect provider limits and avoid spamming users.
  • Provider fallback: if one SMS provider is down, switch to a backup.

Deep Dive: At-Least-Once + Idempotency (the delivery dilemma)

The core reliability tension: you must not lose a notification, but you also must not send the same OTP five times. Queues give at-least-once delivery (Topic 38) — safer than at-most-once, but it means duplicates can happen (a worker crashes after sending but before acking, so the message is redelivered). The fix is idempotency (Topic 72): each notification carries a unique ID, and the worker records "sent ID X" so a redelivery is detected and skipped. So the honest design is "at-least-once delivery made safe by idempotent workers" — stating this trade-off explicitly is exactly what's expected.

Deep Dive: Why Separate Queues = Bulkheads

A subtle but important choice: one shared queue is dangerous. If a marketing blast of 10 million emails is queued, an urgent OTP sent right after waits behind all 10 million — useless. Splitting into priority/channel queues (transactional vs bulk, and per channel) is the bulkhead pattern (Topic 58): a flood in one lane can't starve another. You give the transactional queue more workers and strict latency targets, while bulk drains slowly in the background. This is graceful prioritization, not just tidiness.

Interview tip — what they probe

They'll ask: "How do you avoid sending duplicate notifications?" (idempotency keys + dedup store), "How do you stop bulk emails delaying OTPs?" (priority queues / bulkheads), "What if a provider (Twilio/FCM) is down?" (retry with backoff, DLQ, fallback provider), and "How do you respect opt-outs/quiet hours?" (preference service before sending). Frame it as decoupled queue + workers with idempotent, prioritized delivery.

Key Takeaways

  • Core design: queue + channel workers to decouple and scale sending.
  • Delivery is at-least-once made safe by idempotent workers (dedup by notification ID).
  • Use separate priority queues (bulkheads) so urgent OTPs aren't blocked by bulk marketing.
  • Check user preferences; ensure reliability with retries, DLQ, rate limits, and provider fallback.
Previous← Design Google Drive / Dropbox NextDesign a Rate Limiter →

#99Design a Rate Limiter

The goal

Build a system that limits how many requests a client can make in a time window (e.g. 100 requests/minute), works across many servers, and is fast enough to run on every request.

We covered the concept in Topic 49. Now we design it as a system — especially the tricky part: making it work consistently across a fleet of servers.

Step 1: Requirements

  • Functional: allow up to N requests per window per user/IP/API key; return 429 when exceeded; configurable limits.
  • Non-functional: very low latency (it runs on every request), accurate, and consistent across distributed servers.

Step 2: Pick an Algorithm

From Topic 49: Token Bucket is the popular choice — it allows short bursts and is simple to implement. (Sliding window log/counter gives smoother accuracy if needed.)

Step 3: The Core Challenge — Distributed Counting

Why local counters fail

If each server keeps its own count in memory, a user hitting 5 servers gets 5× their real limit. We need a shared, central counter that all servers read and update.

Use Redis

Store counters in a fast, central store — Redis. It is in-memory (fast), supports atomic increment operations (so concurrent updates are correct), and supports TTL/expiry (perfect for time windows). Each request atomically increments the user's counter in Redis and checks it against the limit.

Any server
→
Redis counteratomic incr + TTL
over limit?
Allow or 429
A shared Redis counter gives a consistent limit across all servers.

Step 4: Where to Run It & Extras

  • Run the rate limiter at the API Gateway (Topic 50) so it protects all services in one place.
  • Return helpful headers (remaining quota, retry-after) with the 429.
  • For extreme performance, a small local cache + periodic sync can reduce Redis calls (trading a little accuracy).

Deep Dive: The Race Condition (why "atomic" matters)

The classic bug: a naive limiter does count = GET key; if count < limit: SET key, count+1. Under concurrency, two requests both read count=99, both think they're under the 100 limit, and both proceed — you've allowed 101. This read-modify-write race is why you must use an atomic operation: Redis INCR (which increments and returns in one step) or a small Lua script that does the check-and-increment atomically on the server. Naming this race and fixing it with atomic ops is precisely the depth interviewers probe.

Deep Dive: Window Algorithms and Their Trade-offs

The algorithm choice has real consequences: Fixed window (count per calendar minute) is simplest but has a boundary burst bug — a user can send 100 requests at 0:59 and 100 more at 1:00, i.e. 200 in two seconds. Sliding window log (store every request timestamp) is perfectly accurate but memory-heavy. Sliding window counter (weighted blend of current + previous window) is the popular middle ground — smooth and cheap. Token bucket allows controlled bursts (good for APIs). Mentioning the fixed-window boundary problem shows you understand why the fancier algorithms exist.

Interview tip — what they probe

Expect: "What if two requests hit at once?" (atomic INCR / Lua — the race), "Fixed vs sliding window?" (boundary burst trade-off), "Where do you enforce it?" (API gateway, central Redis), and "What if Redis is a bottleneck/down?" (local token cache + periodic sync; fail-open vs fail-closed decision). Lead with the distributed-counting problem and the atomicity fix.

Key Takeaways

  • Pick an algorithm: Token Bucket (bursts) or sliding window counter (smooth) are great defaults — fixed window has a boundary-burst bug.
  • The hard part is distributed counting — a central Redis counter with atomic INCR/Lua (avoids the read-modify-write race) + TTL.
  • Run it at the API Gateway to protect everything; return 429 with helpful headers.
  • It must be fast; under extreme load use a local cache + sync, and decide fail-open vs fail-closed if Redis is down.
Previous← Design Notification System NextDesign Search Autocomplete →

#100Design Search Autocomplete (Typeahead)

The goal

Build the feature that suggests completions as a user types in a search box (like Google's suggestions) — instantly, and ranked by popularity.

Step 1: Requirements

  • Functional: as the user types a prefix, return the top few most-relevant suggestions; suggestions ranked by popularity/frequency.
  • Non-functional: extremely low latency (must feel instant, < ~100ms), handle huge query volumes, scale to billions of phrases.

Step 2: The Core Data Structure — The Trie

A tree of prefixes

A Trie (prefix tree) stores words by their letters: each path from the root spells a prefix. To autocomplete "ca", you walk to the "ca" node and all words below it are candidates. This makes prefix lookups extremely fast. To rank, store the top-k most popular completions at each node (precomputed), so you return them instantly without searching the whole subtree.

Type "ca"
→
Trie node "ca"precomputed top-k
→
cat, car, cake…
Walk to the prefix node and return its precomputed top suggestions instantly.

Step 3: Serving Fast

  • Keep the Trie in memory (it must be instant) and replicate it across servers.
  • Cache popular prefixes; the most common prefixes serve from cache.
  • Suggestions can also be served from the edge/CDN for popular queries.

Step 4: Updating Popularity (the write path)

  • Real search queries are logged and aggregated (via streams/Kafka, Topic 41) to count how often each phrase is searched.
  • Offline/async jobs periodically rebuild the Trie with updated top-k rankings — you do not update it on every keystroke (that would be too slow).
  • So reads are instant (in-memory Trie), and popularity updates happen in the background.

Deep Dive: The Read/Write Split Is the Whole Trick

The defining insight is separating the blazing-fast read path from the heavy write path. Reads must be <100ms, so they hit an in-memory Trie with precomputed top-k at each node — no computation, just walk to the prefix node and return its stored list. But you can not update popularity counts on every keystroke (billions of events would crush it and rebuilding rankings live is too slow). So the write path is fully offline/async: real searches are logged, streamed to Kafka, aggregated into frequency counts, and a background job periodically rebuilds the Trie (or just the top-k lists). The new Trie is then swapped in. This "precompute offline, serve from memory" pattern is the same idea as the precomputed feed (Topic 25) — recognizing the read/write asymmetry is what they're testing.

Deep Dive: Scaling and Sampling

A full Trie of all queries is huge, so you shard it (e.g. by first letters, so prefixes "a..m" on one set of nodes, "n..z" on another) and replicate each shard for read throughput. You don't need every search counted exactly — sampling a fraction of query logs gives accurate-enough popularity at a fraction of the cost. You also handle freshness (trending terms) by weighting recent queries more, and personalization/typo-tolerance as add-ons. The honest framing: approximate, eventually-updated rankings are totally fine here — nobody needs the suggestion order to be exact.

Interview tip — what they probe

They'll ask: "How do you keep it under 100ms?" (in-memory Trie + precomputed top-k + cache/CDN), "How do you update popularity without slowing reads?" (offline log aggregation, periodic rebuild + swap), "How do you scale the Trie?" (shard by prefix, replicate), and "How fresh must rankings be?" (eventually — sample logs, weight recent). Emphasize the read/write split.

Key Takeaways

  • The core structure is a Trie (prefix tree) with precomputed top-k suggestions at each node.
  • The trick is the read/write split: in-memory Trie for instant reads; popularity updated offline from query logs.
  • Shard the Trie by prefix and replicate for scale; sample logs since rankings can be approximate/eventual.
  • Reads are blazing fast; the heavy lifting (aggregate, rebuild, swap) happens in the background.
Previous← Design a Rate Limiter NextDesign a Distributed Cache →

#101Design a Distributed Cache

The goal

Build a caching system (like a Redis cluster) that spreads cached data across many machines, so it can hold more than one machine's memory and stay available if a node fails.

One cache server has limited memory and is a single point of failure. A distributed cache spreads data across many nodes — combining several earlier concepts.

Step 1: Requirements

  • Functional: get/set key-value pairs; support eviction (TTL/LRU); scale by adding nodes.
  • Non-functional: very low latency, high availability, scalable, handle node failures gracefully.

Step 2: Spreading Data — Consistent Hashing

The key technique

Use consistent hashing (Topic 66) to decide which node stores each key. Why? Because when you add or remove a cache node, only a small fraction of keys move — instead of remapping everything and wiping the whole cache. Virtual nodes keep the load balanced.

Client
hash(key) →
Hash ring picks node
→
Cache node N
Consistent hashing maps each key to a node with minimal reshuffling on changes.

Step 3: Availability — Replication

  • Replicate each key to one or more other nodes (Topic 26), so if a node dies, a replica still serves the data.
  • A node failure then only causes a brief blip, not data loss across the board.

Step 4: Eviction & Consistency

  • Each node uses an eviction policy like LRU (Topic 33) and TTL to manage its limited memory.
  • Choose a cache strategy (cache-aside is common, Topic 32) for how it syncs with the database.
  • Caches usually favour availability and speed over strict consistency (stale entries expire via TTL).

Deep Dive: Client-Side vs Proxy Routing

A key design question: who knows the hash ring and routes the request? Two models. (1) Smart client: the client library holds the ring and connects directly to the right node — lowest latency (no extra hop), but every client must learn ring changes (this is roughly how Redis Cluster works, with clients following redirects). (2) Proxy layer: clients hit a proxy that knows the ring and forwards (e.g. Twemproxy) — simpler clients, but an extra hop and another thing to scale. Naming this routing trade-off is a sign you've thought beyond "just use consistent hashing."

Deep Dive: The Hard Part Is Consistency, Not Storage

Storing key-values is easy; the genuinely hard problems are the failure and consistency edges. Replication lag: if you replicate for availability, a read from a replica may be slightly stale — usually fine for a cache, but worth stating. Hot keys: one wildly popular key (a celebrity, a viral item) overloads its single node regardless of hashing — you mitigate by replicating that hot key to multiple nodes or adding a small local cache in front. Thundering herd on a cache miss: when a hot key expires, thousands of requests stampede the DB at once — fix with a lock/"single-flight" so only one request recomputes while others wait (ties back to cache stampede, Topic 32). These edge cases are where interviews go deep.

Interview tip — what they probe

Expect: "How do keys map to nodes, and what happens when you add one?" (consistent hashing + virtual nodes, ~1/N moves), "Who routes — client or proxy?" (the trade-off above), "What about a hot key?" (replicate it / local cache), and "Cache miss stampede?" (single-flight lock). It's a great capstone — it stitches together hashing, replication, eviction, and cache strategies.

Key Takeaways

  • A distributed cache spreads data across nodes using consistent hashing + virtual nodes (minimal reshuffle on scaling).
  • Routing is either smart-client (no extra hop) or proxy-based (simpler clients) — a latency vs simplicity trade-off.
  • Replication gives availability (with mild staleness); watch for hot keys and cache-miss stampedes (single-flight lock).
  • Each node uses LRU + TTL eviction; this problem reviews hashing + replication + eviction + cache strategies.
Previous← Design Search Autocomplete NextDesign a Web Crawler →

#102Design a Web Crawler

The goal

Build a system that automatically browses the web — starting from some URLs, downloading pages, extracting links, and following them — to collect data (e.g. for a search engine index).

Step 1: Requirements

  • Functional: start from seed URLs; download pages; extract and follow links; store page content.
  • Non-functional: massive scale (billions of pages), be polite (do not overload sites), avoid loops/duplicates, and be fault tolerant.

Step 2: The Core Loop

URL Frontier (queue)
→
Fetch page
→
Parse + extract links
add new URLs →
back to Frontier
A queue of URLs feeds fetchers; new links found are added back to the queue.
  • URL Frontier: a giant queue of URLs to visit (with priorities).
  • Fetchers (workers): download pages in parallel (massively distributed).
  • Parser: extracts content and links from each page.
  • Storage: save page content (object storage) and metadata.

Step 3: The Tricky Parts

  • Avoid duplicates / loops: keep a huge set of "already seen" URLs (often a Bloom filter — a compact structure that answers "have I seen this?" using little memory). Without this, crawlers loop forever.
  • Politeness: respect each site's robots.txt and rate-limit per domain so you do not hammer one server (a per-domain rate limiter, Topic 49).
  • Prioritization: crawl important/fresh pages more often.
  • Distributed & fault tolerant: thousands of workers; if one dies, its URLs are re-queued.
Why a Bloom filter?

Storing billions of seen URLs in full would need enormous memory. A Bloom filter tells you "definitely not seen" or "probably seen" using a tiny fraction of the space — perfect for deduplication at web scale (a small false-positive rate is acceptable).

Deep Dive: The Bloom Filter Trade-off

Why not a normal hash set of seen URLs? At billions of URLs, even storing 64-byte URLs would need terabytes of RAM — infeasible. A Bloom filter answers "have I seen this URL?" using a tiny bit-array and a few hash functions, at maybe ~10 bits per URL. The catch (and the thing to state): it has false positives but never false negatives — it may occasionally say "seen" for a URL you haven't crawled (so you skip a page you shouldn't), but it will never wrongly say "not seen." For a crawler, missing a tiny fraction of pages is an acceptable price for fitting dedup in memory. Knowing this asymmetry (and that it's a probabilistic, space-vs-accuracy trade) is the depth interviewers look for.

Deep Dive: Politeness via the URL Frontier

"Politeness" isn't a footnote — it shapes the architecture. If thousands of workers all hammer one popular domain in parallel, you'll effectively DDoS it and get banned. So the URL Frontier isn't a single queue; it's cleverly structured to enforce per-domain rate limits — e.g. a set of per-domain queues feeding workers such that any one domain is only hit every few seconds, while overall throughput across many domains stays massive. It also handles prioritization (crawl important/fresh pages more often) and respects robots.txt. This makes the frontier the brain of the crawler, not just a to-do list.

Interview tip — what they probe

They'll ask: "How do you dedup billions of URLs cheaply?" (Bloom filter + its false-positive trade-off), "How do you avoid overloading a site?" (per-domain rate limiting in the frontier, robots.txt), "How do you avoid loops?" (seen-set), and "How does it stay fault tolerant at huge scale?" (distributed workers, re-queue on failure). The frontier and the Bloom filter are the two stars.

Key Takeaways

  • Core loop: URL frontier → fetch → parse → add new links, repeated by many distributed workers.
  • Use a Bloom filter to dedup seen URLs cheaply — it has false positives but never false negatives (acceptable trade).
  • The URL frontier enforces per-domain politeness (rate limits, robots.txt) and prioritization — it's the brain.
  • Store pages in object storage; make it distributed and fault tolerant (re-queue a dead worker's URLs).
Previous← Design a Distributed Cache NextDesign a Stock Exchange →

#103Design a Stock Exchange

The goal

Build a trading system that matches buy and sell orders for stocks in real time — with extremely low latency, perfect correctness, and fairness.

This is one of the most demanding designs because the requirements are unusually strict: microsecond latency and zero tolerance for errors (it is money).

Step 1: Requirements

  • Functional: place/cancel orders (buy/sell at a price); match compatible orders; broadcast price updates; maintain the order book.
  • Non-functional: ultra-low latency (microseconds), strong consistency (no wrong trades), fairness (order priority), high throughput, full audit trail.

Step 2: The Core — The Matching Engine & Order Book

The order book

The heart is the order book: a sorted list of buy orders (bids) and sell orders (asks) for each stock. The matching engine continuously matches the highest bid with the lowest ask when their prices cross, following price-time priority (best price first; ties broken by who arrived first — fairness).

Step 3: Why It Is Different from Other Systems

  • In-memory, single-threaded matching: for determinism and speed, the matching engine often runs the order book in memory on a single thread per stock — no distributed locking, perfectly ordered. Speed beats horizontal sharding of a single book.
  • Strong consistency, not eventual: unlike social feeds, you can never show a wrong or out-of-order trade.
  • Low-level optimization: servers are co-located, networking is tuned to the microsecond. This is the rare case where you optimize hardware and avoid extra hops.

Step 4: Around the Engine

  • Gateway: validates and authenticates incoming orders, then forwards to the engine.
  • Sequencer / event log: every order is written to a durable, ordered log first (for replay and audit) — like Kafka, but ultra-fast.
  • Market data feed: broadcasts price/trade updates to all participants (a pub/sub fan-out, Topic 39).
  • Persistence & reconciliation: async writes of trades to databases for records (the in-memory book stays the fast path).

Deep Dive: Why Single-Threaded Beats Distributed Here

This design inverts the usual instinct ("distribute everything"), which is exactly why it's a great interview topic. For a single stock's order book, distributing it across machines would require coordination/locking on every order to preserve exact ordering and fairness — and that coordination latency would dwarf any parallelism gain. So the matching engine runs the book in memory, on a single thread per stock: deterministic, perfectly ordered, no locks, and brutally fast (microseconds). You still get scale by partitioning across stocks (AAPL on one engine, TSLA on another) — horizontal scaling at the symbol level, sequential processing within a symbol. The principle: when ordering and latency are paramount and a single book fits in memory, sequential in-memory processing wins.

Deep Dive: Durability Without Slowing the Hot Path

How do you keep an in-memory engine both fast and crash-safe? The same way databases do (Topic 21): an append-only event log (sequencer). Every incoming order is written to a durable, ordered log before matching. The in-memory book is just a fast projection of that log — if an engine crashes, you replay the log to rebuild the exact state. This is event sourcing (Topic 40) and gives you durability, a perfect audit trail, and deterministic recovery, all without putting a slow database in the matching hot path. Trades persist to databases asynchronously, off the critical path.

Interview tip — what they probe

Expect: "Why single-threaded / in-memory instead of distributed?" (ordering + latency; partition by symbol), "How is it crash-safe if it's in memory?" (write-ahead event log + replay), "How do you guarantee fairness?" (price-time priority, deterministic sequencing), and "How do prices reach everyone?" (pub/sub fan-out). This is the one design where you argue against distributing the core — make that case clearly.

Key Takeaways

  • The heart is the matching engine on an order book, matching by price-time priority (fairness).
  • It favours strong consistency + ultra-low latency via a single-threaded, in-memory engine per stock; scale by partitioning across symbols.
  • An append-only event log (sequencer) gives durability, deterministic replay recovery, and audit — without a DB in the hot path.
  • Price updates fan out via pub/sub; trades persist asynchronously. (The rare case where you argue against distributing the core.)
Previous← Design a Web Crawler NextDesign a Payment System →

#104Design a Payment System

The goal

Build a system that processes payments reliably — charging customers, paying merchants, and keeping every rupee accounted for, with zero tolerance for errors.

Payments are where correctness matters most. The themes here are consistency, idempotency, and auditability — not raw speed.

Step 1: Requirements

  • Functional: accept a payment; integrate with payment providers/banks; track status; handle refunds; record everything.
  • Non-functional: strong consistency (no double charges, no lost money), highly reliable, secure, fully auditable.

Step 2: The Most Important Idea — Idempotency

Never charge twice

Networks fail and clients retry (Topic 57). If a "charge ₹500" request is sent twice, the customer must not be charged twice. The fix: every payment request carries a unique idempotency key. The server records it; if it sees the same key again, it returns the original result instead of charging again (Topic 72). This is non-negotiable in payments.

Step 3: Tracking Money with a Ledger

  • Use a double-entry ledger: every transaction records where money came from and went to. Debits and credits must always balance — this makes errors detectable and gives a full audit trail.
  • Payment status moves through clear states: pending → authorized → captured → settled (or failed/refunded). Each transition is recorded.
  • Use ACID transactions (Topic 21) / strong consistency for balance changes — never eventual consistency for money.
Pay request+ idempotency key
→
Payment service
→
Provider / bank
→
Ledger (double-entry)
Idempotent requests flow to a provider; every movement is recorded in a balanced ledger.

Step 4: Reliability Across Services

  • A payment touches multiple services/banks — coordinate with the Saga pattern (Topic 63) and compensating actions (e.g. auto-refund if a later step fails), since 2PC across external banks is impractical.
  • Reconciliation jobs compare your records with the provider's daily to catch any mismatch.
  • Security: encrypt everything, never store raw card numbers (use tokenization / PCI-compliant providers).
  • Async notifications/webhooks handle provider callbacks about final status.

Deep Dive: The Lost-Response Problem (why idempotency is non-negotiable)

Picture the exact failure: the client sends "charge ₹500", the bank charges successfully, but the success response is lost on the network. The client times out, assumes failure, and retries. Without protection, the customer is charged twice. The fix: the client generates an idempotency key once and reuses it on every retry. The payment service stores "key X → result"; on seeing key X again, it returns the original result instead of charging again. Crucially, the key must be recorded atomically with the charge (same transaction), or there's a window where you charge but haven't yet saved the key. This is the single most important idea in the whole design — state it first and precisely.

Deep Dive: Why a Double-Entry Ledger, Not Just a Balance Column

It's tempting to store each account's balance as a single number you add/subtract. That's fragile: a bug or partial failure can silently make money appear or vanish with no trace. A double-entry ledger records every movement as paired entries — a debit from one account and an equal credit to another — so the books must always balance (sum of all entries = 0). This makes errors detectable (any imbalance is a red flag), gives an immutable audit trail (you append entries, never edit), and lets you reconstruct any balance by summing its entries. Combined with reconciliation jobs (compare your ledger against the bank's statement daily), it's how real money systems stay provably correct. Mentioning immutability + balancing + reconciliation is exactly the maturity they want.

Interview tip — what they probe

Expect: "What happens if a charge request is retried?" (idempotency key, recorded atomically), "How do you keep money provably correct?" (immutable double-entry ledger + reconciliation), "How do you coordinate across bank/wallet/merchant when 2PC isn't possible?" (saga + compensating refunds, Topic 63), and "How do you handle card data?" (never store it — tokenize via a PCI-compliant provider). Lead with idempotency, then the ledger.

Key Takeaways

  • Payments prioritize correctness: strong consistency, never speed over accuracy.
  • Idempotency keys (recorded atomically with the charge) prevent double charges on retries — the single most important rule.
  • Use an immutable double-entry ledger (must balance) + clear status states + reconciliation for provable auditability.
  • Coordinate multi-step flows with sagas (compensating refunds); never store raw card data — tokenize.
Previous← Design a Stock Exchange NextHow to Approach a SD Interview →

#105How to Approach a System Design Interview

One-line definition

A system design interview is open-ended on purpose. Success is not about knowing one "right answer" — it is about following a clear, structured approach and explaining your trade-offs out loud. This topic gives you that step-by-step framework.

Most candidates fail not from lack of knowledge, but from jumping in randomly. A calm, structured method instantly makes you look senior. Here is a reliable framework you can use for any design question.

The Step-by-Step Framework

  • 1. Clarify requirements (do not skip!): ask questions to define scope. What features matter? Who are the users? What scale? Separate functional (what it does) from non-functional (how well — latency, availability, consistency). Confirm before designing.
  • 2. Estimate scale (back-of-envelope): roughly compute users, requests/sec, storage, bandwidth (Topic 106). This drives your design decisions.
  • 3. Define the API / data model: sketch the main endpoints and core entities. This grounds the discussion.
  • 4. High-level design: draw the big boxes — clients, load balancer, services, databases, cache, queues. Show the request flow. Start simple.
  • 5. Deep dive: the interviewer will pick a component to explore. Go deeper on the interesting part (e.g. the timeline, the matching engine, the sharding scheme).
  • 6. Scale & address bottlenecks: identify the weak points and apply the tools you learned — caching, replication, sharding, CDNs, async processing.
  • 7. Discuss trade-offs & wrap up: summarize choices, mention alternatives, and acknowledge limitations.
Clarify
→
Estimate
→
API + HLD
→
Deep dive + scale
A predictable flow keeps you calm and shows structured thinking.

How to Behave

  • Think out loud — the interviewer is grading your reasoning, not just the final diagram.
  • Drive the conversation, but stay collaborative — check in and adapt to their hints.
  • Justify every choice with a trade-off ("I'd use NoSQL here because we need scale over complex joins").
  • Start simple, then scale — do not over-engineer from minute one.

What's Really Being Tested (it's not the "right answer")

Internalise this and your nerves drop: there is no single correct design. The interviewer is a senior engineer imagining "would I want to build systems with this person?" They're evaluating four things: (1) Structured thinking — do you have a method or do you flail? (2) Communication — can you explain clearly and bring them along? (3) Trade-off reasoning — do you understand why, not just what? (4) Technical depth — can you go deep when pushed? Notice that depth is only one of four. A candidate with average knowledge but great structure and trade-offs often beats a walking encyclopedia who rambles. Every behaviour below maps to one of these four.

A Sample Time Budget (45 minutes)

Time management is itself a graded skill. A rough split: ~5 min clarify requirements + scope, ~5 min estimate scale and define the API/data model, ~10 min high-level design (the big boxes and request flow), ~15–20 min deep dive on the component the interviewer cares about, and ~5 min wrap-up (bottlenecks, trade-offs, what you'd improve). The classic failure is spending 25 minutes perfecting the HLD and never reaching a deep dive — cover breadth first, then go deep. Watch the clock and narrate it ("I'll lock the high-level design and dive into the timeline, since that's the crux").

The mindset shift that wins

Treat the interviewer's hints as gifts, not attacks. If they ask "what happens when this database gets too big?", they're steering you toward the topic they want to explore (sharding) — lean in, don't get defensive. The best sessions feel like two engineers whiteboarding together, not an interrogation.

Key Takeaways

  • Follow a structure: clarify → estimate → API/HLD → deep dive → scale → trade-offs.
  • You're judged on structure, communication, trade-offs, and depth — not on one "right answer".
  • Manage time: breadth first (HLD), then depth; leave room for the deep dive and wrap-up.
  • Always clarify first, think out loud, justify with trade-offs, and treat hints as guidance.
Previous← Design a Payment System NextBack-of-the-Envelope Calculations →

#106Back-of-the-Envelope Calculations

One-line definition

Back-of-the-envelope calculations are quick, rough estimates of scale — requests per second, storage needed, bandwidth — done in your head or on paper. They guide your design decisions and show you think quantitatively.

You do not need exact numbers; you need the right order of magnitude. Is it 100 requests/sec or 1 million? That changes everything. These estimates tell you whether you need caching, sharding, or a CDN.

Numbers Worth Memorizing

  • Time: 1 day ≈ 86,400 seconds (~100,000 for easy math).
  • Powers: 1 thousand = 10³ (KB), 1 million = 10⁶ (MB), 1 billion = 10⁹ (GB), 1 trillion = 10¹² (TB).
  • Storage sizes: a char ≈ 1 byte; a typical tweet ≈ 300 bytes; a photo ≈ a few hundred KB–few MB; a long video ≈ GBs.
  • Latency intuition: memory access is nanoseconds; SSD is microseconds; a network round trip across regions is tens to hundreds of milliseconds.

The Standard Recipe

  • QPS (queries/sec): daily requests ÷ 86,400. Then estimate peak QPS as ~2–3× average.
  • Storage: (new items per day) × (size per item) × (retention in days).
  • Bandwidth: QPS × size per response.
  • Memory for cache: apply the "80/20 rule" — cache the ~20% of data that serves ~80% of requests.
Worked example: a Twitter-like service

Say 200M daily active users, each posting ~2 tweets/day = 400M tweets/day. Writes/sec = 400M ÷ 86,400 ≈ ~4,600 writes/sec (peak ~10K). Storage: 400M × 300 bytes ≈ 120 GB/day → ~44 TB/year. Reads are ~100× writes → ~500K reads/sec. Conclusion: hugely read-heavy → we definitely need heavy caching and sharding. See how the numbers directly justify the design?

Tips

  • Round aggressively — use 100,000 sec/day, powers of 10. Precision is not the point.
  • State your assumptions out loud ("assuming 1M daily users…").
  • Connect numbers to decisions — "500K reads/sec means a single DB can't handle it, so we cache and shard."

The Latency Numbers Every Engineer Should Know

Beyond throughput math, the famous "latency numbers" build the intuition for why we cache, batch, and use CDNs. Rough orders of magnitude: reading from memory ≈ 100 nanoseconds; a fast SSD read ≈ 100 microseconds (~1,000× slower than memory); a network round-trip within a data center ≈ 0.5 milliseconds; and a round-trip across continents ≈ 100+ milliseconds. The punchlines that justify whole architectures: memory is ~1,000× faster than disk (so cache in RAM), and a cross-region hop is ~100ms (so put a CDN near users and avoid chatty cross-region calls). When you say "I'll cache this", you can back it with "because RAM is three orders of magnitude faster than disk."

Why These Estimates Drive the Whole Design

The point of the math isn't the number — it's the decision the number forces. "~40 writes/sec" says a single modest database is fine; "~500K reads/sec" screams caching + read replicas + sharding because no single node serves that. "120 GB/day → 44 TB/year" says you'll outgrow one machine → plan to shard. "Peak is 3× average" sizes your autoscaling headroom. Always close the loop out loud: number → implication → design choice. That linkage is what makes estimation impressive rather than busywork.

Common pitfalls

Forgetting peak vs average (designing for the average then falling over at the dinner rush), ignoring the read:write ratio (it usually dictates caching/replication), and getting lost in precision (arguing 86,400 vs 100,000 wastes time — round and move on). Estimate to inform the design, not to be exact.

Key Takeaways

  • Estimate QPS, storage, and bandwidth to size your system — order of magnitude is enough.
  • Memorize basics: ~86,400 sec/day, powers of ten, rough item sizes, and the latency ladder (RAM ≪ SSD ≪ network ≪ cross-region).
  • Compute peak (~2–3× average) and apply the 80/20 rule for caching.
  • Always close the loop: number → implication → design choice (caching, sharding, CDN).
Previous← How to Approach a SD Interview NextCommon Interview Mistakes →

#107Common Interview Mistakes (and How to Avoid Them)

One-line definition

This final topic lists the most common mistakes that sink system design interviews — so you can consciously avoid every one of them. Knowing the traps is half the battle.

You now have the knowledge and the framework. The last step is to avoid the classic blunders. Most rejections come from these, not from missing some obscure fact.

The Top Mistakes

  • 1. Jumping into the solution without clarifying. Designing before understanding the requirements is the #1 mistake. Fix: always clarify scope and requirements first (Topic 105).
  • 2. Not asking about scale. A design for 1,000 users is totally different from one for 1 billion. Fix: estimate scale early (Topic 106).
  • 3. Over-engineering from the start. Proposing microservices, Kafka, and sharding for a tiny app shows poor judgement. Fix: start simple, scale only when needed.
  • 4. Staying silent. If you think without speaking, the interviewer cannot follow your reasoning. Fix: think out loud constantly.
  • 5. Not justifying decisions / ignoring trade-offs. Saying "I'll use NoSQL" without why is weak. Fix: explain the trade-off behind every choice.
  • 6. Ignoring non-functional requirements. Forgetting availability, latency, consistency. Fix: explicitly address them.
  • 7. No bottleneck analysis. Drawing boxes but never asking "what breaks first under load?". Fix: identify and address bottlenecks.
  • 8. Being rigid / defensive. Dismissing the interviewer's hints. Fix: treat hints as gifts and adapt collaboratively.
  • 9. Poor time management. Spending 20 minutes on one tiny detail. Fix: keep moving; cover breadth before depth.
  • 10. Saying "it depends" and stopping. Fix: say "it depends" and then explain on what, and make a reasoned choice.
The mindset that wins

There is no single perfect answer. Interviewers want to see structured thinking, clear communication, and sound trade-offs. Be the calm engineer who clarifies, reasons aloud, starts simple, scales deliberately, and explains every "why". Do that, and you will stand out.

The Two Mistakes That Sink the Most Candidates

If you fix only two things, fix these. First, jumping straight into solutioning. The moment you hear "design Twitter", the urge is to draw boxes — resist it. Spend the first few minutes clarifying scope and scale. Designing the wrong thing brilliantly still fails. Second, over-engineering. Reaching for Kafka, microservices, and global sharding for a system with 10,000 users signals poor judgment, not seniority. Seniority is shown by starting with the simplest thing that works and adding complexity only when a concrete bottleneck demands it. "I'd start with a single database and a cache; we'd shard only once we cross ~X writes/sec" is a far stronger statement than immediately proposing a distributed everything.

Turn Each Mistake into a Habit

The fix for all ten is to make the good behaviours automatic: open with clarifying questions (kills #1, #2, #6), narrate constantly (kills #4, #5), do quick estimation (kills #2, #7), and always pair a choice with its trade-off and alternative (kills #5, #10). Practise the framework on 3–4 designs until the flow — clarify, estimate, API, HLD, deep dive, scale, trade-offs — runs on autopilot. When the structure is muscle memory, your whole brain is free to reason about the actual problem, and you'll naturally avoid the traps under pressure.

Final interview tip

End strong: in the last few minutes, proactively summarize your design, name its bottlenecks, and say what you'd improve with more time ("with more time I'd add a read-replica strategy and discuss the cache-invalidation edge cases"). Self-critique demonstrates senior judgment and leaves the interviewer with the impression of a thoughtful, honest engineer — exactly who they want to hire.

Key Takeaways

  • Most failures come from process mistakes, not missing facts.
  • The two biggest: jumping in without clarifying and over-engineering — start simple, scale deliberately.
  • Always: clarify first, estimate scale, think out loud, justify with trade-offs; make the framework muscle memory.
  • End by summarizing, naming bottlenecks, and self-critiquing — it signals senior judgment.
